Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, why did God leave the dualists to themselves?
A) Because they were intelligent
B) Because they did not supplicate to Him
C) Because they followed Mani
D) Because they were ignorant

Key Takeaway
The dualists' error stems from a spiritual failure to seek God's guidance, not from mere intellectual confusion.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In what areas of your life have you been relying on your own reasoning without humbly seeking God's guidance? What did that lead to, and what will you do differently starting today?
?
When you read 'God left him to himself,' does that frighten you? Are there places in your heart where you lean toward worldly desires, assuming you can handle it on your own? What is one specific attachment you will surrender to God today through supplication?
?
Imagine standing before God and hearing that He did not protect you from your enemy because you did not turn to Him. Is that scenario real for you right now? What step will you take within the next hour to ensure you are not left to your own devices?

The Self-Contradiction of Dualism
Maturidi analyzes the logical self-destruction of dualism.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the dualists' attempt to separate good and evil led them to reverse the roles, making light the source of evil and darkness the source of good.

Core Idea
Dualism ends up destroying itself by attributing evil to light and good to darkness.

In the second part of the passage, Maturidi reveals the logical incoherence of the dualist position. Starting from the principle that God destroys a person by what they cling to for safety, he shows how the dualists ended up contradicting their own assumptions: they began by making light the source of good and darkness the source of evil, but then they made light ignorant and powerless (thus evil), and darkness powerful and knowing (thus good). This lesson dissects that reversal.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation carefully.
2
Understand the principle
Note the opening statement about God's destruction.
3
Trace the dualist argument
Identify how they started and where they ended.
4
Observe the reversal
See how light and darkness swap roles.
5
Conclude the lesson
Reflect on the self-destructive nature of their reasoning.

Commentary
This is a reflective passage where the author (the faqih) comments on the spiritual and intellectual error of the dualists. Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Dualism consistently separates good and evil into two independent principles.
Correction: Maturidi demonstrates that the dualists' own reasoning forces light (good) to be evil (ignorant, powerless) and darkness (evil) to be good (powerful, knowing), thus contradicting their own starting point.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, what did the dualists make the origin of good (light) into?
A) The ultimate good
B) The ultimate evil
C) The source of knowledge
D) The same as darkness

Key Takeaway
The dualists' system self-destructs because it strips light of power and knowledge, making it evil, and gives darkness those good attributes.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you ever assume that good cannot come from the same source as evil? How might that assumption blind you to the true source of goodness in your life, leading you to trust what is actually harmful?
?
The dualists were destroyed by the very thing they thought would save them. Is there a belief or habit you rely on for protection or escape that might actually be leading you away from God? Be honest: what is it, and what would it cost you to abandon it?
?
Maturidi says true good requires power and knowledge. Think of a time you claimed something was 'good' without really understanding it or having the power to see it through. How did that turn out? How can you guard your heart against calling something good that God might not?

The Logical Collapse of Dualism
Maturidi refutes dualism by logical reduction to infinite regress.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Maturidi exposes the logical absurdities in dualist reasoning, showing that their own method leads to an infinite regress of principles and contradicts their claims about good and evil.

Core Idea
Dualism collapses under its own logic, forcing an endless multiplication of principles.

This passage presents a sharp logical refutation of dualism. Maturidi demonstrates that the dualists' own reasoning, when applied consistently, forces them to accept not just two but many principles, leading to absurdity. He then critiques their underlying assumptions about God and their inability to perceive divine wisdom.

Guided Path
1
Follow the logical chain
Read the passage and notice how Maturidi takes the dualists' method of positing two opposite principles and extends it step by step to more principles, revealing an infinite regress.
Pay attention to the numbers: two, four, six, seven, five, three.
2
Understand the infinite regress argument
Identify each step: from two principles to four (natures), then to six (directions), then to a seventh or fifth. Ask yourself why each step follows from the previous one.
The argument shows that the dualists cannot stop at two without being arbitrary.
3
Examine the third principle argument
Consider the point that if two principles existed before the world, there must be a third principle that caused their mixture. This undercuts the dualist claim of only two.
4
Reflect on the critique of dualist ignorance
Read the second half where Maturidi criticizes the dualists for anthropomorphizing God and failing to understand divine wisdom due to their own limitations.
Notice the contrast between their claim of light's purity and its actual ignorance in the mixture.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مع ما لو كان لذينك الوجهين يجب القول بالاثنين ليجب القول بالأربع نحو الطبائع، إذ هي متضادة، كل يضر الكل. ولو كان بهذا القول بالأربع ليجب القول بالست، بما لا يخلو شيء قائم عن جهات ست؛ وذلك يوجب القول بالسابع ، لما كان حامل تلك الجهات لا يوصف بجهة سابقها ؛ أو بالخمس بما كان الذي فيه اجتماع تلك الطبائع هو الخامس، [وهو ] لا يوصف بحرّ ولا بَرْد ولو كان كما تقول الثنوية ليجب القول بالثالث، لما كانا ولم يكن العالم ولا خير ولا شر. ومحال كون متبائن بنفسه ممتزجا بنفسه [وهو] لا يوجب الاجتماع والتناقض ؛ ثبت كون ذلك بغيرهما، وبه كان كل خير وشر. فيبطل قولهم من حيث راموا إثباته . ثم القول بالواحد لا يضطر صاحبه إلى القول بآخر بوجه وأصل ذلك أن هؤلاء قوم لم تبلغ عقولهم المبلغ الذي تدرك به حكمة الربوبية في الأشياء، وظنوا أن يكون الرب على صفتهم من الحاجات والشهوات واحتمال الآفات وشوائب العاهات، فقدروا فعله بالذي علموا الحكمة بأفعال أنفسهم ولو تأملوا ما هم فيه من الضرورات السواتر المانعة عن الإحاطة بالأشياء، ثم بمصالح أنفسهم التي في ذلك جل كدهم، وجهدوا لعلموا أن الجهل [۸۰] هو الذي سدّهم عن إدراك الحكمة في ذلك . وأحق الناس بهذا هم؛ إذ زعموا أن العالم إنما امتزاج النور والظلمة، فما من جزء من أجزاء النور إلا هو مشوب بجزء من أجزاء الظلمة هي الساترة ثم هي القاهرة للنور . فما من خير يرجى بدؤه منه إلا والظلمة تقهره وتستره عن التجلي لأهل المذهب. فأنى لهم والعلم والوقوف على طريق الحكمة حتى يدعون في الآخر دعوى شر؟ والعجب أن نورهم ـ مع قيامه بنفسه وصفائه عن شوائب الظلمة - لم يعلم [ما ] عليه في الامتزاج من الضنك والضيق ومن الجهل والعجز، ثم يُرجى بجزء منه عند خروجه عن
Moreover, if for those two aspects it were necessary to affirm two [principles], then it would be necessary to affirm four as with the natures, since they are opposites, each harming the whole. And if with this statement there were four, then it would be necessary to affirm six, since every subsistent thing is not devoid of six directions; and that necessitates affirming a seventh, since the bearer of those directions cannot be described by a direction preceding them; or five, since that in which these natures meet is the fifth, which is not described as hot or cold. And if it were as the dualists say, then it would be necessary to affirm a third, since the two [principles] existed while the world did not, and neither good nor evil existed. And it is impossible for something distinct by itself to be mixed by itself, not necessitating union and contradiction; it is established that this is through something other than them, and by that every good and evil came to be. Thus their statement is invalidated from the very angle they sought to prove it. Then the affirmation of the One does not compel its proponent to affirm another in any way. The origin of this is that these people's intellects did not reach the level by which the wisdom of Lordship in things is perceived, and they thought that the Lord is like themselves in needs, desires, susceptibility to afflictions and blemishes, so they estimated His action by what they knew of wisdom in their own actions. If they had pondered the necessities in which they are, veiling them, preventing comprehension of things, and then the interests of themselves in which is all their toil and effort, they would have known that ignorance is what blocked them from perceiving wisdom in that. And the people most deserving of this are they, since they claim that the world is only a mixture of light and darkness—there is no part of light except it is mixed with a part of darkness, which is the veiling one, then the overpowering one over light. So there is no good expected to begin from light except that darkness overpowers it and veils it from manifestation to the followers of this doctrine. So how can they have knowledge and stand on the path of wisdom, until they make a claim of evil in the end? And amazing is that their light—despite its self-subsistence and its purity from the impurities of darkness—did not know what it was in the mixture of constraint, narrowness, ignorance, and incapacity, then one hopes for a part of it when it emerges from...
Source pages p. 232

Commentary
This passage presents a logical refutation of dualism by showing that their own reasoning leads to an infinite regress of principles. The author argues: if we accept the dualists' method of positing two principles because of opposition, then we should also posit four principles (like the four natures), then six (directions), then seven (the bearer of directions), or five (the meeting point of natures). Similarly, if the two principles existed before the world, there must be a third that caused their mixture. Thus their position is internally contradictory. The author then affirms that the belief in one God does not compel one to accept another, and criticizes the dualists for anthropomorphizing God, attributing human limitations to Him. They failed to recognize divine wisdom because they were veiled by their own ignorance. The passage ends with a critique of the dualist concept of light: if light is pure and self-subsistent, how could it be ignorant and constrained in the mixture? Hence their doctrine is absurd.

أربع
طبائع
جهات
حكمة ربوبية
نور
ظلمة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Dualism is a coherent simple system with only two principles.
Correction: Maturidi shows that dualism's own logic forces an infinite regress of principles, making it internally inconsistent.

Checkpoint
What does the author argue would be necessary if we accept the dualists' method of positing principles based on opposition?
A) Three principles
B) Four, six, or seven principles
C) Only two principles
D) No principles at all

Key Takeaway
Maturidi demonstrates that dualism is self-refuting, as it forces an endless series of principles, revealing its inherent logical flaws.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever caught yourself thinking about God in terms of human limitations—as if He needs something, or as if He is subject to afflictions like you? What in this passage exposes that error in your own heart?
?
The dualists could not perceive divine wisdom because their own ignorance veiled them. What veils are you carrying right now—a habit, a sin, a certainty about how God 'should' act—that blocks you from seeing His wisdom in your life?
?
Maturidi says that affirming the One does not compel you to affirm another. But how often do you, in practice, live as if there is another force controlling your happiness, your provision, or your destiny? What would it take for you to truly believe that only Allah is in charge?

Maturidi's Refutation of Dualist Claims to Wisdom
Maturidi ridicules dualist claim to wisdom given their principles.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Maturidi argues that dualist epistemology makes rational discourse about wisdom impossible.

Core Idea
Dualism's own principles render its wisdom claim self-defeating.

Imam Maturidi continues his critique of dualism by exposing the absurdity of their claim to wisdom. He shows that the dualist's own assumptions about light and darkness lead to contradictions that make any meaningful dialogue about wisdom impossible. This passage uses logical analysis to demonstrate that dualism cannot coherently support a claim to knowledge.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let Maturidi's argumentative rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the tone of wonder and sarcasm at the beginning.
2
Identify the dualist contradiction
Maturidi points out that the dualists' light is supposed to be pure and wise, yet in mixture it is distressed and ignorant. Then a part of it is expected to produce wisdom it never had. Find this contradiction.
This is the core logical flaw.
3
Analyze the dialogue scenarios
Maturidi presents three possibilities for who is speaking in a discussion about wisdom. List them and see why each makes communication absurd.
The three are: both light, both darkness, one light one dark.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
Ask yourself: why does this show that the dualist cannot claim wisdom? How does it connect to the larger refutation of dualism?
The conclusion is that dualist epistemology is self-refuting.

والعجب أن نورهم - مع قيامه بنفسه وصفائه عن شوائب الظلمة - لم يعلم ما عليه في الامتزاج من الضنك والضيق ومن الجهل والعجز، ثم يُرجى بجزء منه عند خروجه عن جوهره ووقوعه في يدى عدوه أنه يطلقه على الحكمة التي لم يبلغها هو عند تمامه. وأحق من لا يدعى الحكمة ولا يناظر أهلها ولا يشرع فيها الثنوي؛ لأنه يرجع إلى جوهرين عند نفسه شر وخير، وكذا كل أحد عنده. وأما إن كان الشروع فيها بجوهر النور وكذلك من يكلمه فيها، فهما عندهم حكيمان لا يخفى عليهما شيء، لا معنى لتكليمها، وهما بأنفسهما كذلك؛ أو بجوهر الظلمة، ومحال احتمالهما الحكمة أو كان أحدهما جوهر النور والآخر هي الظلمة، لا يحتمل ذا الجهل ولا الآخر العلم، فيكون التكلم عبثًا لا معنى له. ولا قوة إلا بالله.
And the wonder is that their light - while subsisting by itself and being pure from the admixtures of darkness - did not know what it is upon in the mixture of distress and constriction, and of ignorance and incapacity. Then from a part of it when it comes out of its substance and falls into the hands of its enemy, it is hoped that it will release it upon the wisdom which it did not attain itself at its perfection. And the most deserving that wisdom is not claimed for him, nor does he debate its people, nor does he legislate in it, is the dualist; because he refers to two substances in himself, evil and good, and likewise everyone with him. But if the beginning in it is by the substance of light, and likewise the one who addresses him in it, then they are in their eyes two wise ones from whom nothing is hidden; there is no meaning to addressing them, and they are themselves like that. Or by the substance of darkness, and it is impossible for them to possess wisdom. Or if one is the substance of light and the other is darkness, [then] this one does not possess ignorance and the other does not possess knowledge, so the address becomes absurd, with no meaning. And there is no power but with Allah.
Source pages p. 232

Commentary
This passage further critiques the dualist position by highlighting its internal contradictions. Imam Maturidi expresses amazement that the dualists' light, though self-subsisting and pure, is in a state of distress and ignorance when mixed with darkness, and yet a part of it, when freed from its essence and falling into the hands of its enemy (darkness), is expected to release wisdom that it itself never attained. He then asserts that the dualist is the least entitled to claim wisdom because he posits two substances (good and evil) in himself, and so does everyone in his view. Furthermore, if one begins a discussion about wisdom with someone who is himself a substance of light, then both are considered wise and nothing is hidden, making dialogue unnecessary. If one begins with the substance of darkness, then wisdom is impossible. If one is light and the other darkness, then neither possesses the necessary attributes (ignorance and knowledge respectively), so dialogue is absurd. Thus, the dualist cannot coherently engage in rational discourse about wisdom.

Key Terms
النور
الظلمة
الحكمة
الجوهر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Dualism is a reasonable philosophical view that can coherently discuss wisdom.
Correction: Maturidi shows that dualist principles lead to contradictions that make any rational discourse about wisdom impossible, proving the view self-defeating.

Checkpoint
Why does Imam Maturidi say that dialogue with a dualist about wisdom is meaningless?
A) Because the dualist's principles make it impossible for both parties to be knowledgeable
B) Because dualists refuse to talk
C) Because wisdom is only from revelation
D) Because the light substance cannot speak

Key Takeaway
Maturidi demonstrates that the dualist's own principles make any claim to wisdom self-contradictory, rendering rational discourse about wisdom impossible.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Maturidi shows that the dualist's own principles make him incapable of coherently claiming wisdom. Is there an inconsistency in your own life—a belief you hold that contradicts how you actually live? What would it take for you to honestly examine that inconsistency today?
?
If your source of knowledge is mixed with ignorance—like the dualist's light in darkness—on what basis do you trust that your wisdom is true? Have you ever assumed a flawed foundation and still expected perfect guidance? Pause and name one area where you might be doing that right now.
?
The dualist's dialogue about wisdom is absurd because he cannot communicate meaningfully across substances. Think of a relationship where you hold fundamentally different 'substances' (values or beliefs) with someone. Are you truly seeking wisdom together, or is your conversation as fruitless as light talking to darkness? What step can you take today toward genuine mutual understanding?

Ja'far ibn Harb's Logical Refutation of Dualism
Maturidi narrates Ja'far ibn Harb's logical refutation of a dualist on repentance.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Ja'far ibn Harb used logical necessity to expose inconsistency in dualist metaphysics regarding repentance.

Core Idea
A single agent must be responsible for both evil and its apology.

This lesson presents a famous dialectical exchange from early kalam. Ja'far ibn Harb cleverly traps a dualist by asking about a murderer who apologizes. The dualist's own principles lead to a contradiction, forcing him to concede that both acts must come from the same source. Maturidi highlights this as an example of logical necessity in theological debate.

Guided Path
1
Read the debate narrative
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully. Notice the structure: Ja'far's question, the dualist's forced conclusion, the chief's parable, and Ja'far's response.
Pay attention to how the argument progresses step by step.
2
Identify the logical trap
Ja'far forces the dualist to admit that the apology (second act) is good. Then he argues that if the murder came from a different source (darkness), then the apology would be a lie from the light, which is evil. This creates a contradiction.
The dualist cannot consistently hold that good and evil come from separate principles.
3
Understand the parable and rebuttal
The dualist chief's parable (horse kicks owner) is meant to avoid the trap. Ja'far retorts that the owner's apology is not genuine but merely an expression of pain.
Ja'far insists that true apology must come from the same agent as the harm.
4
Reflect on the outcome
The dualist converts. Maturidi comments that this conclusion is logically necessary. Consider why the argument was compelling within the dualist's own framework.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وذكر عن جعفر بن حرب أنه سأل ثنويا عمن قتل آخر ظلما ثم اعتذر إليه وأقر بالإساءة؟ فألزمه أن [الفعل] الثاني خير، ولو كان من غيره جوهر [الفعل] الأول كان كذبًا من النور وهو شر. فكتب ذلك إلى رئيس لهم، فكتب [الرئيس مجيبا]: إن ذلك كمن تنفح دابته ويعتذر هو. فقال جعفر: إنما ذلك توجع منه، ولو اعتذر في الحقيقة كان جاهلاً، إلا أن يكون الاعتذار من تقريبه الدابة إليه. فأسلم الرجل، وحق له أن يسلم، وما ذكر ابن حرب لازم ولا قوة إلا بالله.
And it is mentioned from Ja'far ibn Harb that he asked a dualist about one who killed another unjustly then apologized to him and admitted the offence? So he forced him to conclude that the second act is good, and if from another had been the substance of the first act, it would have been a lie from the light and it is evil. So he wrote that to their chief, who wrote [the chief answering]: 'That is like one whose mount kicks him and he apologizes.' So Ja'far said: 'That is only from his pain, and if he apologized in reality he would be ignorant, unless the apology is from bringing the mount close to him.' So the man became Muslim, and it was proper for him to become Muslim. And what Ibn Harb mentioned is necessary, and there is no power except with Allah.
Source pages p. 236

Commentary
This passage is a narrative of a debate between the Mu'tazili theologian Ja'far ibn Harb and a dualist. The dualist is asked about a murderer who later apologizes and admits the offence. Ja'far forces him to conclude that the act of apology (the second act) is good, but if the substance of the first act (the murder) came from a different source (i.e., the principle of darkness rather than light), then the apology would be a lie from the light and thus evil. The dualist writes to their chief for an answer, who replies with a parable: it is like a man whose horse kicks him, and then the man apologizes. Ja'far retorts that the man's apology is merely an expression of his own pain, not a genuine apology; if it were a real apology, it would be ignorant unless the apology is from bringing the horse close (i.e., the act of apology belongs to the same agent as the kick). The dualist then converts to Islam. Maturidi comments that what Ibn Harb mentioned is logically necessary. The passage illustrates a typical kalam dialectical exchange aimed at exposing inconsistency in dualist metaphysics.

Key Terms
ثنوي (dualist)
جوهر (substance)
نور (light)
ظلم (injustice)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The dualist converted because of Ja'far's rhetorical skill or emotional persuasion.
Correction: Maturidi explicitly states that what Ibn Harb mentioned is necessary (لازم), meaning the argument was logically compelling within the dualist's own framework.

Checkpoint
What did Ja'far ibn Harb force the dualist to conclude about the act of apology?
A) That it is evil
B) That it is good
C) That it is neutral
D) That it is impossible

Key Takeaway
Ja'far ibn Harb demonstrated that repentance and evil cannot be attributed to separate principles; logical consistency demands a single source for both.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you wrong someone, do you genuinely apologize from the same 'self' that did the wrong, or do you make excuses like the dualist's parable? What would it mean for your repentance to come from the same agent as your sin?
?
The dualist chief offered a clever analogy to avoid the logical conclusion. Think of a time you used a rationalization to justify a wrong. How does this lesson expose the flaw in such reasoning? Are you willing to accept your full responsibility?
?
Ja'far's argument forced the dualist to see that both good and evil must come from a single source. How does this truth affect your understanding of God's justice and your own accountability? Will you stop compartmentalizing your actions and admit that both your good deeds and your sins come from the same created self?

Maturidi's Critique of the Mu'tazila on Evil
Maturidi critiques the Mu'tazila method of denying real evil as leading to dualism.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Maturidi argues that the Mu'tazila's denial of real evil as a metaphor leads to dualism rather than defending monotheism.

Core Idea
Denying real evil to defend God's oneness can inadvertently affirm dualism.

In debating dualists who claimed two principles (good and evil), the Mu'tazila attempted to defend divine unity by insisting that evil is not truly from Allah—it is only called evil metaphorically. Maturidi identifies a fatal flaw in this strategy: by denying the reality of evil while acknowledging that Allah creates both good and evil, they effectively concede that the creator of evil must be distinct from the creator of good. This lesson unpacks his argument.

Guided Path
1
Understand the Mu'tazila position on evil
Read the passage and note that the Mu'tazila deny the real existence of evil in Allah's creation; they say it is only called evil metaphorically.
This is their starting point for debating dualists.
2
Follow Maturidi's identification of their method
Notice that Maturidi points out their method is to remove evil from being truly evil in the debate with dualists. He then argues that this approach is flawed because it leads to a concession.
3
See the self-defeating conclusion
Maturidi shows that by negating the reality of evil while affirming Allah creates both good and evil, the Mu'tazila end up implying that the creator of evil is separate from the creator of good—exactly what the dualists believe.
Thus their defense of tawhid actually results in tathniya (dualism).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم المسألة على قول المعتزلة خطأ؛ إذ من مذهبهم أن ليس في خلق الله شر، وإنما سمي شراً بالمجاز، فإنها طريق مناظرتهم الثنوية في إزالة ما ظنوه شراً أن يكون شراً. فأما أن يسلّموا الثنوية ويلزموهم القول بالخالق الواحد من الوجه الذي يوجد [فيه] من غير الله تعالى الوجهان جميعا ويجعلوه على الصانع بعد القول بنفي ذلك، فهو محال فاسد؛ لما فيه تثبيت معرفته وتوحيده بخلق الشر والخير، ثم بنفي أحدهما في الحقيقة رجعت إلى قول الثنوية بأن الذي منه خلق الشر في الحقيقة غير الذي منه خلق الخير، فيلزمه التوحيد بالتثنية.
Then the issue according to the Mu'tazila is erroneous; for it is their doctrine that there is no evil in the creation of Allah, but it is called evil metaphorically. For that is the method of their debate with the dualists in removing what they thought of as evil from being evil. But as for that they (the Mu'tazila) should concede to the dualists and oblige them to the statement of the one Creator from the aspect that there are found, from other than Allah, both aspects (good and evil), and make them (the dualists) attribute that to the Maker after the statement of negating that – it is impossible and corrupt; because it involves establishing His knowledge and His oneness through the creation of evil and good, then by negating one of them in reality they return to the statement of the dualists that the one from whom the creation of evil comes in reality is other than the one from whom the creation of good comes, so oneness becomes necessitated by duality.
Source pages p. 236

Commentary
This passage is a critique of the Mu'tazila's approach in debating dualists. Maturidi states that the Mu'tazila hold that 'evil' does not truly exist in Allah's creation; it is only called evil metaphorically. They use this to argue against dualists who claim evil exists from a separate principle. However, Maturidi argues that this method is flawed because by denying real evil, the Mu'tazila end up implicitly conceding the dualist position. If they affirm that Allah creates both good and evil but then deny one of them in reality, they effectively admit that the creator of evil in reality is distinct from the creator of good. Thus, their attempt to defend tawhid (oneness) actually leads to tathniya (dualism). The Mu'tazila's error is that they try to remove evil from Allah's creation by calling it metaphorical, but in doing so they fail to maintain a coherent monotheism against the dualists.

Key Terms
المعتزلة (Mu'tazila)
شر (evil)
مجاز (metaphor)
توحيد (tawhid)
تثنية (duality)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think the Mu'tazila are strict monotheists because they deny real evil from Allah.
Correction: Maturidi shows that their denial of real evil in creation leads to dualism, as it implies a separate creator of evil.

Checkpoint
Why does Maturidi consider the Mu'tazila approach in debating dualists erroneous?
A) Because they deny the existence of evil altogether
B) Because they concede that evil has a separate creator
C) Because they affirm both good and evil as real creations of Allah
D) Because they use metaphorical language which is unclear

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazila's attempt to defend tawhid by denying real evil ironically opens the door to dualism.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever caught yourself explaining away a painful event as 'not really from Allah' because it seemed too difficult to reconcile with His goodness? What would it mean for your faith to accept that every evil truly comes from His decree, without compromising His perfection? How might that change your patience and trust?
?
The Mu'tazila's error was subtle—they thought they were defending tawhid but ended up dividing the Creator. In what areas of your life do you unconsciously separate what you attribute to Allah (the good) from what you attribute to other causes (the bad)? What step can you take today to reunify your vision of Allah's absolute control over all things?

Refuting the Dualists on Evil
Maturidi refutes the dualist claim that evil is metaphorical.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the dualist claim that evil is only metaphorical leads to a contradiction with tawhid.

Core Idea
If evil is only metaphorical, dualism wins; tawhid requires real creation of good and evil by one God.

Imam al-Maturidi engages with the dualists (al-thunawiyya) who argued that evil is merely metaphorical and that God does not create evil acts. He shows that if we accept their premise, we end up affirming two creators — which is exactly what dualists believe. This lesson unpacks that refutation and its logical force.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis. Then read the translation. Notice how Maturidi structures his argument: he starts by describing the dualist view, then shows its implications.
2
Identify the dualist claim
Find where the passage states that substances (jawahir) are called evil only metaphorically. This is the key point Maturidi refutes.
3
Understand the logical consequence
Maturidi argues that if we accept the metaphor, then the one who creates evil in reality is different from the one who creates good — leading to dualism. Work through that logic step by step.
This is the heart of the refutation.
4
Connect to tawhid
Ask yourself: why does this matter for tawhid? The conclusion is that denying real creation of evil undermines God's sole sovereignty.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
شراً بالمجاز، فإنها طريق مناظرتهم الثنوية في إزالة ما ظنوه شراً أن يكون شراً. فأما أن يسلّموا الثنوية ويلزموهم القول بالخالق الواحد من الوجه الذي يوجد من غير الله تعالى الوجهان جميعا ويجعلوه على الصانع بعد القول بنفي ذلك، فهو محال فاسد؛ لما فيه تثبيت معرفته وتوحيده بخلق الشر والخير، ثم بنفي أحدهما في الحقيقة رجعت إلى قول الثنوية بأن الذي منه خلق الشر في الحقيقة غير الذي منه خلق الخير، فيلزمه التوحيد بالتثنية. ووجه قولهم في هذا أنهم أنكروا خلق أفعال العباد بما فيها السيئات والمعاصي والشرور، فعورضوا بخلق الشرور من الجواهر، وأنه لم يُسم به شريرا ولا مسيئًا، ولا في إفساد الأشياء مفسدا، فكذلك في خلق أفعال الشر والفساد لا يسمى به. فكان من جوابهم أن الجواهر سميت شراً على المجاز لا على الحقيقة، وهي في الحقيقة ليست بالشر. وأما عندنا فنحن نقول بأن الله جل جلاله خالق جوهر الشر والخير وخالق فعل الخلق شراً وخيراً. ولا يجوز كون شيء في سلطانه لم يخلقه، فيكون له شريك في سلطانه وعديل في خلق عالمه، جل الله عن ذلك وتعالى. ونقول بأن خلق الخلق ليس هو ذلك الخلق، وكذلك فعله؛ ولا يوصف فعله بالشر والخير، ولا يوصف بأن فعله خير وشر، لأنه موصوف بفعله. ولم نقل هو خير ولا شرير؛ ومن فعله ذلك في الحقيقة فهو مسمى به. ولا قوة إلا بالله.
(They called evil) evil metaphorically, for it is the way of debating the dualists in removing what they supposed to be evil. As for them conceding to the dualists and requiring them to affirm the one Creator from the aspect that both aspects (good and evil) exist from other than God, and making that (attribution) to the Maker after having denied it, that is impossible and corrupt; because it involves establishing His knowledge and tawhid by creating evil and good, then by denying one of them in reality it returns to the statement of the dualists that the one from whom the creation of evil comes in reality is other than the one from whom the creation of good comes, so tawhid would necessarily entail dualism. And the explanation of their statement in this is that they denied the creation of the acts of servants including evil deeds, sins, and evils, so they were confronted with the creation of evils from the substances (jawahir), and that He was not called evil or maleficent for that, nor a corrupter in corrupting things, so likewise in the creation of acts of evil and corruption He is not so called. So their answer was that the substances are called evil metaphorically, not in reality, and they are in reality not evil. As for us, we say that God, glorified be His majesty, is the Creator of the substance of evil and good, and the Creator of the act of creation both evil and good. It is not permissible that anything exist in His dominion that He did not create, for then He would have a partner in His dominion and an equal in the creation of His world; exalted is God above that. And we say that the creation of the creature is not that same creature, and similarly His act; His act is not described as evil or good, nor is it described that His act is good or evil, because He is described by His act. And we do not say that He is good or evil; whoever's act is that in reality is named by it. There is no power except with God.
Source pages p. 237

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer argument against the dualists (al-thunawiyya) who claimed that evil is only metaphorically attributed to material substances, and that God does not create evil acts. The refutation shows that if they concede the dualists' premise, they would be forced into a position that undermines tawhid. The Maturidi position is then stated: God creates both good and evil, but His act is not described as evil or good because He is described by His act, not by the moral quality of what He creates. The key distinction is between the act of creation and the created thing—God creates evil acts but is not called evil. The passage emphasizes God's sole sovereignty and the impossibility of a partner in creation.

Key Terms
ثنوية
مجاز
خير
شر
جوهر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That evil is only metaphorically real and not truly created by God.
Correction: The passage shows that if evil is merely metaphorical, then the real source of evil is distinct from God, which leads to dualism, contradicting tawhid.

Checkpoint
According to the refutation, what happens if one accepts that evil is only metaphorical?
A) It leads to affirming one Creator.
B) It leads to dualism.
C) It resolves the problem of evil.
D) It makes God not responsible for evil.

Key Takeaway
Denying that God truly creates evil forces a second creator, destroying the very unity (tawhid) you sought to protect.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face suffering or evil in your life, do you ever find yourself thinking that it comes from a source other than Allah—like bad luck, nature, or other people's malice? How does Maturidi's argument expose this as a subtle form of dualism that undermines your tawhid? What concrete step will you take this week to remind yourself that Allah alone creates both good and evil?
?
Have you ever tried to ‘defend’ Allah by downplaying the reality of evil, saying it's only metaphorical or not truly evil? Maturidi shows that such a defense actually leads to the very dualism it tries to avoid. Are you willing to accept that evil is real and that Allah created it for a wise purpose, even if you do not understand it? What must change in your heart to embrace this truth?
?
If you truly believe that Allah is the sole creator of everything, including the evil deeds you commit, how should that change the way you view your own sins? Do you blame yourself entirely, or do you acknowledge that Allah created the act while you are responsible for your choice? How can you use this lesson to develop both humility before Allah and resolve to repent?

God Creates Both Good and Evil Without Being Evil
Maturidi states the Sunni position on God's creation of good and evil.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Maturidi position that God creates good and evil, yet His act is not described as good or evil, and why this preserves tawhid.

Core Idea
God creates both good and evil, but His act of creation is not morally qualified.

After refuting the dualists, Imam al-Maturidi states the Sunni position clearly: God is the Creator of both the substance of good and evil and the acts of creatures. However, He is not described as evil or good based on what He creates, because His act is distinct from the act of the creature. This lesson explains that key distinction and its implications for divine sovereignty.

Guided Path
1
Read the Maturidi position
Read from 'وأما عندنا' to the end of the passage. Note the positive statements about God's creation and the negation that His act is described as evil or good.
This is the constructive part.
2
Understand the key distinction
Maturidi says 'خلق الخلق ليس هو ذلك الخلق' — the creation of the creature is not the creature itself. Similarly, God's act is not the same as the moral quality of what is created.
3
See why God is not called evil
The passage argues that God's act is not described as evil or good because He is described by His act (i.e., He is the Creator), and the moral label applies to the one whose act it is in reality.
Creatures are called good or evil based on their acts; God is the Creator of those acts but not subject to the same description.
4
Reflect on tawhid
The passage concludes that nothing can exist in God's dominion that He did not create, reaffirming absolute sovereignty without partners.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
شراً بالمجاز، فإنها طريق مناظرتهم الثنوية في إزالة ما ظنوه شراً أن يكون شراً. فأما أن يسلّموا الثنوية ويلزموهم القول بالخالق الواحد من الوجه الذي يوجد من غير الله تعالى الوجهان جميعا ويجعلوه على الصانع بعد القول بنفي ذلك، فهو محال فاسد؛ لما فيه تثبيت معرفته وتوحيده بخلق الشر والخير، ثم بنفي أحدهما في الحقيقة رجعت إلى قول الثنوية بأن الذي منه خلق الشر في الحقيقة غير الذي منه خلق الخير، فيلزمه التوحيد بالتثنية. ووجه قولهم في هذا أنهم أنكروا خلق أفعال العباد بما فيها السيئات والمعاصي والشرور، فعورضوا بخلق الشرور من الجواهر، وأنه لم يُسم به شريرا ولا مسيئًا، ولا في إفساد الأشياء مفسدا، فكذلك في خلق أفعال الشر والفساد لا يسمى به. فكان من جوابهم أن الجواهر سميت شراً على المجاز لا على الحقيقة، وهي في الحقيقة ليست بالشر. وأما عندنا فنحن نقول بأن الله جل جلاله خالق جوهر الشر والخير وخالق فعل الخلق شراً وخيراً. ولا يجوز كون شيء في سلطانه لم يخلقه، فيكون له شريك في سلطانه وعديل في خلق عالمه، جل الله عن ذلك وتعالى. ونقول بأن خلق الخلق ليس هو ذلك الخلق، وكذلك فعله؛ ولا يوصف فعله بالشر والخير، ولا يوصف بأن فعله خير وشر، لأنه موصوف بفعله. ولم نقل هو خير ولا شرير؛ ومن فعله ذلك في الحقيقة فهو مسمى به. ولا قوة إلا بالله.
(They called evil) evil metaphorically, for it is the way of debating the dualists in removing what they supposed to be evil. As for them conceding to the dualists and requiring them to affirm the one Creator from the aspect that both aspects (good and evil) exist from other than God, and making that (attribution) to the Maker after having denied it, that is impossible and corrupt; because it involves establishing His knowledge and tawhid by creating evil and good, then by denying one of them in reality it returns to the statement of the dualists that the one from whom the creation of evil comes in reality is other than the one from whom the creation of good comes, so tawhid would necessarily entail dualism. And the explanation of their statement in this is that they denied the creation of the acts of servants including evil deeds, sins, and evils, so they were confronted with the creation of evils from the substances (jawahir), and that He was not called evil or maleficent for that, nor a corrupter in corrupting things, so likewise in the creation of acts of evil and corruption He is not so called. So their answer was that the substances are called evil metaphorically, not in reality, and they are in reality not evil. As for us, we say that God, glorified be His majesty, is the Creator of the substance of evil and good, and the Creator of the act of creation both evil and good. It is not permissible that anything exist in His dominion that He did not create, for then He would have a partner in His dominion and an equal in the creation of His world; exalted is God above that. And we say that the creation of the creature is not that same creature, and similarly His act; His act is not described as evil or good, nor is it described that His act is good or evil, because He is described by His act. And we do not say that He is good or evil; whoever's act is that in reality is named by it. There is no power except with God.
Source pages p. 237

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer argument against the dualists (al-thunawiyya) who claimed that evil is only metaphorically attributed to material substances, and that God does not create evil acts. The refutation shows that if they concede the dualists' premise, they would be forced into a position that undermines tawhid. The Maturidi position is then stated: God creates both good and evil, but His act is not described as evil or good because He is described by His act, not by the moral quality of what He creates. The key distinction is between the act of creation and the created thing—God creates evil acts but is not called evil. The passage emphasizes God's sole sovereignty and the impossibility of a partner in creation.

Key Terms
خير
شر
خلق
جوهر
فعل

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, why is God not called evil even though He creates evil acts?
A) Because evil does not really exist.
B) Because His act is not described as evil or good; He is described by His act.
C) Because He only creates good, and evil comes from another source.
D) Because He creates evil only metaphorically.

Key Takeaway
God creates both good and evil without being evil because His act of creation is not subject to the moral descriptions that apply to creatures.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you witness evil in the world, do you ever feel that God is unfair? How does this passage's distinction between God's act of creation and the creature's act challenge that feeling—and what will you do the next time that feeling arises?

?
Do you secretly believe that God's goodness depends on Him only creating good things? This passage says God's act is not described as good or evil because He is described by His act—are you willing to trust that God's ways are beyond human moral categories, even when you don't understand them?

Refuting Dualism Through the Substance of Light
Maturidi refutes dualists by pointing out that even the substance of darkness uses light.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam Maturidi refutes dualism by demonstrating that even those supposedly from the substance of darkness depend on light, thereby undermining the claim of two independent eternal principles.

Core Idea
Even dualists depend on light, refuting their own theory.

In this passage, Imam Maturidi addresses dualists who claim two eternal principles: light (good) and darkness (evil). He constructs a clever argument from their own behavior: all humans, regardless of origin, speak and move by light. This shows that light is inherently superior, and the claim that evil is an independent source of evil collapses.

Guided Path
1
Read the argument carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice how Maturidi begins with a general observation about dualists.
The argument is purely logical, not scriptural.
2
Identify the key premise
The key premise is that all dualists agree they speak and move by the substance of light. Even those who claim to be from darkness do so.
This premise is based on observable behavior.
3
Understand the logical conclusion
Since even darkness-affiliated beings prefer light, light is superior. This refutes the idea that evil is an independent source of evil.
The intellect judges that preferring excellence is good.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم الذي ينقض على الثنوية على اختلافهم أنهم اتفقوا في جميع ما ينطقون به أنهم بجوهر النور ينطقون وبه يتقلبون فصار كل الاختلاف به إن صدقوا، وإن كذبوا فصار كل الكذب به، وإن صدق بعضهم وكذب بعض. فثبت ممن هو من جوهر الظلمة تفضيل النور حتى اختار الانتساب إليه دون الظلمة وتفضيل ذي الفضل خير في شهادة العقول، فيلزم بطلان القول بأصل هو شر لا يجئ منه غيره، وخير لا يجئ منه غيره. ولا قوة إلا بالله.
Then what refutes the dualists, despite their disagreement, is that they all agreed in everything they express that they speak by the substance of light (jawhar al-nur) and move by it, so all their differences are through it. If they are truthful, and if they lie, then all lying is through it, and likewise if some are truthful and some lie. Thus it is established that the one who is of the substance of darkness prefers light until he chooses affiliation with it rather than darkness, and preferring one of excellence over another is good in the testimony of intellects. So the invalidity of the statement that there is an origin that is evil from which nothing but evil comes, and an origin that is good from which nothing but good comes, is necessary. There is no power except with God.
Source pages p. 238

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the dualists based on a logical argument from their own actions. The dualists claim two eternal principles: light (good) and darkness (evil). Yet, according to the author, all humans, including those supposedly from the substance of darkness, speak and move by means of the substance of light. If even the people of darkness prefer light and choose to affiliate with it, this shows that light is superior. Since the intellect judges that preferring the excellent is good, the dualist claim that evil is an independent principle that produces only evil is invalidated. The argument thus destroys the core of dualism.

جوهر النور
جوهر الظلمة
تفضيل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The argument is merely rhetorical or based on scripture.
Correction: The argument is a logical refutation grounded in observable behavior and rational judgment, not revelation.

Checkpoint

A) By noting that both light and darkness create equally.
B) By pointing out that even those from darkness speak and move by light, showing its superiority.
C) By arguing that darkness is merely the absence of light.
D) By quoting scripture.

Key Takeaway
The dualists' own reliance on light proves that light is superior and their theory of two independent principles is invalid.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You rely on Allah's light (guidance and blessings) for every word you speak and every movement you make. When you sin, do you act as if you are independent of that light? How does this passage expose the contradiction in your own life? Will you repent and realign yourself today?
?
The dualists preferred light even while claiming darkness. What 'darkness' (sins, habits, beliefs) do you cling to while still benefiting from Allah's light? If you really see Him as the source of all good, why do you still choose that darkness?
?
Maturidi says that preferring excellence is good by the testimony of intellect. Your intellect knows that Allah alone is worthy of worship. What is holding you back from surrendering fully to Him? Will you let your intellect lead you to action today?

The Marcionite Cosmology: Light, Darkness, and the Human Medium
Maturidi presents the Marcionite view of light, darkness, and a medium.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Marcionite three-principle cosmology and how it differs from other dualist systems.

Core Idea
Marcionites uniquely posited a third medium—the human—between light and darkness.

Imam Maturidi presents the Marcionite doctrine to contextualize a heretical view that must be refuted. Understanding their cosmology helps us see how they departed from monotheism. This passage outlines their three principles and their hierarchical alignment.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the literal translation to grasp the overall statement.
The passage is a single continuous statement of Marcionite belief.
2
Identify the three principles
The passage enumerates light, darkness, and a medium. Note that the medium is described as the sentient human, which is neither light nor darkness.
Light is 'above', darkness 'below', and the medium is between them.
3
Understand the role of the human medium
The human is described as 'life in the body' and is the medium that intermingles with light and darkness. This is unique to Marcionites.
In other dualist systems, there are only two principles.
4
Notice the alignment hierarchy
The passage explains that each kind aligns with its neighbor: the medium aligns with light above and darkness below, like the sun and shadow.
This creates a hierarchy: light at top, darkness at bottom, medium in between.
5
Reflect on why this matters
Maturidi presents this to prepare for its refutation. Understanding the precise claim helps in recognizing the error.
This is a typical method in kalam: first present the opposing view accurately.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والمرقيونية قالوا بعلو النور وسفول الظلمة وبمتوسط بينهما ليس بنور ولا ظلمة، وهو الإنسان الحساس الدراك، والإنسان عندهم حياة في البدن، وأن هذه الثلاثة كانت متفرقة فامتزجت، وأن كل جنس منها يحاذي الذي يليه كمحاذاة الشمس الظل نحو أعلى؛ المتوسط يحاذي النور وأسفله الظلمة، والجوهران عند الأولين كذلك في التحاذي.
The Marcionites (al-Marqiyuniyya) said that light is above, darkness below, and a medium between them that is neither light nor darkness, which is the sentient, perceptive human. And man according to them is life in the body, and that these three were separate then intermingled, and that each kind of them aligns with that which is adjacent to it, like the alignment of the sun and the shadow towards the higher: the medium aligns with the light and its lower part with darkness, and the two substances according to the earlier ones are similarly in alignment.
Source pages p. 238

Commentary
This passage is an exposition of the Marcionite doctrine, a dualist sect that posits three principles: light (good, above), darkness (evil, below), and a medium (human) that is neither. Humans are described as life in a body, originating from the intermingling of the three. Each level aligns with its neighbor—the medium with light above and darkness below—forming a hierarchy. The passage sets up the Marcionite cosmology for subsequent refutation (likely on later pages).

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That Marcionites are identical to other dualists who only have light and darkness.
Correction: The passage clearly adds a third medium (the human) that is neither light nor darkness, making their cosmology tripartite.

Key Takeaway
The Marcionites taught a three-tier cosmology of light, darkness, and the human medium as a distinct, intermingled principle.

Refutation of Dualism: Light as the Source of All Actions
Maturidi refutes dualists using their own logic about light.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Maturidi refutes dualists by showing that their own admission that all speech and action come from light implies that light cannot be a purely good principle.

Core Idea
If both truth and falsehood come from light, then light is not purely good.

Dualists claim there are two eternal principles: pure good (light) and pure evil (darkness). Maturidi turns their own logic against them. He points out that dualists agree that all their speech and actions—whether true or false—originate from the substance of light. This undermines their claim that light is purely good, since falsehood and error also stem from it.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once to get a feel for Maturidi's style. Then read the English translation to understand the argument.
Notice how compact the Arabic is.
2
Identify the dualist premise
Maturidi starts by stating that dualists agree all speech and movement come from the substance of light. Find this phrase in the Arabic.
This is the key premise that everything spoken is by light.
3
Trace the implication: truth and falsehood from light
If all speech is by light, then both truthful and false statements come from light. Maturidi explicitly says this: 'if they speak truth, and if they lie, then all lying is by it.' Understand why this is a problem for dualists.
Dualists claim light produces only good, but here it produces falsehood (evil).
4
Understand the argument from preference
Maturidi notes that even someone from the substance of darkness prefers light, choosing affiliation with it. This shows light's superiority, which contradicts the idea of equal opposing principles.
If darkness were truly evil, its substance would not prefer light.
5
Draw the conclusion
Therefore, the dualist position that there are two independent principles, one pure good and one pure evil, is false. Maturidi ends with 'there is no power except with Allah.'
This refutation is based on the dualists' own accepted principles.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم الذي ينقض على الثنوية على اختلافهم أنهم اتفقوا في جميع ما ينطقون به أنهم بجوهر النور ينطقون وبه يتقلبون فصار كل الاختلاف به إن صدقوا، وإن كذبوا فصار كلّ الكذب به وكذا إن صدق بعضهم وكذب بعض. فثبت ممن هو من جوهر الظلمة تفضيل النور حتى اختار الانتساب إليه دون الظلمة وتفضيل ذي الفضل خير في شهادة العقول، فيلزم بطلان القول بأصل هو شر لا يجئ منه غيره، وخير لا يجئ منه غيره. ولا قوة إلا بالله.
Then what refutes the dualists despite their differences is that they agreed in all that they speak that they speak by the substance of light and by it they move, so all difference becomes by it: if they speak truth, and if they lie, then all lying is by it, and likewise if some speak truth and some lie. So it is established that one who is from the substance of darkness prefers light, to the point that he chooses affiliation to it rather than darkness; and preferring what has superiority is good in the testimony of intellects. So the claim of a principle that is evil from which nothing but evil comes, and a good from which nothing but good comes, is necessarily invalid. And there is no power except with Allah.
Source pages p. 238

Commentary
This passage presents a refutation of dualists regardless of their differences. Maturidi argues that they all agree that they speak and move through the substance of light. Therefore, both their truth and falsehood come from light. If they speak truth, it is from light; if they lie, it is also from light. If some are truthful and others false, still all comes from light. This shows that even those who are allegedly from the substance of darkness prefer light, choosing to attribute themselves to light rather than darkness. Preferring what is superior is deemed good by rational testimony. This refutes the claim that there exist two principles, one of pure good and one of pure evil, because if light produces both truth and falsehood, it cannot be purely good.

Key Terms

جوهر النور
جوهر الظلمة
تفضيل
شهادة العقول

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think Maturidi is attacking dualists by simply stating they are wrong.
Correction: Rather, he refutes them on their own grounds, using their agreed premise that all actions are from light.

Checkpoint
What is the main point of Maturidi's refutation of dualists?
A) Dualists contradict themselves by claiming two principles, yet all actions come from light
B) If both truth and falsehood come from light, then light is not purely good
C) Darkness is superior because it refrains from action
D) Dualists are simply ignorant of the truth

Key Takeaway
Maturidi refutes dualism by showing that their own principle of light being the source of all actions leads to a contradiction: light cannot be purely good if it produces falsehood and error.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

Will you now admit that both your virtues and your faults originate from your own soul and turn to Allah for purification?
?
The darkness-substance individual still chooses light, showing that even in wrong belief there is an inherent recognition of what is superior. Are you living in full submission to the truth you have recognized, or do you still cling to parts of darkness—excusing sins or delaying repentance? What dark habit will you abandon today because you know better?
?
Maturidi’s argument forces you to admit that if falsehood comes from the same source as truth, that source cannot be purely good. What does this say about your own heart, which produces both sincere worship and sinful thoughts? Are you willing to acknowledge the mixed nature of your inner self and cry out to Allah to make your heart entirely devoted to Him?

Marcionite Cosmology: Light, Darkness, and the Human
Maturidi expounds the Marcionite beliefs about light, darkness, and the intermediate human.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the core beliefs of the Marcionites as presented by Imam Maturidi, including their dualistic cosmology and the role of the human as an intermediate.

Core Idea
The Marcionites saw the human as a hybrid between light and darkness.

Imam Maturidi here outlines the beliefs of the Marcionite sect, a group that held a dualistic cosmology of light and darkness. They introduced a third element: the human being, who is a sensing, perceiving life in a body, serving as an intermediate. This lesson unpacks their unique worldview, which influenced early Islamic theological debates.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the concise style: Maturidi states their claims without initial commentary.
2
Identify the three entities
Find light, darkness, and the intermediate human. Note how each is described and positioned.
The human is 'sensing, perceiving' and 'life in a body'.
3
Understand the relationship
The passage describes mixing and alignment. Grasp that these three were separate but mixed, and each aligns with its neighbor.
Alignment is like sun-shadow: the intermediate aligns with both.
4
Reflect on the significance
Ask why Maturidi includes this sect. It shows the diversity of dualist beliefs and the need to refute them in Islamic theology.
The lesson title mentions 'exposition of their corruption'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أقاويل المرقيونية وبيان فسادها. والمرقيونية قالوا بعلو النور وسفول الظلمة وبمتوسط بينهما ليس بنور ولا ظلمة، وهو الإنسان الحساس الدراك، والانسان عندهم حياة في البدن، وأن هذه الثلاثة كانت متفرقة فامتزجت، وأن كل جنس منها يحاذى الذي يليه كمحاذاة الشمس الظل نحو أعلى؛ المتوسط يحاذى النور وأسفله الظلمة، والجوهران عند الأولين كذلك في التحاذى.
The sayings of the Marcionites and exposition of their corruption. The Marcionites said: light is above and darkness is below, and there is an intermediate between them that is neither light nor darkness, and it is the sensing, perceiving human; and the human, according to them, is a life in a body; and that these three were separate then mixed; and that each kind aligns with what is adjacent to it, like the alignment of the sun with the shadow towards above; the intermediate aligns with light and its lower part with darkness; and the two substances according to the earlier ones are likewise in alignment.
Source pages p. 238

Commentary
This passage expounds the beliefs of the Marcionites. They claimed that light is above and darkness below, with an intermediate between them that is neither light nor darkness: the sensing, perceiving human. For them, the human is a life in a body. These three (light, darkness, human) were originally separate but became mixed. Each type aligns with its adjacent type: the intermediate aligns with light above and darkness below, similar to how the sun's shadow aligns with the sun. The two substances (light and darkness) also align in this way according to the earlier dualists.

Correction: While both are dualist, the Marcionites specifically posit a human intermediate as a third principle, which is not found in Manichaeism.

The Marcionites believed in a tripartite cosmos with light, darkness, and a human intermediary that mixes the two.

?
The Marcionites thought light and darkness were eternal co-existing principles. How does this contradict the Islamic belief in the absolute oneness and sovereignty of Allah, and how should this truth change the way you face evil in the world today?

Refutation of the Marcionite and Sabian Views
Maturidi refutes the Marcionite intermediate and compares the Sabians to Manichaeans.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's logical refutation of the concept of an intermediate between light and darkness, and why it leads to either a single designer of good and evil or to the reduction of the intermediate to meaninglessness.

Core Idea
An intermediate between light and darkness is logically impossible or meaningless.

Maturidi first compares the Sabians to the Manichaeans, then launches into a powerful logical argument against the idea of an intermediate being (واسط) that mediates between light and darkness. He shows that whether this intermediate acts by design or by nature, its existence leads to absurd consequences. This passage demonstrates how kalam thinkers use reason to dismantle flawed metaphysical claims.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice how Maturidi begins by comparing the Sabians to Manichaeans before focusing on the intermediate.
2
Understand the Manichaean cosmology
The Manichaeans believe light and darkness meet in one direction and interpenetrate. Maturidi questions the origin of their claim.
Note that he refers to a previous discussion with dualists.
3
Analyze the two possibilities for the intermediate
The intermediate must either be by design (تدبير) or by natural association (طبع). Follow each branch to see where it leads.
If by design, then the designer is responsible for mixing good and evil. If by nature, the intermediate's perception is useless.
4
Consider the conclusion
Both branches lead to either a single source of good and evil or the elimination of the intermediate. Understand why Maturidi says the matter reduces to light and darkness.
This refutes the need for an intermediate entity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقول الصابئين مثل قول المنانية، إلا أن بينهما كما زعم ابن شبيب فرق قليل لا يَحدّه. والمنانية زعمت أن النور يلقى الظلمة من الشمال ذاهبًا في مهب الجنوب، والظلمة تلقاه من مهب الجنوب ذاهبة في مهب الشمال. وكانا متلاقيين على جهة دخول بعض الظلمة فيه، ولا يتناهيان من سائر الجهات. فتكلم في هؤلاء بمثل الذي تكلم في الثنوية بعد السؤال من أنهم من أين قالوا. ثم لا يخلو الواسط من أن يكون تدبيرا كان منه أمر العالم، أو على الاجتماع حدث منه. فإن كان بالتدبير بطل الامتزاج وأنى يقع وهو بين النور والظلمة. والظلمة من شأنها التسفّل ومن شأن النور العلوّ وبينهما فاصل يمنع، إلا أن يكون بالتدبير جمع بينهما وامتزاج هو به، فكان أصل كل شر؛ إذ كان من الامتزاج، ولولا أنه مزج بينهما ما وجد أحدهما سبيلاً إلى الآخر، فيصير الأمر إلى أن مدبر الخير والشر واحد. وإن كانا هما غلبا بالطبع وقهرا الواسط حتى امتزجا، فإذا لم ينفعه حسته ودَرْكه؛ إذ صار تحت قهر ذي الطبع، فكونه واسطا لا معنى له، وحصل الأمر على النور والظلمة.
And the saying of the Sabians is like the saying of the Manichaeans, except that between them, as Ibn Shabib claimed, is a slight difference that he did not define. The Manichaeans claimed that light meets darkness from the north going in the direction of the south, and darkness meets it from the direction of the south going in the direction of the north. And they were meeting in one direction by some of the darkness entering into it, and they do not end from the other directions. So he spoke about these people with the same that he spoke about the dualists after the question: from where did they say? Then the intermediate cannot be free from either being a design by which the affair of the world occurred, or having occurred from [natural] association. If it was by design, then mixing is invalid and how can it occur while it is between light and darkness? Darkness is characterized by descending and light by ascending, and between them is a separating barrier, unless by design He brought them together and mixed them, so He is the origin of every evil, since it was from mixing; and had He not mixed them, neither would have found a way to the other. Thus the matter leads to the designer of good and evil being one. And if they both overcame by nature and overpowered the intermediate until they mixed, then its sense and perception do not benefit it, since it comes under the coercion of the possessor of nature; so its being an intermediate has no meaning, and the matter reduces to light and darkness.
Source pages p. 239

Commentary
This passage first compares the Sabians to the Manichaeans, noting only a slight difference. Manichaeans believe light meets darkness from the north going south, and darkness meets light from the south going north, interpenetrating from one direction only. Maturidi then refutes the concept of an intermediate between light and darkness. He argues that the intermediate must either act by design (تدبير) or by nature (طبع). If by design, then the mixing is invalid because the intermediate is between light and darkness with a separating barrier, unless the designer deliberately brought them together, making the designer the source of all evil (since evil comes from mixing). This implies the designer of good and evil is one. If the intermediate was overcome by nature and forced to mix, then its perception and discernment are useless, rendering its existence as an intermediate meaningless. Ultimately, the outcome reduces to only light and darkness.

Key Terms
الصابئين
المنانية
الواسط
التدبير
الطبع
الامتزاج

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The intermediate is a real entity that mediates between light and darkness.
Correction: Maturidi shows that regardless of whether the intermediate acts by design or nature, its existence is either self-contradictory (if by design, the designer becomes the source of evil) or superfluous (if by nature, the intermediate has no effect). Therefore, the concept is logically untenable.

Checkpoint
In refuting the Marcionite intermediate, what does Maturidi conclude if the mixing occurred by design?
A) The intermediate is a free agent
B) The designer of both good and evil is one
C) Light and darkness are equal
D) The mixing is impossible

Key Takeaway
Maturidi's logical refutation shows that the concept of an intermediate between light and darkness is either self-defeating or superfluous.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face a difficult moral choice, do you ever try to rationalize a middle ground that avoids fully committing to truth? What did that cost you specifically?
?
If a neutral intermediate is meaningless, then where do you place the responsibility for the evil you have experienced or committed? Can you honestly attribute it to a neutral cause, or must you confront a deeper source?
?
Maturidi argues that a designed intermediate makes the designer the origin of evil. How does that confront your own understanding of God's role in your suffering? Are you willing to accept that God is sovereign over both good and evil in your life?

Refuting Dualism: The Mediator Argument
Maturidi presents the Sabian/Manichaean view and refutes the concept of a mediator between light and darkness.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam Maturidi refutes the dualistic concept of a mediator between light and darkness.

Core Idea
A mediator between opposites must be subordinate, collapsing dualism into monotheism.

Imam Maturidi addresses the dualistic claims of the Sabians and Manichaeans, who posit two eternal principles of light and darkness. He presents their view and then refutes the idea of a mediator that bridges them, showing that any such mediator either implies a single creator or is meaningless.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Notice the description of the Manichaean view and the two options for the mediator.
The passage uses a dense dialectical style typical of kalam.
2
Understand the dualistic claim
Note how the Manichaeans describe light and darkness meeting from opposite directions with some mixture. This sets up the need for a mediator.
3
Examine the mediator dilemma
Maturidi presents two possibilities: the mediator arises by design (tadbīr) or by coercion (by nature). Trace each option and see where it leads.
The argument is a reductio ad absurdum.
4
Reflect on the implications
Consider how both possibilities undermine dualism. If by design, good and evil come from one source. If by coercion, the mediator is superfluous.
This connects to the learning objective.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقول الصابئين مثل قول المنانية، إلا أن بينهما - [كما] زعم ابن شبيب ـ فرق قليل لا يَحدّه . والمنانية زعمت أن النور يلقى الظلمة من الشمال ذاهبًا في مهب الجنوب، والظلمة تلقاه من مهب الجنوب ذاهبة في مهب الشمال. وكانا متلاقيين على [جهة] دخول بعض الظلمة فيه، ولا يتناهيان من سائر الجهات. فتكلم [في] هؤلاء بمثل الذي تكلم [في] الثنوية بعد السؤال من أنهم من أين قالوا . ثم لا يخلو الواسط من أن يكون تدبيرا كان منه أمر العالم، أو على الاجتماع حدث منه . فإن كان بالتدبير بطل الامتزاج وأنى يقع وهو بين النور والظلمة. والظلمة من شأنها التسفّل ومن شأن النور العلوّ وبينهما فاصل يمنع ، إلا أن يكون بالتدبير جمع بينهما وامتزاج هو به ، فكان أصل كل شر ؛ إذ كان من الامتزاج، ولولا أنه مزج بينهما ما وجد أحدهما سبيلاً إلى الآخر، فيصير الأمر إلى أن مدبر الخير والشر واحد. وإن كانا هما غلبا بالطبع وقهرا الواسط حتى امتزجا ، فإذا لم ينفعه حسته ودَرْكه ؛ إذ صار تحت قهر ذي الطبع، فكونه واسطا لا معنى له، وحصل الأمر على النور والظلمة.
And the statement of the Sabians is like the statement of the Manichaeans, except that between them—as Ibn Shabib claimed—is a slight difference that does not define it. The Manichaeans claimed that light meets darkness from the north going in the direction of the south, and darkness meets it from the direction of the south going in the direction of the north. And they were meeting at [a direction] of some darkness entering into it, and they do not have an end from the other directions. So he spoke concerning these like what he spoke concerning the dualists after the question of from where they said. Then the mediator is not free from being either a design from which the matter of the world came, or occurring by accidental meeting. So if it was by design, the mixture is invalidated and how can it occur while it is between light and darkness? And darkness is characterized by descending and light is characterized by rising, and between them is a barrier that prevents, unless it was by design that He brought them together and a mixture that occurs by it, so it became the origin of every evil, since it was from the mixture, and were it not that He mixed them, neither would have found a way to the other. So the matter comes to that the designer of good and evil is one. And if they both overcame by nature and conquered the mediator until they mixed, then if his sense and perception do not benefit him, since he came under the coercion of the natural, then his being a mediator has no meaning, and the matter becomes that of light and darkness.
Source pages p. 239

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of dualistic doctrines, particularly those of the Sabians and Manichaeans, as well as the concept of a mediator (al-wasit) between light and darkness. Maturidi first notes that the Sabians and Manichaeans are similar, with only a slight undefinable difference according to Ibn Shabib. He then describes the Manichaean view: light meets darkness from the north, darkness from the south, and they meet in a certain direction with some darkness entering light, and they are infinite in other directions. Maturidi then addresses the mediator: it must be either a design that caused the world or an accidental meeting. He presents two possibilities: First, if the mediator is by design, then the mixture is invalid because light and darkness have opposite natures (light rises, darkness descends) with a barrier between them; if they are mixed by design, then the designer becomes the source of both good and evil, implying a single designer. Second, if the mediator is overcome by force of nature, then it is helpless and its role as mediator is meaningless, leaving only light and darkness. Thus, the dualistic position collapses.

Key Terms
صابئون
منانية
واسط
امتزاج
تدبير
طبع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think the mediator is a third independent entity.
Correction: Maturidi shows the mediator must be subordinate to either design (implying a single creator) or to nature (making it powerless), so it cannot be an independent principle.

Checkpoint
What are the two possibilities Maturidi considers for the origin of the mediator (al-wasit)?
A) By nature or by chance
B) By design or by coercion
C) By light or by darkness
D) By itself or by another

Key Takeaway
The mediator argument shows that dualism cannot logically posit a third entity between two opposing principles without undermining itself.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face a situation where good seems from God and evil from people or circumstances, does this passage challenge you to see God's hand in all things? What would change in your response to hardship if you truly believed that the same Lord designs both blessings and trials?
?
Have you ever harbored a subtle dualism in your heart—attributing your successes to your own effort and your failures to external forces? How does Maturidi's argument about a single designer confront that division?
?
If the mediator argument shows that any third entity is pointless, what 'mediators' do you rely on in your life—people, systems, or excuses—that you need to abandon in order to entrust everything directly to Allah?
