Refutation of the Magian Myth of God and Iblis
Maturidi narrates and refutes the Magian story of God's amazement and pact with Iblis.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Maturidi considers the Magian story about God and Iblis to be logically incoherent and worse than dualism.

Core Idea
The Magian myth makes God the source of evil, contradicted by divine perfection.

Maturidi quotes and then refutes a peculiar Magian (Zoroastrian) account of the origin of evil. The story claims that God admired His creation, feared opposition, and through a thought Iblis came into being. Maturidi shows that this narrative, far from solving the problem of evil, actually worsens it by attributing both good and evil to God.

Guided Path
1
Read the Magian claim
Identify the elements of the Magian story: God's admiration, fear, thought, and the emergence of Iblis. Notice how they try to separate good and evil.
2
Compare to dualism
Maturidi explains why dualists believed in two eternal principles. Contrast this with the Magians' acceptance of creation from nothing but their failure to avoid attributing evil to God.
Dualists at least keep good and evil separate, but Magians end up with both from God.
3
Analyze the refutation
Follow Maturidi's logical arguments: the inconsistency of the thought being evil yet from God, the problem of God's ignorance in the truce, and how this leads to contradiction.
The refutation shows the internal inconsistency of the Magian position.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أقاويل المجوس وبيان فسادها
قال الشيخ رحمه الله : قالت المجوس : أعجب الله حسن خلقه فتخوف ما يضاده فيه، فتفكر في ذلك فكرة ، فحدث منها إبليس . وقال بعضهم : أصابه بعينه فالتفت وراءه فرأى إبليس، فصالحه على أن يمهله إلى مدة ووادعه على ذلك، حتى إذا مضت المدة أهلكه الله . فكان من إبليس كل شر، ومن الله كل خير . وهذا الذي حكوا إن كان هو قولهم في الحقيقة فهم شر من جميع الثنوية؛ لأن الثنوية قالت باثنين لما رأوا خلق الشيء لا عن شيء غير متصور في الوهم [و]عظم عليهم القول بحدث العالم لا عن شيء. ثم رأوا العالم مشتملاً على خير وشر موصوفا كل من فعله الخير والعدل بالصفات المحمودة، ومن فعله الشر والجور بالصفات المذمومة، فاستعظموا نسبتهما إلى الواحد، فيكون واحداً محموداً مذموماً بما عليه العرف، فقالوا باثنين قديمين. والمجوس قد استجازوا حدث العالم لا عن شيء وأصل، وإنما عظم عندهم وصف من منه الخيرات بفعل الشر. [ثم إنهم لم يلزموه فعل إله شر [ولكن] صيروه أمه؛ إذ الفكرة الرديئة شر ، وما حدث [منها] وهو إبليس شر وكان منه ، فقد [ ثبت] منه الأمران جميعا، وهو السبب الذي دعاهم إلى القول باثنين فتناقض قولهم مع ما لا يؤمن منه حدوث الفكر وقتا بعد وقت، فيكون جميع الشر بذلك. وإن أريد إحالة ذا دل وجوده مرة على دفع الإحالة، إلا أن يقول بالخير، فلعل بدأه عن الفكرة التي هي خير . على أنه إذا وادعه على الترك إلى تلك المدة فإما أن لم يعلم أنه يعمل ما يعمل من الشر، والجهل شر، فهو شر آخر.
The sayings of the Magians and the exposition of their corruption. The Sheikh, may God have mercy on him, said: The Magians said: God admired the beauty of His creation and feared what would oppose Him in it, so He thought a thought about that, and from it Iblis came to be. And some of them said: He struck him with his eye, so he turned behind him and saw Iblis, and he made peace with him that he would give him respite for a period and made a truce with him on that, until when the period passed God destroyed him. So from Iblis was all evil, and from God all good. And this that they related, if it is truly their saying, then they are worse than all the dualists; because the dualists asserted two [principles] when they saw that the creation of a thing from nothing is inconceivable in imagination and they found it grave to say the world came to be from nothing. Then they saw the world containing good and evil, each one whose act is good and just being described with praiseworthy attributes, and the one whose act is evil and unjust being described with blameworthy attributes, so they deemed it great to attribute both to the One, so that He would be praised and blamed according to custom, so they said there are two eternal beings. But the Magians permitted the coming-to-be of the world from nothing and an origin, and what was grave to them was the description of the one from whom come good things as doing evil. [Then they did not attribute the act of the evil god to Him], but rather made it from Him; for the evil thought is evil, and what came about from it—and it is Iblis—is evil, and it was from Him, so both matters are [established] from Him, and that is the reason that called them to say there are two [principles], so their saying becomes contradictory with what cannot be safe from the occurrence of thought time after time, so all evil would be by that. And if impossibility is intended, its existence once indicates the refutation of impossibility, unless he says by good, then perhaps its beginning is from the thought that is good. Moreover, if he made a truce with him to refrain until that period, then either he did not know that he would do what he does of evil, and ignorance is evil, so it is another evil.
Source pages p. 241

Commentary
This passage presents and refutes the Magian (Zoroastrian) account of the origin of evil. Maturidi begins by quoting their claim: God admired His creation, feared opposition, so He thought, and Iblis (Satan) came from that thought. Some add that God accidentally looked upon Iblis and made a pact with him, giving him a temporary lease, after which God will destroy him. From Iblis comes all evil, from God all good. Maturidi then declares that if this is truly their belief, they are worse than the dualists. He explains that the dualists believed in two eternal principles because they could not conceive creation from nothing and saw both good and evil in the world. They attributed good to one deity and evil to another. However, the Magians accept creation from nothing, yet they balk at the idea that the good God does evil. But in their story, both good and evil come from God: the evil thought is from God, and Iblis is from that thought, so evil ultimately originates from God. Their position is contradictory because they try to separate good and evil but end up making God the source of both. Additionally, if God made a truce out of ignorance of Iblis's future evil, that ignorance is itself evil, compounding the problem. Maturidi refutes the narrative by demonstrating its inconsistency with divine perfection.

Key Terms
المجوس
إبليس
الفكرة
الثنوية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: God created evil through a thought or by making a pact with Iblis.
Correction: Maturidi shows this leads to attributing evil to God and contradicts His perfection.

Checkpoint
Why does Maturidi consider the Magians worse than the dualists (الثنوية) in this passage?
A) The Magians deny the existence of God
B) The Magians attribute both good and evil to God, while dualists separate them into two principles
C) The Magians believe in more than two eternal principles
D) The Magians reject the creation of the world

Key Takeaway
Maturidi refutes the Magian myth by demonstrating that it ultimately makes God responsible for evil, unlike dualism which at least separates the sources.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face trials or see evil in the world, do you ever, even in the depths of your heart, question God's perfection or attribute any flaw to Him? This lesson shows that any such thought is a dangerous departure from tawhid. What will you do to protect your belief when suffering strikes?
?
The Magians tried to separate good and evil but ended up making God the author of evil. Are there areas in your life where you subtly shift blame for your sins or shortcomings onto others or even onto divine decree, rather than taking full responsibility? How can you practice true repentance and accept that all evil comes from your own soul?
?
Maturidi exposes the absurdity of a God who makes a truce with evil without knowing the consequences. Reflect on your own trust in God's wisdom: do you sometimes wish He would explain His decrees to you? Are you willing to submit completely, even when you cannot see the wisdom? What one step can you take today to deepen your reliance on His perfect knowledge?

The Magian Account and Its Comparison to Dualism
Maturidi presents the Magian account and compares it to dualism.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Magian claim about the origin of Iblis and why Maturidi considers it worse than dualistic beliefs.

Core Idea
The Magians made God the source of evil, a greater contradiction than dualism.

The Magians, or Zoroastrians, attempted to explain the origin of evil by suggesting that God produced a thought that gave rise to Iblis. Maturidi begins by presenting this account and then contrasts it with the dualist position, showing that the Magians fall into a worse inconsistency.

Guided Path
1
Read the Magian claim
Read the opening lines carefully: 'The Magians said: God admired the beauty of His creation and feared what might oppose it, so He thought about that with a thought, and from that Iblis came into being.' Notice the attribution of evil to God's own action.
This claim is the target of Maturidi's refutation.
2
Understand the dualist position
Maturidi explains that the Dualists (al-Thanawiyya) posited two eternal principles—one good, one evil—because they found it inconceivable to attribute both good and evil to one God. Reflect on why this might seem reasonable to them.
The Dualists at least maintained a logical consistency.
3
Compare and identify the flaw
Maturidi argues that the Magians are worse because they accept creation from nothing—something the Dualists rejected—yet still end up attributing evil to God as its source through His thought. See how this leads to a self-contradiction.
The key point: the Magians' solution fails to solve the problem of evil.

فصالحه على أن يمهله إلى مدة ووادعه على ذلك، حتى إذا مضت المدة أهلكه الله . فكان من إبليس كل شر، ومن الله كل خير . وهذا الذي حكوا إن كان هو قولهم في الحقيقة فهم شر من جميع الثنوية؛ لأن الثنوية قالت الخيرات بفعل الشر. ثم إنهم لم يلزموه فعل إله شر ولكن صيروه أمه؛ إذ الفكرة الرديئة شر وما حدث منها وهو إبليس شر وكان منه، فقد ثبت منه الأمران جميعا، وهو السبب الذي دعاهم إلى القول باثنين فتناقض قولهم مع ما لا يؤمن منه حدوث الفكر وقتا بعد وقت، فيكون جميع الشر بذلك. وإن أريد إحالة ذا دل وجوده مرة على دفع الإحالة، إلا أن يقول بالخير، فلعل بدأه عن الفكرة التي هي خير. على أنه إذا وادعه على الترك إلى تلك المدة فإما أنْ لم يعلم أنه يعمل ما يعمل من الشر، والجهل شر، فهو شر آخر، أو علم فتركه على ما علم من الفساد به، فذلك منه شر. ومثله إما أن يكون علم من قبل ما يعمل فكره، ففكر على العلم بما يكون منه، وهو شر، أو لم يعلم، والجهل شر. ثم لا يخلو من أن يكون قادرا على منع إبليس وقهره أو لا. فإن قدر ثم أمهله ليفسد الخلق فهو شر عندهم، وإن لم يقدر فلا يكون العاجز رب العالمين. مع ما يقال : ثم علم أن إبليس عند المدة يفى له بالذي وعد. ووفاء الوعد خير وحق، فإذا يكون من الشر ذلك. مع ما كان هذا لازما له، لأنه إذا كان ممن هو أصل الخير يجئ الشر، فنعكس عليهم ونجعل كل خير من إبليس، وكل شر من غيره. وبعد، فكيف يأمن بالقدرة عليه في الوقت الذي لم يكن لإبليس غير نفسه عون وللذي به كان كل الأشياء أعوان؟ ثم اختلط خلقه الذين هم أعوانه بالذين هم أعوان الله في منعهم عن المعونة عليه. جل الله عما وصفه الملحدون. وإن قالوا: الموادعة كانت لبعض المصالح، فمثله الهوام الضارة والأشياء المؤذية. وبعد، فإن تخوفه من يضاده يوجب الجهل بأنه رب كل شيء؛ وكذلك إصابة العين، فإذا ضر به العين ومن تقهره العين، وتزيل قدرته، وتدفع علمه فهو رب بغيره لا بنفسه، خالق بغيره فيلزم القول في معبودهم: إنه عبد لا معبود. ثم لا شيء من تلك الجواهر المؤذية إلا وهو ينفع خلقا، فلم
The sayings of the Magians and the exposition of their corruption. The Shaykh, may God have mercy on him, said: The Magians said: God admired the beauty of His creation and feared what might oppose it, so He thought about that with a thought, and from that Iblis came into being. And some of them said: He (God) affected him (Iblis) with the evil eye, so He turned behind Him and saw Iblis, and He made peace with him to grant him respite for a period and made a truce with him on that, until when the period elapsed, God would destroy him. So from Iblis came all evil, and from God all good. This which they narrated, if it is indeed their statement in reality, then they are worse than all the Dualists (al-Thanawiyya); because the Dualists said with two (principles) since they saw the creation of a thing from nothing inconceivable in imagination, and the statement of the temporal origination of the world from nothing was difficult for them. Then they saw the world comprising good and evil, each one whose act is good and justice described with praiseworthy attributes, and one whose act is evil and injustice described with blameworthy attributes, so they considered it too great to attribute them to the One, for then He would be one praised and blamed according to custom, so they said with two eternal principles. And the Magians have permitted the temporal origination of the world from nothing and a principle, and what was difficult for them was the description of the one from whom come good things as doing evil. Then they did not make Him do the act of a god of evil, but rather made Him its source; since the base thought is evil, and what arose from it, namely Iblis, is evil, and it was from Him, so both matters have been established from Him, and this is the cause that called them to say with two (principles), so their statement contradicts itself, along with the possibility of the occurrence of thought at one time after another not being secure, so all evil would be by that. And if an impossibility is intended, its existence once indicates the repulsion of impossibility, unless He says with good, for perhaps its beginning was from the thought which is good. Moreover, if He made a truce with him to leave it until that period, then either He did not know that he would do what he does of evil — and ignorance is evil — so it is another evil, or He knew and left him upon what He knew of corruption from him, so that is from Him evil. And similarly, either He knew beforehand what he would do with His thought, so He thought upon knowledge of what would be from him, and it is evil, or He did not know, and ignorance is evil. Then it is inevitable that He is either able to prevent Iblis and overpower him or not. If He is able then He granted him respite to corrupt creation, so that is evil according to them; and if He is not able, then the incapable is not the Lord of the worlds. Along with what is said: Then He knew that Iblis at the period would fulfill what he promised; and fulfillment of the promise is good and right, so evil would come from that. Along with this being necessary for Him, because if He is the one who is the origin of good, evil comes, so we reverse upon them and make every good from Iblis and every evil from other than him. Moreover, how can He be secure in power over him at the time when Iblis had no help but himself, while the one through whom all things came has helpers? Then His creation, who are His helpers, interfered with those who are helpers of God in preventing them from assistance against him. Exalted is God above what the atheists describe. And if they say: the truce was for some benefits, then the same applies to harmful vermin and injurious things. Moreover, His fear of one who opposes Him necessitates ignorance that He is the Lord of everything; and likewise the affliction by the evil eye: if the eye harms Him and the eye overpowers Him, removes His power, and repels His knowledge, then He is a lord by other than Himself, not by Himself, a creator by other than Himself, so it necessitates saying about their object of worship that He is a servant, not a worshipped one. Then there is no harmful essence except that it benefits a creation, so it did not become
Source pages p. 241

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the Magian (Zoroastrian) explanation for the origin of evil. It begins by presenting their claim: that God, after creating the world, admired it and feared opposition, so He had a thought from which Iblis (Satan) arose. Some say God gave Iblis the evil eye, then made a truce with him, granting him respite until a set time when God would destroy him, attributing all evil to Iblis and all good to God. Maturidi then argues that if this is truly their belief, they are worse than the Dualists (al-Thanawiyya). He explains that Dualists posited two eternal principles because they could not conceive of creation from nothing and found it difficult to attribute both good and evil to one God; but the Magians actually accept creation from nothing and yet attribute the origin of evil to God Himself (through His thought), thus they fall into greater contradiction. The Magians made God the source (umm) of evil by saying that the base thought is evil, and Iblis came from it. Maturidi points out that this makes God responsible for both good and evil, which is the very problem the Dualists sought to avoid, so the Magians' position is inconsistent. He then examines the truce narrative: if God did not know Iblis would do evil, that is ignorance (evil); if He knew and allowed it, that is also evil. Similarly, if God is able to prevent Iblis but does not, that is evil; if He is unable, then He is not Lord. Maturidi also notes that the truce implies God trusted Iblis to fulfill his promise, but the fulfillment would be good, yet evil results—another contradiction. He then reverses the argument: if God is the source of good and yet evil comes from Him, one could just as well say all good comes from Iblis and all evil from another. Next, he questions how God could feel secure in power over Iblis when Iblis had no helpers while God had many, and those helpers were mingled with God's helpers? He then addresses the objection that the truce was for some benefit, saying this argument would also apply to harmful vermin—they too might have a beneficial aspect. Finally, he attacks the notion that God could be affected by the evil eye: if the eye can harm God, overpower Him, remove His power, and repel His knowledge, then He is not independent but dependent on another, which would make Him a servant, not a lord. The passage ends by noting that no harmful essence fails to benefit some creation.

Key Terms
المجوس
إبليس
الثنوية
الفكرة الرديئة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Magians are simply a type of dualist.
Correction: Maturidi shows they are worse because they accept creation from nothing and still make God the source of evil, whereas Dualists avoid that by positing two principles.

Checkpoint
Why does Maturidi consider the Magians worse than the Dualists (al-Thanawiyya)?
A) Because the Magians deny the existence of a good God.
B) Because the Magians accept creation from nothing and then attribute the origin of evil to God's own thought, leading to greater contradiction.
C) Because the Magians believe in three principles.
D) Because the Magians reject the truce between God and Iblis.

Key Takeaway
The Magians' attempt to absolve God of evil by making Iblis its source fails because they still trace evil back to God's own thought, making Him responsible for both good and evil.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you encounter evil or suffering in your life, do you ever find yourself silently attributing it to God in your heart, even as you profess that all good comes from Him? How does Maturidi's exposure of the Magian inconsistency challenge you to examine your own heart's attitude toward God in times of hardship?
?
The Magians tried to protect God's goodness by creating a separate source of evil. Are there areas in your life where you unconsciously erect compartments—things you label as 'from God' and things you feel He has nothing to do with? How might this compartmentalization actually distance you from a genuine trust in His absolute sovereignty and goodness?
?
If you were to be honest right now, do you truly believe that God is good even when you experience evil? Or is there a part of you that, like the Magians, seeks to shield Him from responsibility by blaming other factors? What specific step can you take this week to align your heart with the truth that God is entirely good, despite the presence of evil in the world?

Logical Contradictions in the Magian Truce Narrative
Maturidi critiques the truce narrative and exposes contradictions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical contradictions in the Magian story of a truce between God and Iblis, especially regarding God's knowledge and power.

Core Idea
The truce story forces God into ignorance or impotence, both of which are evil.

Maturidi now deconstructs the Magian narrative of a truce between God and Iblis. He shows that the story leads to absurd consequences about God's knowledge, power, and goodness, ultimately making God responsible for evil regardless of how the details are interpreted.

Guided Path
1
Examine the truce options
Maturidi presents a dilemma: either God knew Iblis would do evil or He did not. If He did not know, that is ignorance (evil); if He knew and allowed it, that is also evil. Follow each branch carefully.
This is a classic theological dilemma.
2
Consider God's power
Next, Maturidi asks whether God had the power to prevent Iblis. If He was able but did not, that is evil (allowing corruption). If He was not able, then He is not Lord. Both options undermine the Magian claim.
Think about what 'Lord' implies about power.
3
Reflect on the reversal argument
Maturidi then reverses the Magian logic: if God is the origin of good and yet evil comes from Him, one could equally say all good comes from Iblis and all evil from another. This exposes the arbitrariness of their attribution.
This shows the inconsistency in assigning moral origins.

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the Magian (Zoroastrian) explanation for the origin of evil. It begins by presenting their claim: that God, after creating the world, admired it and feared opposition, so He had a thought from which Iblis (Satan) arose. The Magians made God the source (umm) of evil by saying that the base thought is evil, and Iblis came from it. Maturidi points out that this makes God responsible for both good and evil, which is the very problem the Dualists sought to avoid, so the Magians' position is inconsistent. He then examines the truce narrative: if God did not know Iblis would do evil, that is ignorance (evil); if He knew and allowed it, that is also evil. Similarly, if God is able to prevent Iblis but does not, that is evil; if He is unable, then He is not Lord. Maturidi also notes that the truce implies God trusted Iblis to fulfill his promise, but the fulfillment would be good, yet evil results—another contradiction. He then reverses the argument: if God is the source of good and yet evil comes from Him, one could just as well say all good comes from Iblis and all evil from another. The passage ends by noting that no harmful essence fails to benefit some creation.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The truce narrative solves the problem of evil by limiting God's responsibility.
Correction: Maturidi shows it creates more problems: God either lacks knowledge or power, or acts unjustly.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, what follows if God knew Iblis would do evil but still granted him respite?
A) God is ignorant.
B) God is not the Lord.
C) God is responsible for the evil that results.
D) The truce is a good act.

Key Takeaway
The Magian truce narrative inevitably makes God either ignorant, impotent, or complicit in evil, contradicting their own premises.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When evil strikes your life, do you instinctively question God’s knowledge or power? How does Maturidi’s argument force you to reject those doubts and trust His perfect attributes?
?
Have you ever excused a personal sin by saying 'God knew I would do it anyway'? How does this lesson expose the danger of using divine foreknowledge to justify complacency?
?
If the Magian truce narrative is irrational, what does that reveal about your own attempts to explain away evil without upholding God’s honor? Will you commit to facing evil with humble submission rather than theological excuses?

intermediate

The Evil Eye Argument and Final Refutation
Maturidi refutes the evil eye claim and concludes the refutation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's refutation of the Magian claim that God can be affected by the evil eye, and how it undermines His lordship.

Core Idea
If the evil eye can harm God, then He is not the Lord; He is a servant.

Maturidi concludes his refutation by attacking the notion that God could have given Iblis the 'evil eye.' He argues that if God can be affected by an eye, He is not independent but dependent on others, which reduces Him to a mere creature. This final argument seals the case against the Magian cosmology.

Guided Path
1
Understand the evil eye story
Some Magians claimed God gave Iblis the evil eye, causing him to turn and see Iblis. Maturidi finds this notion absurd because it implies God can be harmed or influenced by a creature.
This is a physicalist and anthropomorphic view of God.
2
Analyze the consequence
If the eye can harm God, then His power and knowledge are not absolute. He would be a lord by another's permission, not by His own essence. This makes Him a servant, not the true God.
Maturidi uses a reductio ad absurdum.
3
Generalize the principle
Maturidi notes that every harmful thing also benefits some creation, so the Magians' argument for a truce due to some benefit would apply to all harmful things, nullifying their special case.
This undercuts the 'benefit' excuse for the truce.

والجور بالصفات المذمومة، فاستعظموا نسبتهما إلى الواحد، فيكون واحداً محموداً مذموماً بما عليه العرف، فقالوا باثنين قديمين. والمجوس قد استجازوا حدث العالم لا عن شيء وأصل، وإنما عظم عندهم وصف من منه الخيرات بفعل الشر. ثم إنهم لم يلزموه فعل إله شر ولكن صيروه أمه؛ إذ الفكرة الرديئة شر وما حدث منها وهو إبليس شر وكان منه، فقد ثبت منه الأمران جميعا، وهو السبب الذي دعاهم إلى القول باثنين فتناقض قولهم مع ما لا يؤمن منه حدوث الفكر وقتا بعد وقت، فيكون جميع الشر بذلك. وإن أريد إحالة ذا دل وجوده مرة على دفع الإحالة، إلا أن يقول بالخير، فلعل بدأه عن الفكرة التي هي خير. على أنه إذا وادعه على الترك إلى تلك المدة فإما أنْ لم يعلم أنه يعمل ما يعمل من الشر، والجهل شر، فهو شر آخر، أو علم فتركه على ما علم من الفساد به، فذلك منه شر. ومثله إما أن يكون علم من قبل ما يعمل فكره، ففكر على العلم بما يكون منه، وهو شر، أو لم يعلم، والجهل شر. ثم لا يخلو من أن يكون قادرا على منع إبليس وقهره أو لا. فإن قدر ثم أمهله ليفسد الخلق فهو شر عندهم، وإن لم يقدر فلا يكون العاجز رب العالمين. مع ما يقال : ثم علم أن إبليس عند المدة يفى له بالذي وعد. ووفاء الوعد خير وحق، فإذا يكون من الشر ذلك. مع ما كان هذا لازما له، لأنه إذا كان ممن هو أصل الخير يجئ الشر، فنعكس عليهم ونجعل كل خير من إبليس، وكل شر من غيره. وبعد، فكيف يأمن بالقدرة عليه في الوقت الذي لم يكن لإبليس غير نفسه عون وللذي به كان كل الأشياء أعوان؟ ثم اختلط خلقه الذين هم أعوانه بالذين هم أعوان الله في منعهم عن المعونة عليه. جل الله عما وصفه الملحدون. وإن قالوا: الموادعة كانت لبعض المصالح، فمثله الهوام الضارة والأشياء المؤذية. وبعد، فإن تخوفه من يضاده يوجب الجهل بأنه رب كل شيء؛ وكذلك إصابة العين، فإذا ضر به العين ومن تقهره العين، وتزيل قدرته، وتدفع علمه فهو رب بغيره لا بنفسه، خالق بغيره فيلزم القول في معبودهم: إنه عبد لا معبود. ثم لا شيء من تلك الجواهر المؤذية إلا وهو ينفع خلقا، فلم
The sayings of the Magians and the exposition of their corruption. The Shaykh, may God have mercy on him, said: The Magians said: God admired the beauty of His creation and feared what might oppose it, so He thought about that with a thought, and from that Iblis came into being. And some of them said: He (God) affected him (Iblis) with the evil eye, so He turned behind Him and saw Iblis, and He made peace with him to grant him respite for a period and made a truce with him on that, until when the period elapsed, God would destroy him. So from Iblis came all evil, and from God all good. This which they narrated, if it is indeed their statement in reality, then they are worse than all the Dualists (al-Thanawiyya); because the Dualists said with two (principles) since they saw the creation of a thing from nothing inconceivable in imagination, and the statement of the temporal origination of the world from nothing was difficult for them. Then they saw the world comprising good and evil, each one whose act is good and justice described with praiseworthy attributes, and one whose act is evil and injustice described with blameworthy attributes, so they considered it too great to attribute them to the One, for then He would be one praised and blamed according to custom, so they said with two eternal principles. And the Magians have permitted the temporal origination of the world from nothing and a principle, and what was difficult for them was the description of the one from whom come good things as doing evil. Then they did not make Him do the act of a god of evil, but rather made Him its source; since the base thought is evil, and what arose from it, namely Iblis, is evil, and it was from Him, so both matters have been established from Him, and this is the cause that called them to say with two (principles), so their statement contradicts itself, along with the possibility of the occurrence of thought at one time after another not being secure, so all evil would be by that. And if an impossibility is intended, its existence once indicates the repulsion of impossibility, unless He says with good, for perhaps its beginning was from the thought which is good. Moreover, if He made a truce with him to leave it until that period, then either He did not know that he would do what he does of evil — and ignorance is evil — so it is another evil, or He knew and left him upon what He knew of corruption from him, so that is from Him evil. And similarly, either He knew beforehand what he would do with His thought, so He thought upon knowledge of what would be from him, and it is evil, or He did not know, and ignorance is evil. Then it is inevitable that He is either able to prevent Iblis and overpower him or not. If He is able then He granted him respite to corrupt creation, so that is evil according to them; and if He is not able, then the incapable is not the Lord of the worlds. Along with what is said: Then He knew that Iblis at the period would fulfill what he promised; and fulfillment of the promise is good and right, so evil would come from that. Along with this being necessary for Him, because if He is the one who is the origin of good, evil comes, so we reverse upon them and make every good from Iblis and every evil from other than him. Moreover, how can He be secure in power over him at the time when Iblis had no help but himself, while the one through whom all things came has helpers? Then His creation, who are His helpers, interfered with those who are helpers of God in preventing them from assistance against him. Exalted is God above what the atheists describe. And if they say: the truce was for some benefits, then the same applies to harmful vermin and injurious things. Moreover, His fear of one who opposes Him necessitates ignorance that He is the Lord of everything; and likewise the affliction by the evil eye: if the eye harms Him and the eye overpowers Him, removes His power, and repels His knowledge, then He is a lord by other than Himself, not by Himself, a creator by other than Himself, so it necessitates saying about their object of worship that He is a servant, not a worshipped one. Then there is no harmful essence except that it benefits a creation, so it did not become
Source pages p. 241

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the Magian (Zoroastrian) explanation for the origin of evil. It begins by presenting their claim: that God, after creating the world, admired it and feared opposition, so He had a thought from which Iblis (Satan) arose. Some say God gave Iblis the evil eye, then made a truce with him, granting him respite until a set time when God would destroy him, attributing all evil to Iblis and all good to God. Maturidi then argues that if this is truly their belief, they are worse than the Dualists (al-Thanawiyya). He explains that Dualists posited two eternal principles because they could not conceive of creation from nothing and found it difficult to attribute both good and evil to one God; but the Magians actually accept creation from nothing and yet attribute the origin of evil to God Himself (through His thought), thus they fall into greater contradiction. The Magians made God the source (umm) of evil by saying that the base thought is evil, and Iblis came from it. Maturidi points out that this makes God responsible for both good and evil, which is the very problem the Dualists sought to avoid, so the Magians' position is inconsistent. He then examines the truce narrative: if God did not know Iblis would do evil, that is ignorance (evil); if He knew and allowed it, that is also evil. Similarly, if God is able to prevent Iblis but does not, that is evil; if He is unable, then He is not Lord. Maturidi also notes that the truce implies God trusted Iblis to fulfill his promise, but the fulfillment would be good, yet evil results—another contradiction. He then reverses the argument: if God is the source of good and yet evil comes from Him, one could just as well say all good comes from Iblis and all evil from another. Next, he questions how God could feel secure in power over Iblis when Iblis had no helpers while God had many, and those helpers were mingled with God's helpers? He then addresses the objection that the truce was for some benefit, saying this argument would also apply to harmful vermin—they too might have a beneficial aspect. Finally, he attacks the notion that God could be affected by the evil eye: if the eye can harm God, overpower Him, remove His power, and repel His knowledge, then He is not independent but dependent on another, which would make Him a servant, not a lord. The passage ends by noting that no harmful essence fails to benefit some creation.

Key Terms
إصابة العين

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: God can be affected by the evil eye like a human.
Correction: If so, He would not be God but a dependent being, contradicting His divinity.

Checkpoint
What does Maturidi conclude if the evil eye can harm God?
A) God is still all-powerful.
B) God is a servant, not a lord.
C) Iblis is all-powerful.
D) The truce was necessary.

Key Takeaway
The evil eye claim reduces God to a dependent being, undermining His essential lordship.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever acted as if God's power is limited by your actions—such as feeling that your sins 'harm' Him or that your worship 'benefits' Him? Reflect on how this subtle belief turns God into a dependent being. What specific thought or habit do you need to repent of today?
?
When you fear the evil eye or harm from others, do you truly believe that nothing can touch you except what Allah has decreed? Or does your fear reveal a hidden assumption that God can be overpowered? Examine your heart: where have you placed more trust in your own precautions than in Allah's absolute sovereignty?
?
Maturidi says that if the eye can harm God, then He is not truly worshipped. In your daily life, do you worship a God who is completely independent, or do you sometimes act as if He needs your defense or support? Commit to one change in your worship that acknowledges His absolute self-sufficiency.

Refuting the Denier: Intellect and Praise
Maturidi refutes a denier who claims intellect prohibits praise, showing it stems from ignorance.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's refutation of the claim that intellect forbids praising prophets, and how he exposes the denier's ignorance and desire-driven reasoning.

Core Idea
True intellect never forbids praise; desire masquerades as intellect.

This passage from Imam Maturidi directly confronts a skeptic who argues that reason itself prohibits praising prophets. Maturidi does not simply reject the claim — he diagnoses its root: the denier has confused his own desires with rational judgment. By exposing this confusion, Maturidi defends the rational basis of prophetic praise and affirms that sound intellect leads to recognition of truth.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own argumentative rhythm come through.
Notice Maturidi's direct engagement with the denier's claim.
2
Identify the two parts of the refutation
The passage has two main parts: first, Maturidi criticizes the denier's method of using observational reasoning without proper grounds; second, he directly analyses the claim about intellect and praise.
Look for the transition: 'يناظر فيما قال…'
3
Understand the diagnosis
Maturidi states that the denier's propositions issue from 'ignorance of the intellect' and are driven by desire (الهوى). Ask yourself: how does following desire lead to overturning realities?
4
Reflect on the solution
Maturidi says the denier should learn the reality of the intellect so he can invalidate falsehood and despise his own ignorance. Think about the role of self-awareness in correct judgment.
The phrase 'فبطل بحكمه ويمقت نفسه' is key.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المقابلات بالخروج على غير الحقائق من طريق العيان أظهر مما قال، ثم لم يجب به نفي علم العيان، فكيف وجب ما ذكر؟ وما ذكر من غير عصر الرسل فذلك كلام في قبول الأخبار يلزمه من وجه يُضطر إليه، فيُبطل سعيه. ويُناظر فيما قال: «إنه مما يحرمه العقل من المديح. فهذه حق لمن ذلك علمه مما يوجبه العقل أو الطبيعة: أن يجعل الكل ما هوته نفسه وما نفرت عنه طبيعته بضده. فيقلب الأحكام عن حقائقها، ويبيّن أنه عن جهل بالعقل خرجت قضاياه. فحق مثله أن يعلم حقيقة العقل، فيُبطل بحكمه ويمقت نفسه بجهله العقل من الهوى. والله الموفق.
The comparisons by going out from the truths by way of direct observation are more evident than what he said, then he did not answer with a negation of observational knowledge, so how is what he mentioned necessitated? And what he mentioned from other than the era of the messengers, that is speech about accepting reports that obligates him from a perspective that he is compelled to, so his effort is invalidated. And he argues regarding what he said: "Indeed, it is something that the intellect forbids from praise." This is true for one who knows that from what the intellect or nature necessitates: that he makes everything [according to] what his soul desires and what his nature recoils from as its opposite. So he overturns judgments from their realities, and it becomes clear that his propositions issued from ignorance of the intellect. So it is right for someone like him to know the reality of the intellect, so that he may invalidate [falsehood] by its judgment and despise himself for his ignorance of the intellect from desire. And God is the Granter of success.
Source pages p. 253

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of a denier of prophethood who argued that intellect prohibits praising messengers (or prophethood itself). Maturidi begins by stating that comparisons based on direct observation (العيان) are more evident than the denier's claim, yet the denier failed to negate observational knowledge, so his conclusion is unwarranted. Furthermore, the denier's argument about accepting reports from non-prophetic eras is self-defeating because it relies on accepting reports in a way that compels acceptance, thus invalidating his own stance. Maturidi then directly engages the denier's statement: 'Intellect forbids praise.' He explains that this claim holds only for one who, due to following his soul's desires and natural aversions, overturns truths and issues judgments from ignorance of the intellect's true nature. Such a person must learn the reality of the intellect so that he can use it to refute falsehood and condemn his own ignorance driven by desire (الهوى). The passage ends with 'والله الموفق' (And God is the Granter of success).

Key Terms
العيان
العقل
الهوى
المديح
قبول الأخبار

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Intellect inherently forbids praising prophets.
Correction: Maturidi shows that this claim arises from confusing personal desire with rational intellect; true intellect does not prohibit praise but leads to recognizing truth.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, what does the denier's claim that 'intellect forbids praise' actually reveal?
A) The denier has superior intellectual insight.
B) The denier is following his soul's desires and is ignorant of the true intellect.
C) The denier has correctly applied rational judgment.
D) The denier has proven that messengers are unnecessary.

Key Takeaway
Maturidi shows that the claim 'intellect forbids praise' is a symptom of following desire, not a rational conclusion; true intellect leads to recognizing truth.

Refuting Objections to the Quran's Inimitability
Maturidi refutes four objections against Quranic miracle.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the four objections raised by al-Warraq against the Quran's miracle and Maturidi's refutations showing why each objection fails.

Core Idea
The Quran's inimitability is proven by the Arabs' failure despite their intense effort and hostility.

In this passage, Imam Maturidi engages with the arguments of a skeptic, al-Warraq, who questioned the miraculous nature of the Quran. He presents four distinct objections and carefully refutes each, demonstrating that the Arabs' inability to produce a similar text was not due to lack of effort or external factors, but to its divine origin. This lesson will guide you through these objections and their refutations.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the literal translation, to grasp the flow of objections and responses.
2
Identify the four objections
Locate each of al-Warraq's objections: (1) difference in eloquence, (2) war occupation, (3) lack of reflection, (4) special gift or ability.
3
Understand Maturidi's refutations
For each objection, note how Maturidi counters it using logical arguments and historical evidence, such as the Arabs' long labor on poetry.
4
Reflect on the final summary
The passage ends with a second section emphasizing that the objections are implausible given the Arabs' willingness to sacrifice their lives.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم طعن الوراق المحتج بالقرآن بأوجه؛ أحدها تفاوتهم في البلاغة، ولعله تأليف أبلغهم. والثاني أن الحروب معه شغلتهم [عن إتيان مثله]. والثالث أنهم لم يكونوا أهل نظر ومعرفة، ألا ترى أنهم صدوا عن الإقرار مع توفر أسبابه عند أصحاب الضرورة، وعن النظر والمعرفة مع أسباب ذلك عند أصحاب الاكتساب. والرابع خصوص واحد بقوة من بين الجميع من غير أن يوجب ذلك له شيئًا، فمثله النبوة، أو أن يكون قدرتهم كانت بالفكر والتخيير، فلم يتكلفوا ذلك. فأما الأول فإنه لو كان ما قال يمتنعون عن ذلك بعد الجهد، فدل تركهم دونه أنهم تركوه طباعا. وأيضاً إنه لو كان كذلك لم يحتمل مثله ممن يقول: لئن اجتمعت الإنس والجن [الإسراء، 88/17] الآية، أن يكون أحد من البشر يبلغ علمه باللسان ذلك. والثالث/ أنه إذ نشأ بينهم، ومن عندهم عرف اللسان، فلولا أن له في ذلك من الله خصوصا لم يكن لغيره لا يحتمل أن يصير بهذا المحل. والرابع قد تكلفوا المجاوبات لأقوام معروفين في فن حتى اجتهدوا في قصيدة حولاً، فلو كان يحتمل وسعهم أو يرجون البلوغ بطرق ما احتمل تركهم، وفي ذلك تشنيع على القوم، وقد بذلوا مهجهم ودنياهم في إطفاء هذا النور. والفصل الثاني، لا يحتمل الذي ذكر، لما بذلك غنى لهم عن بذل المهج، ولما أُمهلوا
Then al-Warraq, who argued using the Qur'an, objected in several ways: First: their difference in eloquence, and perhaps it is the composition of the most eloquent of them. Second: that the wars with him occupied them [from bringing its like]. Third: that they were not people of reflection and knowledge – do you not see that they turned away from acknowledging despite the availability of its causes among those of necessity, and from reflection and knowledge despite the causes of that among those of acquisition. Fourth: the specificity of one [person] with power from among all without that necessitating anything for him, like prophethood, or that their ability was through thought and choice, so they did not undertake that. As for the first: if what he said [were true] – that they refrained after effort – then their abandonment of it would indicate that they abandoned it out of nature. Also, if it were so, it would not be plausible that one who says: 'If mankind and jinn gathered together' [17:88] the verse – that any human could reach with his knowledge of language that level. The third: since he grew up among them, and from them he learned the language, so if it were not that he had from Allah a specificity in that, it would not be plausible for anyone else to reach that station. The fourth: they undertook responses to known groups in an art until they labored over a poem for a year; so if it had been within their capacity or they hoped to reach it by means, they would not have abandoned it, and in that is a disgrace upon the people, and they sacrificed their lives and their world to extinguish this light. The second section: that what he mentioned is not plausible, because there was no need for them to sacrifice their lives, and they were not given respite.
Source pages p. 262

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of objections raised by a skeptic (al-Warraq) against the miraculous inimitability (إعجاز) of the Qur'an. Maturidi presents four objections and then answers them. The first objection is that the Arabs' inability to produce a text like the Qur'an might be due to the fact that the Qur'an is merely the composition of the most eloquent among them. The response: if the Arabs had the ability to produce its like after serious effort, their failure to do so shows that their abandonment was due to natural incapacity, not choice. Moreover, if that were the case, the Qur'anic challenge ('If mankind and jinn gathered...') would be empty, because no human could match it by mere linguistic skill. The second objection is that wars preoccupied the Arabs from attempting to produce a like. Maturidi does not directly answer here but moves on. The third objection is that the Arabs were not people of reflection and knowledge, as they rejected clear proofs. The response: the Prophet grew up among them and learned their language, so if he could produce the Qur'an only through a special divine gift, then no one else could reach that level. The fourth objection is that the Qur'an might be a special gift to one person (the Prophet) without being a miracle, or that the Arabs' ability was through thought and choice and they simply did not exert themselves. The response: the Arabs were known to labor over poetry for a year, so if they could have matched the Qur'an with effort, they would have done so, given their intense hostility and willingness to sacrifice everything to extinguish Islam. Their failure proves its divine origin. The final sentence ('والفصل الثاني') indicates a second refutation: the objection is implausible because if they could have matched it, they would not have needed to sacrifice their lives, and they were not given respite to do so.

Key Terms
طعن
الوراق
إعجاز
بلاغة
طباع
خصوص
نبوة
تكلف
تشنيح

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Arabs failed to produce a text like the Quran because of external factors such as war or lack of skill.
Correction: Maturidi shows that their intense effort in poetry and willingness to sacrifice everything prove that their failure was due to the Quran's divine origin, not external obstacles.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi's refutation, what does the fact that the Arabs worked for a year on a single poem indicate about their ability to produce something like the Qur'an?
A) They were too busy with poetry to attempt the Qur'an.
B) They had the ability but chose not to copy the Qur'an.
C) Their failure to match the Qur'an despite such effort proves its miraculous nature.
D) They were not interested in literary challenges.

Key Takeaway
The Quran's inimitability is confirmed by the Arabs' failure to produce its like, despite their literary expertise and desperate attempts to disprove it.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever entertained doubts about the Quran's divine origin because of objections like those of al-Warraq? What would it take for you to sincerely commit to studying its inimitability until those doubts are replaced with certainty?
?
The Arabs sacrificed their lives and worldly goods to extinguish the Quran's light. What are you sacrificing to keep that light alive in your own heart and in the world? Is your life a testimony to the truth of the Quran, or does it contradict it?
?
If you were challenged to produce something like the Quran today, would you even dare to try? What does your answer reveal about your conviction in its divine authorship—and what practical change does that conviction demand from you starting tonight?

Al-Warraq's Four Objections
Lists four objections by al-Warraq against the Quran's inimitability.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand al-Warraq's four objections to the Quran's inimitability and how they challenge the standard argument for i'jāz.

Core Idea
Al-Warraq's objections attack the Quran's divine origin from four angles: eloquence, distraction, ignorance, and special ability.

In this passage, Imam Maturidi presents the arguments of a critic named al-Warraq, who challenges the claim that the Quran is inimitable. Al-Warraq raises four distinct objections, each attempting to explain away the Quran's uniqueness. As you study these objections, keep in mind that Maturidi is not endorsing them — he is laying them out to refute them later.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that al-Warraq's objections are numbered.
2
Identify each objection
Find each of the four objections and note the reasoning behind each. The passage explicitly lists them with 'أحدها', 'والثاني', 'والثالث', 'والرابع'.
3
Reflect on how each objection challenges i'jāz
For each objection, ask yourself: how does this objection try to explain away the Quran's miraculous nature? This will prepare you for Maturidi's refutation.
The objections are: (1) disparity in eloquence, (2) distraction by wars, (3) lack of rational examination, (4) special ability of an individual.
4
Consider the context
Think about why Maturidi includes these objections. He is presenting the views of an opponent to later refute them. Understanding the objections helps you appreciate his response.
This passage is a set-up for a counter-argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم طعن الوراق المحتج بالقرآن بأوجه؛ أحدها تفاوتهم في البلاغة، ولعله تأليف أبلغهم. والثاني أن الحروب معه شغلتهم عن إتيان مثله. والثالث أنهم لم يكونوا أهل نظر ومعرفة، ألا ترى أنهم صدوا عن الإقرار مع توفر أسبابه عند أصحاب الضرورة، وعن النظر والمعرفة مع أسباب ذلك عند أصحاب الاكتساب. والرابع خصوص واحد بقوة من بين الجميع من غير أن يوجب ذلك له شيئًا، فمثله النبوة، أو أن تكون قدرتهم كانت بالفكر والتخيير، فلم يتكلفوا ذلك.
Then al-Warraq, who argued by means of the Qur'an, attacked in several ways: one of them is the disparity among them in eloquence, perhaps it being the composition of the most eloquent among them. The second is that the wars with him preoccupied them from producing its like. The third is that they were not people of examination and knowledge—do you not see that they were turned away from affirmation while its causes were abundant among the people of necessity, and from examination and knowledge while the means for that were present among the people of acquisition—and the fourth is a special [power] of one individual by strength from among all without that necessitating anything for him—for example prophethood—or that their power was by thought and choice, so they did not undertake that.
Source pages p. 262

Commentary
This passage enumerates four objections raised by a critic named al-Warraq against the argument that the Quran is a miracle because the Arabs could not produce its like. The first objection claims that the Arabs' disparity in eloquence suggests the Quran might be the composition of the most eloquent among them, not a divine speech. The second objection argues that the wars with the Prophet preoccupied the Arabs, preventing them from attempting to produce something similar. The third objection asserts that the Arabs were not people of rational examination and knowledge, as they rejected faith even when evidence was present, and they turned away from inquiry despite having the means. The fourth objection proposes that either a single individual was given a special power (like prophethood) to produce the Quran, or that the Arabs' ability was based on free thought and choice, but they did not bother to produce a similar text. These objections challenge the standard argument for Quranic inimitability (i'jāz).

Key Terms
الوراق
تفاوتهم في البلاغة
شغلتهم الحروب
أهل نظر ومعرفة
خصوص واحد بقوة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: These objections are Maturidi's own views.
Correction: They are objections raised by a critic, which Maturidi is presenting to refute.

Checkpoint
According to al-Warraq's first objection, why could the Quran be a human composition?
A) Because the Arabs were too busy with wars
B) Because of the disparity in eloquence among the Arabs
C) Because the Arabs lacked knowledge
D) Because a special individual was given power

Key Takeaway
Al-Warraq's four objections attempt to naturalize the Quran's uniqueness, but Maturidi's inclusion sets up their refutation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever secretly doubted that the Qur'an could be from God, thinking perhaps it was just eloquent speech? This lesson shows that even skeptics have raised such questions—but why does your heart feel uneasy when you consider that possibility? Confess honestly: do you approach the Qur'an as divine revelation or as a text to be judged by your own standards?
?
Al-Warraq claimed the Arabs were not people of examination and knowledge. But you are a person of examination today. How often do you actually examine the Qur'an's claims seriously? When was the last time you read even a single verse with the intention of understanding its divine message, not just reciting it? What would it take for you to become a true 'person of examination'?
?
The fourth objection suggests the Prophet had a special power. But you believe he was a prophet. If the Qur'an truly is from God, what are you doing in your life right now that proves you accept it as such? If you were to be honest, is your obedience to the Qur'an consistent with your claim that it is inimitable and divine? What would change today if you truly lived as if the Qur'an were the very words of your Creator?

Ibn al-Rawandi's Refutation of the Objection from Non-Muslim Consensus
An objection based on the consensus of Jews and Christians is refuted by Ibn al-Rawandi.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why citing the consensus of Jews and Christians is not a valid objection against Islamic claims, and how Ibn al-Rawandi turns the argument back on the objector using a dilemma about the acceptance of reports.

Core Idea
Consensus of false religions proves nothing; true consensus belongs to the People of Truth.

This passage presents a sophisticated refutation of an objection that tries to use the consensus (ijma) of Jews and Christians as evidence against Islamic doctrines. Ibn al-Rawandi constructs a logical dilemma that forces the objector to either reject all transmitted reports (which undermines his own position) or to accept reports and then must accept the consensus of the true believers in intellectual principles. Let's examine how this argument works.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
First, read the Arabic passage and its English translation carefully. Notice the concise and argumentative style of Ibn al-Rawandi.
Pay attention to the two options he presents.
2
Understand the objection
The objector is using the consensus (ijma) of Jews and Christians as evidence to challenge Islamic claims. Recognize why this might seem problematic.
Consensus from false religions could undermine the uniqueness of Islamic consensus.
3
Analyze the dilemma
Identify the two horns of the dilemma: (1) reject all reports (akhbar) absolutely, which invalidates the objector's own reliance on Mani's reports; (2) accept some reports, then must accept the consensus of the people of truth (ahl al-haqq) in rational principles.
Think about how accepting some reports leads to acknowledging the true believers' consensus.
4
See the conclusion
Ibn al-Rawandi concludes that his own group (the Muslims) are the people of truth, so their consensus should be accepted. The objection backfires.
The argument turns the tables on the objector.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وطعن أيضا بإجماعات اليهود والنصارى. قال ابن الروندي: إما أن ينكر الخبر ألبتة فيبطل مذهبه في تقليد الماني وقوله هذا، أو يجيز خبرا، لا بد إذ ذاك من الرجوع إلى إجماع أهل الحق في الأصول العقلية فيقبل أخبارهم وإجماعاتهم؛ إذ هم المتمسكون به، ونحن أولئك بحمد الله.
And he also objected with the consensuses (ijma'at) of the Jews and Christians. Ibn al-Rawandi said: Either he denies the report absolutely, thus invalidating his own doctrine in following al-Mani and this statement of his, or he permits a report; then necessarily he must refer to the consensus of the people of truth in the intellectual principles, so he accepts their reports and consensuses; for they are the adherents of it, and we are those, praise be to Allah.
Source pages p. 268

Commentary
This passage addresses an objection that uses the consensus (ijma) of Jews and Christians as evidence against Islamic claims. Ibn al-Rawandi presents a dilemma: either the objector rejects all reports (akhabar) outright, which would undermine his own reliance on the reports of Mani (the founder of Manichaeism), or he accepts some reports. If he accepts reports, he must then accept the consensus of the true believers (ahl al-haqq) in intellectual principles, because they are the ones who adhere to truth. Ibn al-Rawandi concludes that he and his school are those true believers, by the grace of Allah.

Key Terms
إجماعات
أهل الحق
الأصول العقلية
الماني

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The consensus (ijma) of any religious community, including Jews and Christians, can be used as evidence against Islamic doctrines.
Correction: This passage shows that the consensus of false religions is not authoritative; only the consensus of the people of truth (ahl al-haqq) in rational principles holds weight. The objector's argument backfires because it forces acceptance of Islamic consensus.

Checkpoint
What is the first horn of Ibn al-Rawandi's dilemma?
A) The objector must accept the consensus of Jews and Christians.
B) The objector rejects all reports, invalidating his reliance on Mani's reports.
C) The objector accepts all consensuses equally.
D) The objector denies intellectual principles.

Key Takeaway
Ibn al-Rawandi refutes the objection by showing that the objector cannot cite non-Muslim consensus without either undermining his own basis or accepting the consensus of the true believers.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you encounter an argument against your faith that appeals to the consensus of non-Muslims, does your heart ever waver? Ibn al-Rawandi shows that such an argument either destroys its own foundation or leads to acceptance of the truth. In what area of your life might you be relying on a worldview that would crumble if you applied the same logical scrutiny to it as Ibn al-Rawandi applies to the objector? What will you do today to examine one such inconsistency?
?
Ibn al-Rawandi confidently declares, 'we are those, praise be to Allah'—the people of truth. Do you share that same confidence that your intellectual principles are the truth? Or do you avoid examining your beliefs because you fear they might not hold up? Which belief of yours are you least willing to subject to Ibn al-Rawandi's method of turning the argument back on itself? Ask yourself: why?
?
The objector's position is self-defeating: he relies on reports to build his case, yet rejects reports when they support Islam. Are there any standards you apply to others that you do not apply to yourself? For example, do you require more evidence for Islamic truth claims than for secular worldviews? How can you use Ibn al-Rawandi's lesson to cultivate intellectual consistency in your own life starting today?

Is the Quran Khabar al-Ahad?
First objection and its refutation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first objection that the Quran is transmitted as single-chain reports (khabar al-ahad) and the response that it is actually mutawatir and even if it were ahad, it is still a proof.

Core Idea
The Quran is mutawatir, not ahad, and ahad reports can be proof.

The first objection against the Quran's reliability claims it is khabar al-ahad (single-chain report), thus unreliable. Maturidi records this and Ibn al-Rawandi's response: the Quran is transmitted by many from many (mutawatir), and even if it were ahad, the objector's own admission that such reports can be proof (hujja) undermines his criticism.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage carefully, then the English translation. Notice the direct style of objection and response.
2
Identify the objection and its claim
The objector claims the Quran's reports are ahad and therefore false. Mark how the objection is framed.
The objection is a common skeptical tactic.
3
Analyze the response
Ibn al-Rawandi gives two counterpoints: (1) the Quran is actually mutawatir (kulla 'an kulla), and (2) even if it were ahad, it is accepted as proof (hujja) according to the objector's own admission.
The second point turns the objection back on the objector.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وطعن في إخبار القرآن : إنه خبر الآحاد وذلك كذب، بل رواه كافة عن كافة، مع ما في هذا إقرار أنه حجة . وطعن التواتر بما لا تخلو الجماعة من البعد من السمع، فيحتمل الحيلة، أو القرب فلا يحتمل مباشرة مثله إلا اليسير قال ابن الروندي: هذه [هي] الجهلة بالمحافل، وإلا الأمر في ذلك ينتشر ما كان من قيد أو قيل، حتى لا يكاد شيء منه يخفى على الأبعدين فضلاً عن الأقربين. وطعن أيضاً بإجماعات اليهود والنصارى . قال ابن الروندي: إما أن ينكر الخبر ألبتة فيبطل مذهبه في تقليد الماني وقوله هذا، أو يجيز خبرًا، لا بد إذ ذاك من الرجوع إلى إجماع أهل الحق في الأصول العقلية فيقبل أخبارهم وإجماعاتهم؛ إذ هم المتمسكون به، ونحن أولئك بحمد الله .
And he [the objector] attacked the reports of the Qur'an: that it is a single-chain report (khabar al-ahad) and that is false, rather it is transmitted by many from many (kulla 'an kulla), along with the fact that in this is an admission that it is a proof (hujja). And he attacked tawatur (widespread transmission) by [arguing] that the group (al-jama'a) is not free from being distant from the hearing, so trickery is possible, or being close so that direct witnessing of the like is not possible except for a few. Ibn al-Rawandi said: These are the ignorant in the gatherings (al-mahafil), otherwise the matter in that [case] spreads, whatever was bound or said, until hardly anything of it is hidden from the farthest, let alone the nearest. And he also attacked the consensuses (ijma'at) of the Jews and Christians. Ibn al-Rawandi said: Either he denies reports altogether, thus nullifying his own doctrine in following Mani and this statement of his, or he accepts reports; then inevitably he must return to the consensus of the people of truth (ahl al-haqq) regarding the intellectual principles (al-usul al-'aqliyya), and thus he accepts their reports and their consensuses; for they are the ones holding to it, and we are those, praise be to God.
Source pages p. 268

Commentary
This passage presents three objections to the reliability of transmitted reports (tawatur) and their refutations by Ibn al-Rawandi. The first objection claims that the Quran is transmitted as ahad (single chains) and thus unreliable; the response is that it is actually transmitted by many from many (mutawatir), and furthermore, even if it were ahad, the objector's own admission that ahad reports can be proof (hujja) is implicit. The second objection attacks the concept of tawatur itself: the witnessing group may be far from the source, allowing for error, or too close to witness many events directly. Ibn al-Rawandi retorts that such objectors are ignorant of actual scholarly gatherings, because in reality, reports of significant events spread widely and become known even to distant people. The third objection points to the consensus (ijma') of Jews and Christians as an example of false consensus. Ibn al-Rawandi responds with a dilemma: either the objector rejects all reports entirely, which would nullify his own Manichaean tradition, or he accepts reports, in which case he must accept the consensus of the people of truth (ahl al-haqq) regarding rational principles, and that consensus belongs to the Muslims. This passage shows the dialectical method of refuting criticisms of tawatur.

Key Terms
خبر الآحاد
تواتر
حجة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Quran is transmitted as isolated reports (khabar al-ahad) and thus unreliable.
Correction: The Quran is mutawatir, transmitted by numerous chains at every generation, and even ahad reports are accepted as proof in Islamic epistemology.

Checkpoint
According to Ibn al-Rawandi's response, what is the status of the Quran's transmission?
A) It is khabar al-ahad
B) It is mutawatir (kulla 'an kulla)
C) It is uncertain
D) It is only for legal matters

Key Takeaway
The Quran is transmitted by many at each level (mutawatir), not as isolated reports, and even ahad reports are valid proofs.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You claim to believe the Quran is from Allah. But do you treat it as mutawatir in your heart—certain and undeniable—or do you let doubts seep in from ahad objections? Today, what is one verse you have been ignoring that you must now submit to as an undeniable proof?
?
The objector admitted that even ahad reports are proof. When you hear a weak argument against Islam, do you reject it outright, or do you examine it on its own terms? How can you strengthen your certainty in the Quran's message?
?
If the Quran is transmitted by many, does your life reflect that you have received it from the community? Are you isolated in your practice, or are you connected to the chain of believers who passed it down?

Objection Against Tawatur Itself
Second objection and refutation of tawatur

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second objection that attacks the concept of tawatur using distance/proximity arguments and Ibn al-Rawandi's refutation based on the spread of significant reports.

Core Idea
News of significant events inevitably spreads and becomes known to all, making tawatur reliable.

The second objection attacks the notion of tawatur (widespread transmission) directly: the observing group may be either too far from the event (allowing trickery) or too close (able to witness only a few things). Ibn al-Rawandi responds by pointing out the ignorance of such objectors regarding real scholarly gatherings, where reports of important matters spread widely and are known even to distant people.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Examine the objection's logic carefully: it claims tawatur fails due to distance or proximity.
2
Understand the objection's two-pronged attack
The objector says if the group is distant, trickery is possible; if close, they cannot witness many things directly. This is meant to undermine tawatur.
This is a skeptical argument against the reliability of collective transmission.
3
Analyze Ibn al-Rawandi's response
He calls the objectors 'ignorant in the gatherings' because in reality, important news spreads far and wide; nothing significant is hidden from the farthest, let alone the nearest.
The response appeals to common experience: major events become universally known.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

The first objection claims that the Quran is transmitted as ahad (single chains) and thus unreliable; the response is that it is actually transmitted by many from many (mutawatir), and furthermore, even if it were ahad, the objector's own admission that ahad reports can be proof (hujja) is implicit. جهلة
محافل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Tawatur is invalid because the reporting group might be too far or too close.
Correction: Ibn al-Rawandi shows that actual observation contradicts this: major reports spread and become known to everyone, regardless of distance.

Checkpoint
What does Ibn al-Rawandi call the objectors who attack tawatur?
A) The wise
B) The ignorant (جهلة)
C) The truthful
D) The scholars

Key Takeaway
Tawatur is reliable because important events inevitably become known to all, countering the skeptical argument about distance.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you encounter a doubt about a widely agreed-upon Islamic belief, do you treat it as a genuine possibility to be investigated, or do you recognize it as the product of ignorance—similar to Ibn al-Rawandi’s dismissal of the objector’s argument? Where does your uncertainty actually stem from?
?
The objector claimed that distance from an event makes reports unreliable. In your own life, have you ever distanced yourself from the company of scholars and found yourself questioning matters that were once clear to you? Are you willing to return to the 'mahafil' to recover that certainty?
?
Ibn al-Rawandi says that important news spreads so that hardly anything is hidden from the farthest. Consider one key Islamic truth (e.g., the prophethood of Muhammad, the reality of the Hereafter) that you know only through tawatur. Does this knowledge move you to action with the same force as if you had witnessed it yourself? If not, what is lacking in your certainty?

Objection from Consensus of Jews and Christians
Third objection and refutation using a dilemma

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the third objection that cites the consensus (ijma') of Jews and Christians to discredit tawatur, and Ibn al-Rawandi's dilemma response that forces the objector to accept the consensus of the people of truth.

Core Idea
If the objector accepts any reports, he must accept the consensus of the people of truth.

The third objection points to the consensuses (ijma'at) of Jews and Christians as examples of false consensus, implying that consensus itself is unreliable. Ibn al-Rawandi replies with a dilemma: either the objector rejects all reports (which nullifies his own Manichaean tradition) or he accepts reports—and if he does, he must accept the consensus of the people of truth (ahl al-haqq) regarding intellectual principles (al-usul al-'aqliyya), which are the Muslim scholars.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Notice the objector uses the false consensus of other religions to attack the concept of consensus itself.
2
Understand the dilemma presented by Ibn al-Rawandi
Two horns: deny all reports (which invalidates his own doctrine of following Mani) or accept reports (then he must accept the consensus of the people of truth).
The dilemma forces the objector into a contradiction.
3
Reflect on the conclusion
Ibn al-Rawandi identifies the people of truth (ahl al-haqq) as those who adhere to rational principles—and that is the Muslim community.
The response turns the objection into an affirmation of Muslim consensus.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Ibn al-Rawandi retorts that such objectors are ignorant of actual scholarly gatherings, because in reality, reports of significant events spread widely and become known even to distant people. The third objection points to the consensus (ijma') of Jews and Christians as an example of false consensus. Ibn al-Rawandi responds with a dilemma: either the objector rejects all reports entirely, which would nullify his own Manichaean tradition, or he accepts reports, in which case he must accept the consensus of the people of truth (ahl al-haqq) regarding rational principles, and that consensus belongs to the Muslims. This passage shows the dialectical method of refuting criticisms of tawatur.

Key Terms
إجماع
أهل الحق
الأصول العقلية
تقليد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The existence of false consensus (e.g., among Jews/Christians) invalidates the concept of consensus entirely.
Correction: Ibn al-Rawandi shows that if one accepts any reports, one must accept the consensus of those who adhere to rational principles (ahl al-haqq), which is the Muslim community.

Checkpoint
According to Ibn al-Rawandi, what happens if the objector denies all reports (al-khabar) altogether?
A) He becomes an atheist
B) He nullifies his own doctrine of following Mani
C) He must accept only intellectual principles
D) He is free from any obligation

Key Takeaway
If reports are accepted at all, the consensus of the people of truth (Muslims) on rational principles must be accepted.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you encounter a claim about religious truth, do you have a consistent method to accept or reject it? Or do you accept without scrutiny? Reflect on one belief you hold and ask yourself: would you accept it even if it were held by a false group? How do you know your criterion is objective?
?
The dilemma forces the objector to either abandon his own tradition or accept the people of truth. Are you willing to apply that same logic when your own assumptions are challenged? What cherished belief are you possibly protecting by double standards?
?
Ibn al-Rawandi argues that accepting any reports requires accepting the consensus of the people of truth. Does your acceptance of reports from the Quran and Sunnah lead you to submit to the consensus of the scholars? Or do you selectively pick and choose? Will you examine your posture toward scholarly consensus today?

Refuting the Objection of Jewish and Christian Consensus
Maturidi refutes the objection of Jewish and Christian consensus.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Maturidi argues that the consensus of Jews and Christians does not undermine the tawatur of prophetic reports.

Core Idea
Consensus on opinion does not equal consensus on miracles.

Maturidi now addresses a third objection: if consensus (ijma) is a proof, why not accept the consensus of Jews and Christians? He replies by distinguishing the type of matters on which they agree, showing that their consensus does not apply to the divine signs and critical truths that validate prophethood.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once to get the overall argument. Then read the translation to ensure understanding.
Notice how Maturidi contrasts two categories of matters.
2
Identify the two categories
Find where Maturidi describes what the consensuses of Jews and Christians are about, and what they are not about.
The key contrast is between 'matters of opinion' and 'signs/dangerous matters'.
3
Reflect on the significance
Ask yourself: Why does this distinction protect the concept of tawatur? How does it answer the objection?
This connects to the learning objective.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وما ذكر من إجماعات اليهود والنصارى إنما ذلك في أمور اختلفوا فيها على قدر ما احتمل آراؤهم فانتشرت في أتباع كل منهم، ليس ذلك في الآيات ولا في الأمور الخطرة.
And what was mentioned of the consensuses (ijma'at) of the Jews and Christians – that is only in matters in which they differed according to what their opinions could bear, and it spread among the followers of each of them; that is not regarding the signs (ayat) nor regarding dangerous matters (al-umur al-khatira).
Source pages p. 269

Commentary
This short passage is a specific refutation of the third objection raised earlier (the consensus of Jews and Christians). Maturidi argues that the consensuses of Jews and Christians are only on matters of opinion where they differ among themselves; these consensuses spread among their respective followers based on their own opinions. Such consensus does not apply to the divine signs (ayat) or to dangerous/critical matters (al-umur al-khatira) that are central to the prophetic missions. Therefore, the existence of Jewish and Christian consensus does not undermine the tawatur of prophetic reports, because prophetic reports concern miracles and vital truths, not mere opinions.

Key Terms
إجماعات
يهود
نصارى
آيات
أمور خطرة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Any consensus of a large group proves widespread transmission and thus can challenge the uniqueness of prophetic tawatur.
Correction: Maturidi distinguishes between consensus on opinions (which can spread from one source) and consensus on miracles and critical truths (which require tawatur). The Jewish and Christian consensus is only on opinions, not on the signs that validate prophethood.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, the consensuses of Jews and Christians are valid for what type of matters?
A) Matters of miracles and signs.
B) Matters of opinion in which they differ, not the signs.
C) All religious matters equally.
D) Only matters of history.

Key Takeaway
Jewish and Christian consensus only covers opinions, not the miracles that validate prophets.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever blindly followed a religious opinion because 'everyone around you believes it'? How can you ensure your faith is based on clear signs from Allah rather than inherited consensus?
?
If the most critical truths of your faith—like the prophethood of Muhammad (peace be upon him)—depend on tawatur, does your certainty match that? Or is your conviction stuck at the level of mere opinion?
?
When you share your beliefs with others, do you present them as matters of opinion or as confirmed truths backed by transmitted miracles? Which category would Maturidi place your most cherished belief in?
