The Inconsistency of Al-Warraq: Accepting Manichaean Reports Over Prophetic Ones
Ibn al-Rawandi criticizes al-Warraq for double standards.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Ibn al-Rawandi exposes al-Warraq's double standard in accepting Manichaean absurdities while rejecting prophetic reports supported by evidence.

Core Idea
Rejecting truth while embracing absurdity is the height of inconsistency.

The passage presents a sharp critique by Ibn al-Rawandi against al-Warraq, a contemporary who claimed to be a skeptic of prophethood. Despite demanding evidence for prophetic reports, al-Warraq uncritically accepted fantastical Manichaean stories. This lesson examines the force of that criticism and what it reveals about intellectual honesty.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Begin by reading the Arabic passage and its literal translation carefully. Notice the tone of astonishment and the specific Manichaean doctrines mentioned.
The passage is short but dense with sarcasm.
2
Identify the inconsistency
Pinpoint exactly what al-Warraq rejects (prophetic reports) and what he accepts (Manichaean reports). Understand why these are contradictory.
The contrast is between well-evidenced reports and absurd fables.
3
Reflect on the examples of absurdity
Consider the examples Ibn al-Rawandi gives: heavens from devil skins, earth disturbed by snakes, and activity of light and darkness. Ask why these are considered irrational.
These examples highlight the absurdity of Manichaean cosmology.
4
Connect to broader lesson
Think about how this critique relates to the reliability of transmitted knowledge and the importance of consistency in evaluating claims.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال ابن الروندي العجب من الوراق حيث جحد أخبار الرسل مع البراهين ودعا إلى قبول قول المنانية، وألزم القوم حماقاتهم من بسط السموات من جلود الشياطين، واضطراب الأرض باضطراب الحيات والعقارب فيها، وقبول أخبارهم بعمل النور والظلمة، ودفع ما هو في عقولهم حسنة. وبالله التوفيق.
Ibn al-Rawandi said: Amazing is al-Warraq who denied the reports of the messengers along with the proofs, and called for accepting the saying of the Manichaeans, and imposed on people their follies: spreading out the heavens from the skins of devils, the earth's disturbance by the disturbance of snakes and scorpions in it, and accepting their reports about the activity of light and darkness, and rejecting what is in their intellects as good. And with God is success.
Source pages p. 271

Commentary
This passage expresses astonishment at al-Warraq's inconsistency. He rejects the well-evidenced reports of the messengers yet accepts Manichaean doctrines that involve absurdities: heavens made from devil skins, earth disturbed by snakes and scorpions, and narratives about light and darkness. Furthermore, he rejects what reason clearly deems good. Maturidi (via Ibn al-Rawandi) points out the double standard.

Key Terms
المنانية
بسط السموات من جلود الشياطين
عمل النور والظلمة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think the passage provides a detailed refutation of Manichaeism itself.
Correction: The passage does not refute Manichaean doctrines in depth; it only uses them as examples to highlight al-Warraq's inconsistency.

Checkpoint
According to Ibn al-Rawandi, what does al-Warraq accept instead of prophetic reports?
A) Jewish traditions.
B) Christian teachings.
C) Manichaean sayings about light and darkness.
D) Philosophical reasoning.

Key Takeaway
Consistency in accepting or rejecting reports should be based on evidence, not bias, and absurd claims undermine credibility.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Where in your own life have you accepted a belief or claim that, if you were honest, you'd admit has less evidence than something else you've rejected? What is the real reason you cling to it?
?
Ibn al-Rawandi uses the word 'amazing' for al-Warraq's inconsistency. If your own patterns of accepting and rejecting truth were put on display, what would be the one thing that would most amaze an honest observer about your double standards?
?
What absurdity are you currently tolerating in your worldview simply because it comes from a source you trust, while you dismiss a truth from a source you distrust? Are you willing to re-examine that bias today?

Ibn al-Rawandi's Critique of Double Standards
Ibn al-Rawandi criticizes al-Warraq's double standard.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Ibn al-Rawandi exposes al-Warraq's double standard: rejecting prophethood while embracing Manichaean absurdities.

Core Idea
Selective skepticism betrays intellectual inconsistency.

In this passage, Ibn al-Rawandi highlights a striking contradiction in al-Warraq's position. Al-Warraq denies the reports of the prophets despite their compelling evidence, yet he uncritically accepts the bizarre myths of the Manichaeans. This lesson unpacks the criticism and its implications for evaluating claims to truth.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Observe the tone of astonishment from Ibn al-Rawandi.
The phrase 'العجب من الورّاق' sets the tone of criticism.
2
Identify the two opposing views
Note that al-Warraq rejects the reports of the messengers (أخبار الرسل) and yet calls for accepting the sayings of the Manichaeans (المنانية).
3
Examine the Manichaean absurdities
Ibn al-Rawandi lists examples: heavens stretched from skins of devils, earthquakes caused by snakes and scorpions, the interplay of light and darkness. These are presented as foolish.
The phrase 'حماقاتهم' (their foolishnesses) indicates the assessment.
4
Reflect on the inconsistency
Ask yourself: Why would someone reject well-evidenced prophetic reports but accept such myths? Ibn al-Rawandi points out the selective application of reason.
This is the core of the critique.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال ابن الراوندي: العجب من الورّاق حيث جحد أخبار الرسل مع البراهين ودعا إلى قبول قول المنانية، وألزم القوم حماقاتهم من بسط السموات من جلود الشياطين، واضطراب الأرض باضطراب الحيات والعقارب فيها، وقبول أخبارهم بعمل النور والظلمة، ودفع ما هو في عقولهم حسنة. وبالله التوفيق.
Ibn al-Rawandi said: Amazing is al-Warraq, for he denied the reports of the messengers despite the evidences, and called for accepting the saying of the Manichaeans, and imposed upon people their foolishnesses: that the heavens are stretched from the skins of devils, and the earth is disturbed by the agitation of snakes and scorpions in it, and accepting their reports about the actions of light and darkness, and rejecting what is good in their minds. And with Allah is success.
Source pages p. 271

Commentary
This passage is a polemical critique by Ibn al-Rawandi against al-Warraq. He finds it astonishing that al-Warraq rejects the reports of the prophets despite the clear evidences supporting them, yet he calls for accepting the doctrines of the Manichaean dualists. Ibn al-Rawandi lists the absurdities of Manichaean beliefs: that the heavens are made from the skins of devils, that the earth's disturbances are caused by snakes and scorpions, and that they accept reports about the interplay of light and darkness. Yet al-Warraq dismisses what is naturally recognized as good by human minds. The point is that critical standards are applied selectively. Al-Warraq uses reason to reject prophetic reports but uncritically accepts far less plausible myths.

Key Terms
الورّاق
ابن الراوندي
المنانية
أخبار الرسل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Rejecting prophetic reports is a sign of rational consistency.
Correction: Ibn al-Rawandi shows that selective rejection while accepting even more implausible myths reveals inconsistency, not rationality.

B) He rejects prophetic reports while accepting Manichaean myths
C) He believes in angels but not in devils
D) He follows reason in every matter

Key Takeaway
Ibn al-Rawandi exposes the double standard of rejecting justified prophetic reports while embracing absurd Manichaean myths.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
Ibn al-Rawandi notes that al-Warraq rejected 'what is good in their minds.' Is there a truth you know is right but have been avoiding because it would demand a change in your lifestyle? What would it take for you to submit to it completely?

The Dualists' Own Principle of Separation Justifies Sacrifice
Maturidi's first argument from separation and intermingling.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Maturidi uses the Dualists' own concept of separation and intermingling to argue that sacrifice is permissible.

Core Idea
The Dualists already accept separation—sacrifice is simply that separation.

Maturidi begins his refutation by pointing out an inconsistency in the Dualist position. They believe in two principles, light and darkness, and allow them to separate, intermingle, and separate again. But then they object to slaughter, which is precisely a separation. In this lesson, we examine how he turns their own metaphysics against them.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation to see Maturidi's first point.
Notice the key terms: تباين, امتزاج, ذبح.
2
Identify the Dualist premise
Identify that the Dualists allow تباين (separation) and امتزاج (intermingling).
3
See the conclusion
Understand that slaughter (ذبح) is essentially separation, so it aligns with their own system.
This undercuts their objection.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأحق من يقول [في] الثنوية لأوجه أحدها استجازتهم تباين النور والظلمة ثم الامتزاج ثم التباين، وفي ذلك تفرّق بين كل مقترنين وتميز بين كل ممتزجين، وذلك معنى الذبح. والثاني أن الألم إما أن يحل بجوهر النور فيصير محتملاً للأذى، وهو شر، ولولا ذلك لم ينه عن الذبح، إذ هو ذلك. ثم هو لا يخلو من أن يحل بجوهر النور، فقد عمل الشر، أو بجوهر الظلمة، فالنهي والإنكار مما لا معنى له؛ لأنه ينكر على من لا يحتمل طبعه القبول في ذلك، كمن يأمر من ليس له ما يطير [به] بالطيران أو أن يكون الألم يحل بجوهر وذلك هو الحق عندهم. ثم إما أن دخل عليه ذلك بجوهر النور فهو يصنع ما يذم عليه، أو بجوهر الظلمة فقد أحسن حيث آلم، إذ ذلك عدل. والله الموفق. وأيضا إن في الذبح إخراج الروح الصافي من الظلمة الكدرة، وذلك الحق، وهو عاقبة كل شيء.
And more rightful to speak [about] the Dualists are aspects: first, their allowing the separation of light and darkness, then intermingling, then separation—and in that is separation between every two associated things and distinction between every two intermingled things, and that is the meaning of slaughter. Second, that pain either befalls the substance of light, so it becomes susceptible to harm, which is evil, and were it not for that, he would not have prohibited slaughter, since it is that. Then it is inevitable that either it befalls the substance of light—then he has done evil—or it befalls the substance of darkness, so prohibition and denial are meaningless; because one denies someone whose nature is not capable of acceptance in that, like one who commands one who has no means to fly to fly. Or that pain befalls a substance, and that is the truth according to them. Then either that enters upon him by the substance of light—so he does what is blameworthy—or by the substance of darkness—so he has done well by causing pain, since that is justice. And God is the giver of success. And also, in slaughter is the extraction of the pure spirit from turbid darkness, and that is the truth, and it is the end of everything.
Source pages p. 273

Commentary
This passage refutes the Dualists (ثنوية) who believe in two principles: light and darkness. The author gives several points. First, the Dualists themselves allow separation and intermingling of light and darkness; slaughter (ذبح) is precisely a separation of the pure spirit from the dark body, so it aligns with their principles. Second, regarding pain: if pain affects the substance of light, it becomes susceptible to harm, which is evil; if not, they would not prohibit slaughter. If pain befalls light, then the agent does evil; if it befalls darkness, then prohibition is pointless because darkness is not receptive to harm, like commanding someone without wings to fly. Alternatively, if pain befalls a substance (either light or darkness), that is their truth. If it is a substance of light, the agent is blameworthy; if darkness, he does good by inflicting pain, as that is justice for darkness. Finally, the author adds that in slaughter, the pure soul is extracted from turbid darkness, which is the truth and the ultimate outcome of all things. Thus, the Dualist position is self-refuting when applied to sacrifice.

Key Terms
ثنوية
النور
الظلمة
الذبح
تباين
امتزاج

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the first argument Maturidi uses against the Dualists regarding sacrifice?
A) He argues that sacrifice is forbidden by light.
B) He argues that the Dualists' own acceptance of separation and intermingling justifies slaughter.
C) He denies the existence of darkness.
D) He says sacrifice is an act of darkness.

Key Takeaway
Maturidi uses the Dualists' own concept of separation to show that sacrifice is not inconsistent with their worldview.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In what area of your life do you hold a principle in one situation but deny it in another, like the Dualists accepting separation yet rejecting sacrifice? What would it cost you to be consistent?
?
When you separate yourself from a sin or a harmful attachment, do you recognize it as a kind of spiritual 'slaughter' that purifies? Or do you avoid the painful separation?
?
If your own reasoning contradicts itself, will you have the courage to abandon the inconsistency, or will you cling to it out of habit or pride? What step will you take today to align your actions with the truth you claim to believe?

The Dilemma of Pain in Dualist Metaphysics
Maturidi's second argument regarding the location and implication of pain.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's argument that the Dualists cannot consistently object to sacrifice based on pain.

Core Idea
Pain either harms light or darkness—either way, prohibition is invalid.

The Dualists might argue that sacrifice causes pain, which is evil. Maturidi responds by analyzing where pain can occur in their two-substance system. His argument shows that their own logic leads to absurdity or self-contradiction.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on pain
Read the Arabic passage about pain (الألم) and its possible locations.
Pay attention to the two possibilities: pain in light or darkness.
2
Analyze the first possibility
If pain befalls the substance of light, then light becomes susceptible to harm, which is evil. But if not, they would not prohibit slaughter.
3
Analyze the second possibility
If pain befalls darkness, then prohibition is meaningless because darkness is not receptive; it's like commanding a wingless bird to fly.
This leads to absurdity.
4
Consider the third alternative
If pain befalls a substance (their truth), then either it comes from light (blameworthy) or from darkness (praiseworthy as justice).
This completes the dilemma.

Commentary

First, the Dualists themselves allow separation and intermingling of light and darkness; slaughter (ذبح) is precisely a separation of the pure spirit from the dark body, so it aligns with their principles. جوهر

الظلمة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Dualists think that pain is always evil and so sacrifice is wrong.
Correction: Maturidi shows that pain's evil or good depends on which substance it affects, and in their own system, causing pain to darkness is just.

Checkpoint
What is Maturidi's point when he says that if pain befalls darkness, prohibition is like commanding someone to fly without wings?
A) Darkness is too pure to feel pain.
B) Darkness cannot receive pain, so objection is absurd.
C) Darkness is the source of all evil.

Key Takeaway
Maturidi demonstrates that the Dualist objection based on pain leads to absurdity or self-contradiction, leaving no ground to forbid sacrifice.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

?

Sacrifice as the Extraction of Pure Spirit
Maturidi's third argument: sacrifice as extraction of pure spirit from darkness.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's concluding argument that sacrifice aligns with the Dualists' own concept of ultimate truth.

Core Idea
Slaughter extracts the pure spirit from darkness—exactly what Dualist metaphysics aims for.

In his final point, Maturidi turns to the spiritual dimension of sacrifice. He argues that the act of slaughter liberates the pure soul from its dark, turbid container, which is precisely the goal of the Dualist worldview—the separation of light from darkness. This reinforces that sacrifice is not only permissible but metaphysically sound.

Guided Path
1
Read the final argument
Read the Arabic sentence about extracting the pure spirit.
Key terms: الروح الصافي, الظلمة الكدرة.
2
Understand the Dualist goal
The Dualists seek to separate light from darkness; sacrifice achieves this by freeing the pure spirit.
3
Reflect on the conclusion
Realize that this argument shows sacrifice is the very embodiment of their ultimate truth (عاقبة كل شيء).
This is a powerful rhetorical closure.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأحق من يقول [في] الثنوية لأوجه أحدها استجازتهم تباين النور والظلمة ثم الامتزاج ثم التباين، وفي ذلك تفرّق بين كل مقترنين وتميز بين كل ممتزجين، وذلك معنى الذبح. والثاني أن الألم إما أن يحل بجوهر النور فيصير محتملاً للأذى، وهو شر، ولولا ذلك لم ينه عن الذبح، إذ هو ذلك. ثم هو لا يخلو من أن يحل بجوهر النور، فقد عمل الشر، أو بجوهر الظلمة، فالنهي والإنكار مما لا معنى له؛ لأنه ينكر على من لا يحتمل طبعه القبول في ذلك، كمن يأمر من ليس له ما يطير [به] بالطيران أو أن يكون الألم يحل بجوهر وذلك هو الحق عندهم. ثم إما أن دخل عليه ذلك بجوهر النور فهو يصنع ما يذم عليه، أو بجوهر الظلمة فقد أحسن حيث آلم، إذ ذلك عدل. والله الموفق. وأيضا إن في الذبح إخراج الروح الصافي من الظلمة الكدرة، وذلك الحق، وهو عاقبة كل شيء.
And more rightful to speak [about] the Dualists are aspects: first, their allowing the separation of light and darkness, then intermingling, then separation—and in that is separation between every two associated things and distinction between every two intermingled things, and that is the meaning of slaughter. Second, that pain either befalls the substance of light, so it becomes susceptible to harm, which is evil, and were it not for that, he would not have prohibited slaughter, since it is that. Then it is inevitable that either it befalls the substance of light—then he has done evil—or it befalls the substance of darkness, so prohibition and denial are meaningless; because one denies someone whose nature is not capable of acceptance in that, like one who commands one who has no means to fly to fly. Or that pain befalls a substance, and that is the truth according to them. Then either that enters upon him by the substance of light—so he does what is blameworthy—or by the substance of darkness—so he has done well by causing pain, since that is justice. And God is the giver of success. And also, in slaughter is the extraction of the pure spirit from turbid darkness, and that is the truth, and it is the end of everything.
Source pages p. 273

Commentary
This passage refutes the Dualists (ثنوية) who believe in two principles: light and darkness. The author gives several points. First, the Dualists themselves allow separation and intermingling of light and darkness; slaughter (ذبح) is precisely a separation of the pure spirit from the dark body, so it aligns with their principles. Second, regarding pain: if pain affects the substance of light, it becomes susceptible to harm, which is evil; if not, they would not prohibit slaughter. If pain befalls light, then the agent does evil; if it befalls darkness, then prohibition is pointless because darkness is not receptive to harm, like commanding someone without wings to fly. Alternatively, if pain befalls a substance (either light or darkness), that is their truth. If it is a substance of light, the agent is blameworthy; if darkness, he does good by inflicting pain, as that is justice for darkness. Finally, the author adds that in slaughter, the pure soul is extracted from turbid darkness, which is the truth and the ultimate outcome of all things. Thus, the Dualist position is self-refuting when applied to sacrifice.

Key Terms
الروح الصافي
الظلمة الكدرة
الحق
الذبح

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Dualists think sacrifice is a dark act.
Correction: Maturidi shows that sacrifice actually separates light from darkness, aligning with their truth.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, what is the result of slaughter?
A) The pure spirit becomes more entangled in darkness.
B) The pure spirit is extracted from turbid darkness.
C) Both light and darkness are destroyed.
D) The darkness becomes pure.

Key Takeaway
Sacrifice, by extracting the pure spirit from darkness, fulfills the Dualists' own metaphysical goal.

The Dualist Meaning of Slaughter as Separation
First aspect of dualist argument: separation and mixing equate to slaughter.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the dualist concept of slaughter as separation and Maturidi's identification of it with their own principles.

Core Idea
Slaughter, for dualists, is just separation—but their own logic undermines any moral objection to it.

The dualists argue that sacrifice is wrong because it separates light from darkness. But Maturidi turns their own reasoning against them: if separation is allowed in their cosmology, why object to slaughter? This passage lays out the first of three aspects of their flawed argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the first aspect carefully
Read the Arabic passage for the first aspect and its translation. Notice how Maturidi defines the dualist position: they allow separation, mixing, then separation again.
The key term 'تباين' means separation or distinction.
2
Identify the logical move
Maturidi points out that this process of separation and mixing is exactly what happens in slaughter. Ask yourself: why does he equate the two?
He is showing that their objection to slaughter contradicts their own metaphysical principles.
3
Consider the implication
If dualists accept separation as a natural process, they cannot consistently condemn slaughter, which is merely another instance of separation.
This is a classic reductio ad absurdum.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأحق من يقول في الثنوية لأوجه أحدها استجازتهم تباين النور والظلمة ثم الامتزاج ثم التباين، وفي ذلك تفرق بين كل مقترنين وتميز بين كل ممتزجين، وذلك معنى الذبح. والثاني أن الألم إما أن يحل بجوهر النور فيصير محتملا للأذى، وهو شر، ولولا ذلك لم ينه عن الذبح، إذ هو ذلك. ثم هو لا يخلو من أن يحل بجوهر النور، فقد عمل الشر، أو بجوهر الظلمة، فالنهي والإنكار مما لا معنى له؛ لأنه ينكر على من لا يحتمل طبعه القبول في ذلك، كمن يأمر من ليس له ما يطير به بالطيران أو أن يكون الألم يحل بجوهر وذلك هو الحق عندهم. ثم إما أن دخل عليه ذلك بجوهر النور فهو يصنع ما يذم عليه، أو الظلمة فقد أحسن حيث آلم، إذ ذلك عدل. والله الموفق. وأيضا إن في الذبح إخراج الروح الصافي من الظلمة الكدرة، وذلك الحق، وهو عاقبة كل شيء.
And the most deserving of the dualists in [their] saying are three aspects: one of them is their allowing the separation of light and darkness, then mixing, then separation, and in that is separation between every pair and distinction between every mixed pair, and that is the meaning of slaughter. The second is that pain either occurs in the substance of light, so it becomes capable of receiving harm, and that is evil; and were it not for that, it would not be forbidden to slaughter, since it is that. Then it is not free from either occurring in the substance of light, so it has done evil, or in the substance of darkness, so prohibition and disapproval are meaningless; because one disapproves of one whose nature does not accept that, like one who commands one who has no wings to fly. Or that pain occurs in the substance—and that is the truth according to them. Then either that enters upon it by the substance of light, so it does what is blameworthy, or by the substance of darkness, so it has done good by causing pain, since that is justice. And God is the granter of success. And also, in slaughter is the extraction of the pure soul from the turbid darkness, and that is the truth, and it is the end of everything.
Source pages p. 273

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the dualist (Thanawiyya) position regarding sacrifice and the problem of pain. Maturidi presents three aspects (awjuh) of the dualist claim and critiques them. The first aspect: dualists allow the separation of light and darkness, then their mixing, then separation again, and they compare this to slaughter which separates life from the body. The second aspect: they argue that pain either affects the substance of light (making it susceptible to harm, which is evil) or the substance of darkness. If pain affects light, then the one causing pain does evil; if it affects darkness, then prohibition and disapproval are meaningless because darkness naturally accepts pain (like commanding a wingless bird to fly). Maturidi points out the incoherence: the dualist framework cannot consistently attribute pain and moral responsibility. The third aspect (implied) is that if pain enters through light, the agent is blamed; if through darkness, the agent has done good because causing pain to darkness is just. Maturidi then concludes that slaughter (extracting the pure soul from the turbid darkness) is actually the truth and the ultimate outcome for everything. This refutation undermines the dualist objection that sacrifice is inherently wrong by showing their own logic leads to contradictions.

Key Terms
ثنوية
النور
الظلمة
الذبح
الامتزاج
التباين

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The creation of light and darkness.
B) The separation of light and darkness, then mixing, then separation.
C) The mixing of pure and impure substances.
D) The extraction of the soul from the body.

Key Takeaway
Maturidi shows that the dualist objection to sacrifice collapses because their own cosmology embraces the very separation they condemn.

?
The dualists allowed separation in their metaphysics but rejected it in slaughter. Can you think of a time when you accepted a principle in theory but rejected its real-world application? How will you live out the full implications of your faith starting today?

Pain, Responsibility, and the Absurdity of Dualist Ethics
Second and third aspects: pain's location leads to moral absurdity; slaughter is actually extraction of soul from darkness.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's refutation of dualist arguments about pain and moral responsibility, and how their attribution of pain to light or darkness leads to absurd conclusions.

Core Idea
If pain occurs in darkness, blaming the agent is meaningless—like commanding a wingless bird to fly.

Dualists claim that pain either affects light (making it evil) or darkness (where it is just). Maturidi unravels this by showing that if pain belongs to darkness by nature, then prohibition is futile; if it belongs to light, then the agent is blamed for harming light. The passage concludes with a profound insight: slaughter actually extracts the pure soul from turbid darkness, aligning with truth.

Guided Path
1
Read the second and third aspects
Read the Arabic from 'والثاني' to the end. Notice the two possibilities for where pain occurs: in the substance of light or darkness.
The terms 'جوهر النور' and 'جوهر الظلمة' mean 'substance of light' and 'substance of darkness'.
2
Analyze the logical options
Follow Maturidi's reasoning: if pain in light → light becomes capable of harm (evil). If pain in darkness → prohibition is meaningless because darkness naturally accepts pain.
This is a dilemma that forces the dualist into incoherence.
3
Examine the final analogy
Maturidi compares prohibitting pain in darkness to commanding a wingless bird to fly. This highlights the absurdity of blaming an agent for an action that is inevitable by nature.
The analogy is a powerful rhetorical device.
4
Reflect on the concluding statement
The final sentence reframes slaughter as the extraction of the pure soul from turbid darkness, declaring it the truth and end of all things.
This directly counters the dualist rejection of sacrifice.

Christian Views on the Nature of Christ According to Maturidi
Maturidi catalogues Christian sects' views on Christ's composition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three Christian views on Christ's nature that Maturidi reports as a prelude to his refutation.

Core Idea
Maturidi reports Christian views to refute them, not to endorse them.

In this passage, Imam Maturidi begins his refutation of Christian doctrines by first accurately reporting what Christians believe about Christ. He distinguishes between those who say Christ has two spirits (human and divine) and those who say the spirit in Christ is God Himself. This careful reporting is part of his method: you must understand an opponent's position before you can refute it.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyze it. Then read the English translation. Notice the incomplete ending and the technical terms.
The passage is fragmentary; it breaks off at 'وفريقا'.
2
Identify the two main groups
The passage presents two main Christian groups: those who ascribe to Christ two spirits (one human, one divine part) and those who say the spirit in Christ is God Himself (not just a part). Locate these in the text.
3
Examine the subgroup within the second group
Within the second group, one subgroup holds that the spirit indwells the body as one thing resides in another. Consider how this differs from the two-spirit view and why Maturidi might detail these differences.
The passage ends before the opposing subgroup is described.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال الشيخ رحمه الله : وتفرقت النصارى في المسيح فمنهم من جعل له روحين؛ أحدهما محدث وهو روح الناسوتية، يشبه أرواح الناس، وروح لاهوتى قديمة جزء من الله، صار في البدن ذلك. وقالوا : ليس إلا أب وابن وروح القدس. وآخرون جعلوا الروح الذي في المسيح الله، لا الجزء. لكن فريقا منهم يجعله في البدن على كون الشيء في الشيء، وفريقا
The Shaykh, may God have mercy on him, said: The Christians differed concerning the Messiah. Some of them ascribed to him two spirits: one created, which is the spirit of humanity, resembling the spirits of people, and an ancient divine spirit, a part of God, which came into that body. And they said: There is only Father, Son, and Holy Spirit. Others made the spirit that is in the Messiah God, not the part. But a group among them makes it in the body as the indwelling of a thing in a thing, and a group
Source pages p. 286

Commentary
This passage is a classification of Christian views on Christ, likely part of a refutation. Maturidi reports that Christians split into factions. Some believed Christ had two spirits: a created human spirit (like other humans) and an ancient divine spirit that is a part of God, which entered the body. They likewise affirmed a trinity of Father, Son, and Holy Spirit. Another group held that the spirit in Christ is God Himself, not merely a part. Within that group, some believed this spirit indwells the body as one thing resides in another (perhaps a literal incarnation), while another subgroup (the text breaks off) held a different view. The passage is incomplete, ending abruptly at 'and a group'. The commentary notes that this is a standard doxographical account of Christian heresies, preparing for Maturidi's refutation.

المسيح

أب وابن وروح القدس

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Maturidi is presenting his own belief about Christ.
Correction: Maturidi is reporting Christian beliefs in order to refute them. He does not affirm these doctrines.

Key Takeaway
Maturidi reports that Christians held various views on Christ's nature, including a composite of human and divine spirits, and the indwelling of God in a human body, in order to prepare for their refutation.

Christian Views on Christ
Maturidi classifies Christian sects' doctrines about Christ's nature.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's classification of Christian doctrines regarding Christ's nature and the variations within their teachings.

Core Idea
Maturidi maps Christian views on Christ like a scholar charting errors.

Maturidi, in his systematic refutation of non-Islamic beliefs, first presents the actual doctrines held by Christians about Christ. He identifies several distinct positions, ranging from dual-spirit theories to various understandings of God's presence in Christ. This passage prepares the ground for his theological critique by accurately describing what Christians themselves claimed.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that Maturidi uses a very concise style to list different sects.
2
Identify the main groups
Find the different Christian positions Maturidi mentions. Look for phrases like 'منهم من جعل' (among them are those who made) and 'وآخرون' (and others).
There are at least three distinct positions described.
3
Analyze the variations
For each group, note what they say about the divine element in Christ: is it a part of God, or God Himself? How does it relate to the body?
Pay attention to the phrases 'على كون الشيء في الشيء' (as a thing in a thing) and 'على التدبير' (on administration).
4
Consider the purpose
Reflect on why Maturidi lists these views. He is not just describing but setting up a refutation. Understanding the views accurately is necessary to refute them.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وتفرقت النصارى في المسيح فمنهم من جعل له روحين؛ أحدهما محدث وهو روح الناسوتية، يشبه أرواح الناس، وروح لاهوتى قديمة جزء من الله، صار في البدن ذلك. وقالوا: ليس إلا أب وابن وروح القدس وآخرون جعلوا الروح الذي في المسيح الله، لا الجزء. لكن فريقا منهم يجعله في البدن على كون الشيء في الشيء، وفريقا [على] التدبير لا على إحاطة البدن به وفيهم من يقول: يصل إليه جزء من الله تعالى، ويفصل جزء آخر.
And the Christians divided concerning the Messiah. Among them are those who assigned to him two spirits: one created, which is the spirit of humanity, resembling the spirits of people, and a divine spirit, eternal, a part of God, which entered that body. And they said: There is only Father, Son, and Holy Spirit. And others made the spirit that is in the Messiah be God, not a part. But a group among them makes it in the body in the manner of a thing being in a thing, and a group [according to] administration, not by the body's encompassing it. And among them are those who say: A part of God, exalted, reaches him, and another part separates.
Source pages p. 286

Commentary
This passage is a classification of Christian views on Christ. Maturidi describes the main divisions: some say Christ has two spirits—a created human spirit and an eternal divine spirit that is a part of God. Others say the spirit in Christ is God himself, not a part. Within that, some say the divine part is in the body like a thing in a thing (spatially), while others say it administers the body without spatial containment. Still others say a part of God reaches him and a part separates. This sets up the refutation.

Key Terms
روح لاهوتى (divine spirit)
ناسوتية (humanity)
أب وابن وروح القدس (Father, Son, Holy Spirit)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Maturidi is endorsing one of these Christian views as correct.
Correction: Maturidi is simply describing the views of Christians in order to refute them later. He does not affirm any of these positions.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, which Christian view claims that a part of God entered Christ's body?
A) Those who say the spirit is God Himself
B) Those who say Christ has two spirits, one human and one divine part
C) Those who say the divine part administers from outside
D) Those who say Christ is fully God and fully man

Key Takeaway
Maturidi catalogues diverse Christian doctrines about Christ to systematically prepare for their refutation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Māturīdī carefully describes views he believes are false before refuting them. In your own faith journey, do you take the time to truly understand perspectives you disagree with—or do you dismiss them without listening? What would it cost you to listen first?
?
The passage shows how Christians used human reasoning to describe the nature of Christ and ended up in multiple contradictory positions. Are there any areas in your own understanding of God where you rely more on your own logic than on clear revelation? Will you submit to the Quran and Sunnah even when they challenge your intellect?
?
The first Christian group claimed that a 'part of God' entered Christ. Can you honestly say you have never, even subtly, compromised God's absolute oneness by attributing human limitations to Him in your thoughts or actions? What concrete step can you take this week to purify your understanding of tawḥīd?

Ibn Shabib on the Metaphorical 'Son' in Christian Terminology
Ibn Shabib explains the metaphorical use of 'son' in Christian terminology.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that some early Christian scholars used the term 'son' metaphorically to refer to Jesus, and how this compares to similar figurative language in Islamic tradition.

Core Idea
Christian 'son' is metaphorical, like calling the Prophet's wives 'mothers'.

This lesson presents a brief but important report from Ibn Shabib, a scholar who heard from Christian theologians that the term 'son' applied to Jesus was not literal. He draws parallels with Islamic figurative speech, where the Prophet's wives are called 'mothers of the believers' and a man may address another as 'my son'. Understanding this helps clarify the distinction between metaphorical and literal offspring.

Guided Path
1
Read the source passage
Read the Arabic text and its literal translation to grasp the essence of Ibn Shabib's report.
2
Understand the two key terms
Notice the distinction between 'son of the road' (metaphorical) and 'son of birth' (literal). Reflect on how this distinction impacts Christian claims about Jesus.
The term 'son of the road' implies a follower or disciple, not biological offspring.
3
Examine the analogies
Compare the Christian usage to Islamic figurative language: 'mothers of the believers' for the Prophet's wives, and the common address 'O my son'.
These analogies show that calling someone 'son' can be metaphorical in both traditions.
4
Consider the source context
Remember that this quote is not Maturidi's own theological view but a citation from Ibn Shabib. This methodological point is important for interpreting the text.
Maturidi is reporting an opinion, not necessarily endorsing it.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال ابن شبيب: سمعت من مولديهم أنه كان ابن التبنى لا ابن الولاد، كما سميت أزواج محمد عليه السلام أمهات، وكما يقول الرجل لآخر: يا بني.
Ibn Shabib said: I heard from their scholars that he was the son of the road, not the son of birth, just as the wives of Muhammad, peace be upon him, were called mothers, and as a man says to another: O my son.
Source pages p. 287

Commentary
This is a marginal comment by Ibn Shabib quoting Christian scholars who interpret 'son' metaphorically. They claim Christ is 'son of the road' (i.e., of the way) not literal offspring. The comparison is made to Islamic usage where the Prophet's wives are called 'mothers of the believers' and a man may address another as 'my son' figuratively.

Key Terms
ابن التبنى (son of the road)
ابن الولاد (son of birth)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that Maturidi himself is claiming that Jesus is metaphorically the son of God.
Correction: Maturidi is only reporting Ibn Shabib's citation of Christian scholars. The text does not necessarily endorse this interpretation; it is an example of how the term 'son' can be used figuratively.

Checkpoint
According to Ibn Shabib, how did some Christians describe Jesus?
A) As the literal son of God
B) As the son of the road
C) As the son of birth
D) As a prophet

Key Takeaway
Ibn Shabib's report shows that some Christians used 'son' metaphorically, similar to Islamic figurative language.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you hear someone use a term that seems to contradict your beliefs, do you immediately assume they mean exactly what you think, or do you pause to understand their intended meaning as Ibn Shabib did with the Christians? Will you commit to asking clarifying questions before judging this week?
?
The honorific 'mothers of the believers' gives deep respect to the Prophet's wives. How does reflecting on the metaphorical nature of this title change the way you honor them in your heart? Are you willing to extend similar respectful interpretation to the terms used by people of other faiths?
?
Ibn Shabib sought to understand Christian terminology from their own scholars. Is there a person (or group) whose beliefs you disagree with but have never tried to truly understand from their perspective? What one step will you take this week to listen to their explanation before offering your own?

Jesus' Crying and Supplication: Natural Acts, Not Divine Proof
Maturidi refutes the claim that Jesus's crying and supplication prove his special status.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the acts of crying and supplication are natural human behaviors and cannot serve as proof of divine status, as they are shared by other prophets like Moses.

Core Idea
Crying and supplication are natural, not exceptional.

Maturidi begins his refutation by addressing a common Christian argument: that Jesus's crying and supplication were meant to teach people. He counters that Moses did the same, and these are simply natural acts. This lesson examines that exchange and its implications.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice how Maturidi quotes a Gospel verse and then responds.
The quote 'This bitter cup…' is from Matthew 26:39.
2
Identify the claim and counterclaim
Find the Christian claim (that Jesus's crying was to teach) and Maturidi's response (Moses did it, it's natural).
3
Reflect on natural vs. divine
Consider why an act that is natural to humans cannot prove divinity. How does this apply to other arguments?
This is a key principle in kalam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
هذه الكأس المرة عن أحد فاصرفها عني . فإن قيل : كان من عيسى البكاء والتضرع ليعلم الناس قيل مثله من موسى. وبعد، فإنه وموسى كانا يصليان نحو بيت المقدس ويتضرعان. ثم البكاء والتضرع فعل الطباع لا تمتنع عنهما، فما معنى التعليم؟ ثم إن استحق هو ذلك بالعمل لزم ذلك في موسى وغيره. فإن قيل : استحق ذلك بإحياء الموتى لا غير، قيل : قد أحيا حزقيل إنسانًا . فإن عارض بالكثرة، قيل : اليهود يقولون موسى كان أكثر منه . وله إحياء عصا ميتة ثعبانا غير مرة، فهو أعظم. وإن احتج بإطعامه البشر الكثير من طعام يسير عورض بنبينا ، إنه أحدث في إناء دقيقاً لم يكن فيه فإن قيل : صير الماء خمرا، قيل : اليسع [أحدثه ] بلا عِدَّة [في] آنية لامرأة ثم صيّره زيتاً وإن احتج بالمشي على الماء فهم يقرون بذلك ليوشع بن نون ولإيليا واليسع . وإن استدلوا بالرفع إلى السماء فهم يقرون بذلك لإيليا ، وقالوا : ارتفع إلى السماء بمشهد من جماعة. وإن احتجوا بإبراء الأكمه والأبرص ونحو ذلك فإحياء الميت أعظم منه، وقد أقروا به لإيليا واليسع . مع ما عليهم في إقرارهم أن اليهود صلبوه وهزؤا به . فإن كان الأول يدل على التعظيم فهذا يدل على التصغير . وهلا صنع كصنيع إيليا حيث أتوه ـ أن أرسل عليهم نارا فأكلتهم، أكرمه الله به. وإن رجعوا إلى إظهار العجائب في تحقيق التخصيص عورضوا بمن ذكرت .
This bitter cup, turn it away from me. If it is said: Crying and supplication came from Jesus to teach the people, it is said: the like came from Moses. Moreover, he and Moses used to pray towards Jerusalem and supplicate. Then crying and supplication are acts of nature, they are not impossible; so what is the meaning of teaching? Then if he deserved that by works, that would follow for Moses and others. If it is said: he deserved that by raising the dead only, it is said: Ezekiel raised a person. If he objects by abundance, it is said: the Jews say Moses had more. And he revived a dead staff as a serpent many times, so it is greater. And if he argues by feeding many people with little food, he is opposed by our prophet, that he produced flour in a vessel that was not there. If it is said: he turned water into wine, it is said: Elisha [produced it?] without measure [in] a vessel for a woman then turned it into oil. And if he argues by walking on water, they acknowledge that for Joshua son of Nun and for Elijah and Elisha. And if they argue by ascension to heaven, they acknowledge that for Elijah, and they said: he ascended to heaven in the sight of a group. And if they argue by healing the blind and leprous and the like, revival of the dead is greater than that, and they acknowledged it for Elijah and Elisha. Along with what they have in their acknowledgment that the Jews crucified him and mocked him. If the first indicates veneration, this indicates belittlement. Why did he not do like Elijah's deed when they came to him, that he sent fire upon them and it consumed them, God honored him with it. And if they resort to displaying wonders in realizing specification, they are opposed by those I mentioned.
Source pages p. 288

Commentary
This passage is a series of refutations of Christian claims about Jesus's special status. The author begins by quoting a Gospel verse where Jesus asks the Father to take away the cup. He then addresses an objection: if Jesus's crying and supplication were meant to teach people, Moses did the same. Crying and supplication are natural acts, not special. If Jesus's merit came from works, then Moses and others also had works. If the claim is raising the dead, Ezekiel did that too. If more miracles, Moses had more. The author then lists miracles claimed for Jesus and counters each with examples from other prophets: turning water into wine is matched by Elisha's multiplication of oil; walking on water was done by Joshua, Elijah, Elisha; ascension by Elijah; healing the blind and leprous is less than raising the dead, which Elijah and Elisha did. Moreover, Christians admit Jesus was crucified and mocked, which belittles him. Jesus did not call down fire like Elijah. Thus the argument from miracles fails to prove Jesus's divinity.

Key Terms
بكاء
تضرع
طباع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That Jesus's crying and supplication demonstrate his divine teaching role, unlike other prophets.
Correction: Maturidi shows that Moses also cried and supplicated, making it a shared human act, not a proof of divinity.

Checkpoint
Which prophet does Maturidi compare to Jesus regarding crying and supplication?
A) Ezekiel
B) Moses
C) Elijah
D) Joshua

Key Takeaway
Crying and supplication are natural human actions, not proofs of divinity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you see someone weeping and supplicating to Allah intensely, do you automatically assume they are more spiritually advanced than others? How does Maturidi's argument that these acts are 'natural' challenge your assumption and force you to look deeper into sincerity?
?
Moses and Jesus both prayed towards Jerusalem and supplicated—yet one is a prophet of Islam and the other a prophet of Christianity. If outward acts do not prove status, what truly distinguishes a believer in your own heart? Are you guilty of judging your own faith by external emotions rather than internal conviction?
?
If crying and supplication are simply part of human nature, what does that mean for your own acts of worship? Will you stop relying on emotional highs as a sign of Allah's acceptance and instead focus on obedience even when your heart feels dry? What change will you make tonight in your prayer?

Raising the Dead: Not Unique to Jesus
Maturidi counters the claim that raising the dead proves Jesus's divinity by citing other prophets.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that raising the dead is not exclusive to Jesus, as other prophets like Ezekiel and Moses performed similar or greater acts.

Core Idea
Raising the dead is not unique; others did it too.

Christians claim Jesus's resurrection of others proves his divinity. Maturidi counters by citing prophets who also raised the dead, and even notes that Moses turned a staff into a serpent repeatedly. This lesson explores these comparisons.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage and translation. Notice the structure: if-then argument.
2
List the miracles mentioned
Identify the miracles: raising the dead (Ezekiel), raising a staff as a serpent multiple times (Moses).
The staff miracle is considered a form of revival.
3
Understand the quantity argument
Maturidi also uses the quantity of miracles: Moses had more than Jesus according to Jews.
This undermines the argument from abundance.

Source Text

?

?

?

Miracles of Provision: Feeding Multitudes and Transforming Substances
Maturidi refutes claims about Jesus's provision miracles by citing similar acts by Muhammad and Elisha.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that miracles involving food and water transformations are not exclusive to Jesus, as comparable miracles were performed by Prophet Muhammad and Elisha.

Core Idea
Feeding many and changing substances are shared miracles.

Christians point to Jesus feeding the multitude and turning water into wine. Maturidi counters by citing Prophet Muhammad's multiplication of flour and Elisha's transformation of oil. This lesson compares these miracles.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic and translation. Note the two claims and their counterexamples.
2
Identify each miracle and its counterpart
First: feeding many with little food → Muhammad produced flour from nothing. Second: water to wine → Elisha turned oil.
The Elisha miracle is from 2 Kings 4.
3
Consider the significance
Both claims are matched by miracles of other prophets, showing Jesus's acts are not unique.

?

?
The miracles in this lesson show that God provides from nothing. What is one worry you have about your provision (money, health, or time) that you have not fully surrendered to God? Take a moment to write a prayer of surrender, trusting that He can provide as He did for the prophets.

Walking on Water and Ascension: Shared Miracles
Maturidi counters claims of Jesus walking on water and ascension by citing earlier prophets who did the same.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that walking on water and ascension to heaven are not unique to Jesus, as they were also performed by Joshua, Elijah, and Elisha.

Core Idea
Walking on water and ascension are not exclusive to Jesus.

Christians cite Jesus walking on water and his ascension as proofs of divinity. Maturidi counters by noting that Joshua, Elijah, and Elisha also walked on water, and Elijah ascended to heaven. This lesson examines these biblical parallels.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic and translation. Note the two arguments: walking on water and ascension.
2
Identify the counterexamples
Walking on water: Joshua, Elijah, Elisha. Ascension: Elijah in sight of witnesses.
These are from Jewish tradition.
3
Reflect on the implication
If Jesus's miracles are shared, they cannot prove uniqueness or divinity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
هذه الكأس المرة عن أحد فاصرفها عني . فإن قيل : كان من عيسى البكاء والتضرع ليعلم الناس قيل مثله من موسى. وبعد، فإنه وموسى كانا يصليان نحو بيت المقدس ويتضرعان. ثم البكاء والتضرع فعل الطباع لا تمتنع عنهما، فما معنى التعليم؟ ثم إن استحق هو ذلك بالعمل لزم ذلك في موسى وغيره. فإن قيل : استحق ذلك بإحياء الموتى لا غير، قيل : قد أحيا حزقيل إنسانًا . فإن عارض بالكثرة، قيل : اليهود يقولون موسى كان أكثر منه . وله إحياء عصا ميتة ثعبانا غير مرة، فهو أعظم. وإن احتج بإطعامه البشر الكثير من طعام يسير عورض بنبينا ، إنه أحدث في إناء دقيقاً لم يكن فيه فإن قيل : صير الماء خمرا، قيل : اليسع [أحدثه ] بلا عِدَّة [في] آنية لامرأة ثم صيّره زيتاً وإن احتج بالمشي على الماء فهم يقرون بذلك ليوشع بن نون ولإيليا واليسع . وإن استدلوا بالرفع إلى السماء فهم يقرون بذلك لإيليا ، وقالوا : ارتفع إلى السماء بمشهد من جماعة. وإن احتجوا بإبراء الأكمه والأبرص ونحو ذلك فإحياء الميت أعظم منه، وقد أقروا به لإيليا واليسع . مع ما عليهم في إقرارهم أن اليهود صلبوه وهزؤا به . فإن كان الأول يدل على التعظيم فهذا يدل على التصغير . وهلا صنع كصنيع إيليا حيث أتوه ـ أن أرسل عليهم نارا فأكلتهم، أكرمه الله به. وإن رجعوا إلى إظهار العجائب في تحقيق التخصيص عورضوا بمن ذكرت .
This bitter cup, turn it away from me. If it is said: Crying and supplication came from Jesus to teach the people, it is said: the like came from Moses. Moreover, he and Moses used to pray towards Jerusalem and supplicate. Then crying and supplication are acts of nature, they are not impossible; so what is the meaning of teaching? Then if he deserved that by works, that would follow for Moses and others. If it is said: he deserved that by raising the dead only, it is said: Ezekiel raised a person. If he objects by abundance, it is said: the Jews say Moses had more. And he revived a dead staff as a serpent many times, so it is greater. And if he argues by feeding many people with little food, he is opposed by our prophet, that he produced flour in a vessel that was not there. If it is said: he turned water into wine, it is said: Elisha [produced it?] without measure [in] a vessel for a woman then turned it into oil. And if he argues by walking on water, they acknowledge that for Joshua son of Nun and for Elijah and Elisha. And if they argue by ascension to heaven, they acknowledge that for Elijah, and they said: he ascended to heaven in the sight of a group. And if they argue by healing the blind and leprous and the like, revival of the dead is greater than that, and they acknowledged it for Elijah and Elisha. Along with what they have in their acknowledgment that the Jews crucified him and mocked him. If the first indicates veneration, this indicates belittlement. Why did he not do like Elijah's deed when they came to him, that he sent fire upon them and it consumed them, God honored him with it. And if they resort to displaying wonders in realizing specification, they are opposed by those I mentioned.
Source pages p. 288

Commentary
This passage is a series of refutations of Christian claims about Jesus's special status. The author begins by quoting a Gospel verse where Jesus asks the Father to take away the cup. He then addresses an objection: if Jesus's crying and supplication were meant to teach people, Moses did the same. Crying and supplication are natural acts, not special. If Jesus's merit came from works, then Moses and others also had works. If the claim is raising the dead, Ezekiel did that too. If more miracles, Moses had more. The author then lists miracles claimed for Jesus and counters each with examples from other prophets: turning water into wine is matched by Elisha's multiplication of oil; walking on water was done by Joshua, Elijah, Elisha; ascension by Elijah; healing the blind and leprous is less than raising the dead, which Elijah and Elisha did. Moreover, Christians admit Jesus was crucified and mocked, which belittles him. Jesus did not call down fire like Elijah. Thus the argument from miracles fails to prove Jesus's divinity.

Key Terms
المشي على الماء
الرفع إلى السماء

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That only Jesus walked on water and ascended to heaven.
Correction: Joshua, Elijah, and Elisha walked on water; Elijah ascended.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, who ascended to heaven in the sight of a group?
A) Jesus
B) Joshua
C) Elijah
D) Elisha

Key Takeaway
Walking on water and ascension are not unique to Jesus; other prophets experienced them.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you hear about the miracles of a beloved figure like Jesus, do you ever feel that such experiences are reserved only for the most special? This lesson shows that others shared them. Where in your life have you overlooked God's gifts in ordinary people because you assumed greatness is exclusive? Will you now actively look for signs of God's favor in those you consider 'lesser'?
?
The Christians acknowledged these miracles for earlier prophets but still insisted on Jesus' divinity. Are there areas in your own belief where you hold onto an idea despite evidence that it is not unique? Are you willing to let go of a cherished assumption if it does not stand up to proof?

Healing, Crucifixion, and Final Refutation
Maturidi refutes healing claims and uses crucifixion and Elijah's actions to undermine Jesus's divinity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that Jesus's healings and even greater miracles like raising the dead were performed by other prophets, and that his crucifixion and mockery actually diminish claims of divinity, as he did not act like Elijah who called down fire.

Core Idea
Greater miracles exist, and Jesus's humiliation contradicts divinity.

Maturidi concludes his refutation by addressing healings, the crucifixion, and Jesus's failure to act like Elijah. He argues that if Jesus's miracles indicate veneration, his crucifixion indicates belittlement. This lesson ties together the overall argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the final part. Note the list of healings and the comparison with Elijah.
2
Understand the greater miracle argument
Maturidi says raising the dead is greater than healing, and Elijah and Elisha did that.
3
Reflect on the crucifixion argument
If Jesus was divine, why was he humiliated? Elijah was protected.
4
Consider the conclusion
All claims are countered by examples from other prophets, so the argument from miracles fails.

Source Text

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

?
The Christian claim that Jesus's healings prove his divinity is refuted by pointing to greater miracles in other prophets. In your own life, do you ever rely on outward signs or emotional experiences as proof that something is from Allah? How can you guard against misinterpreting blessings or trials as exclusive signs of divine favor or disfavor, and instead anchor your faith solely on the Quran and authentic revelation?

The Dialectical Argument on Jesus's Power
Maturidi dialectically refutes Christian claims about Jesus's creative power.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's dialectical refutation of Christian claims about Jesus's actions, demonstrating that attributing creative power to Jesus leads to contradictions about God's nature.

Core Idea
Jesus's power cannot be a part of God without compromising divine unity.

This passage presents a classical Islamic theological argument against the Christian claim that Jesus performed miracles by God's power. Maturidi uses a dialectical method, posing questions that force the opponent into logical contradictions. As you work through this lesson, you will see how the argument systematically eliminates possible explanations for Jesus's actions, ultimately affirming that only God is the true agent.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Understand the flow of the argument before diving into details.
Notice the repeated conditional constructions (if... then...).
2
Identify the initial claim and the first question
The passage starts with the Christian claim that God is everywhere and shows wonders. Then Maturidi asks: Did Jesus create bodies? This launches the dialectic.
The question 'Did he (Jesus) make bodies?' is crucial.
3
Trace the two main branches of the argument
The argument branches based on the source of Jesus's power: either a part of God or an originated power. Follow each branch to its conclusion.
Each branch leads to a contradiction or absurdity.
4
Consider the final alternative
The last option considered is that Jesus acts by himself. Maturidi dismisses it based on earlier established points.
This rounds off the argument by showing all options are untenable.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وبعد، فقولو : الله في السماء وفي الأرض، لما أظهر في كل شيء منهما عجائب، فيوجب تخصيص كل شيء من الوجه الذي يخصونه. فإن قال : قَوَّى المسيح على فعله، لا أن فعل هو به . قيل : أكان يفعل الأجسام ؟ فإن قال : نعم، قيل : أهو مخلوق ؟ فإذا قال: نعم، قيل : بدنه وروحه كبدننا وروحنا، ما باله قدر على ما لا نقدر عليه؟ وقدر على ذلك بقوة هي جزء الله أو قوة محدثة ؟ فإن قال : بجزء هو يفعل أبطل قوله في المسيح وإله المسيح ، ويكون الله لا المسيح، وإن انقطع الجزء عن الله فإذا فعل كثيرًا من الأجسام غير الله . وإن ادعوا اتصاله بالله فيكون الفعل لهما، وهما الله ، فصار إلى أن الله هو الفاعل. وإن زعموا أن فيه قوة يفعل بها الأجسام لا أنها بفعله جعلوا إله المسيح بعضه يصرفه كيف شاء. وإن قال: يفعل بنفسه لا بقوة حادثة عورض بناء على ما قررنا.
Moreover, they say: God is in heaven and on earth, because He shows wonders in everything from them, so it necessitates specifying everything in the aspect they specify. If he says: God strengthened Jesus for his action, not that the action is done by him. It is said: Did he (Jesus) make bodies? If he says: Yes, it is said: Is he created? If he says: Yes, it is said: His body and spirit are like our body and spirit, how did he have power over what we do not have power over? And he had power over that by a power that is a part of God or an originated power? If he says: By a part he acts, he invalidates his statement about the Messiah and the God of the Messiah, and it is God, not the Messiah; and if the part is separated from God, then if he does many bodies, it is not God. And if they claim his connection to God, then the action is for both, both are God, so it results that God is the agent. And if they claim that in him is a power by which he makes bodies, not that it is by His action, they make the God of the Messiah be some part of Him that directs Him as He wishes. And if he says: He acts by himself, not by an originated power, he is opposed based on what we have established.
Source pages p. 289

Commentary
This passage is a dialectical argument against the Christian claim that Jesus performed actions by God's power. The author first notes that Christians say God is everywhere and shows wonders. He then poses a series of questions: Did Jesus create bodies? If yes, is he created? If yes, then his body and spirit are like ours, so why can he do what we cannot? The power by which he acts is either a part of God or an originated power. If a part of God, then God is the actor, not Jesus, and if the part is separate, then many bodies are acts not of God. If they claim connection (hypostatic union), then both are God and God is the agent. If they claim Jesus has a power to produce bodies, that makes the God of the Messiah partially controllable. If Jesus acts by himself, that contradicts earlier points about creation. Thus the argument forces the opponent into contradictions.

Key Terms
جزء الله
قوة محدثة
اتصال
إله المسيح
الفاعل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Jesus's power to create bodies proves his divinity.
Correction: Maturidi shows that attributing creative power to Jesus leads to absurd consequences about God's nature, such as God being divisible or controllable, thus not proving divinity but undermining it.

Checkpoint
According to this argument, what is the consequence if Jesus's power is claimed to be a part of God?
A) Jesus becomes a different God
B) God remains the sole agent
C) Jesus's humanity is denied
D) The part must be originated

Key Takeaway
Maturidi's dialectic shows that any attempt to attribute creative power to Jesus, whether through a part of God or an originated power, ultimately leads to a contradiction, affirming that only God is the agent of creation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In your daily life, do you ever subconsciously attribute ultimate power to a created being—a leader, a doctor, or even yourself—as if they can act independently of Allah? How does this argument challenge you to realign your heart?
?
When you witness someone's extraordinary ability or success, do you remember that all power is from Allah alone, or do you feel a sense of awe toward the person? What would it take to shift that awe entirely to the Creator?
?
If you truly internalize that no created being can have an iota of creative power, how would it change your reliance on Allah for your provision, healing, and guidance? Commit to one specific way you will depend on Him more today.

Refuting Christ's Power from a Part of God
Maturidi refutes Christian claims about Christ's power being from God.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's logical refutation of the Christian claim that Christ's power comes from a part of God, and why each possible source of that power leads to theological inconsistency or shirk.

Core Idea
Christ's power cannot be from God's essence without creating a partner or absurdity.

Maturidi engages directly with Christian claims about Christ's miraculous abilities. He does not deny the miracles but challenges the source of that power—if it comes from God's essence, it leads to associating partners with God or to absurdities. This passage is a model of dialectical theology, using logical alternatives to force the opponent into contradiction.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once to get the flow of the argument, then read the literal translation. Notice the style of hypothetical questions and responses.
The argument proceeds by examining each possible source of Christ's power.
2
Identify the two options
The passage presents two possibilities: Christ's power is either a part of God or an originated (created) power. Locate where these are stated.
The key phrase is 'بقوة هي جزء الله أو قوة محدثة'.
3
Trace the consequences of the first option
If the power is a part of God, follow the two sub-options: the part remains attached or becomes detached. See how each leads to shirk or absurdity.
Attached leads to two gods acting; detached leads to a part of God acting independently.
4
Trace the consequences of the second option
If the power is originated (created), then Christ acts by a created power, not by God directly, which undermines the Christian claim.
This option also fails to support the special divine empowerment.
5
Reflect on the method
Notice that Maturidi does not simply assert; he uses the opponent's own premises to derive contradictions. This is a key method in kalam.
Understanding this strengthens one's ability to defend tawhid.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وبعد، فقولو : الله في السماء وفي الأرض، لما أظهر في كل شيء منهما عجائب، فيوجب تخصيص كل شيء من الوجه الذي يخصونه. فإن قال : قَوَّى المسيح على فعله، لا أن فعل هو به . قيل : أكان يفعل الأجسام ؟ فإن قال : نعم، قيل : أهو مخلوق ؟ فإذا قال: نعم، قيل : بدنه وروحه كبدننا وروحنا، ما باله قدر على ما لا نقدر عليه؟ وقدر على ذلك بقوة هي جزء الله أو قوة محدثة ؟ فإن قال : بجزء هو يفعل أبطل قوله في المسيح و إله المسيح ، ويكون الله لا المسيح، وإن انقطع الجزء عن الله فإذا فعل كثيرًا من الأجسام غير الله . وإن ادعوا اتصاله بالله فيكون الفعل لهما، وهما الله ، فصار إلى أن الله هو الفاعل. وإن زعموا أن فيه قوة يفعل بها الأجسام لا أنها بفعله جعلوا إله المسيح بعضه يصرفه " كيف شاء. وإن قال: يفعل بنفسه لا بقوة حادثة عورض بناء على ما قررنا.
And after this, they say: Allah is in heaven and on earth, because He has manifested wonders in every thing from them, so it necessitates specifying each thing from the aspect they specify. If he says: He strengthened Christ for his action, not that he (Christ) acts by himself. It is said: Did he act upon bodies? If he says: Yes, it is said: Is he created? When he says: Yes, it is said: His body and soul are like our body and soul, why is he able to do what we cannot? And he has power over that by a power that is a part of God or an originated power? If he says: By a part he acts, he invalidates his statement concerning Christ and the God of Christ, and it would be Allah, not Christ. And if the part is separated from Allah, then it would act many bodies other than Allah. And if they claim its connection with Allah, then the act would belong to both, and they are both God, so it leads to Allah being the agent. And if they claim that in him (Christ) there is a power by which he acts upon bodies, not that it is by His (God's) act, they make the God of Christ a part of Him that directs it as he wishes. And if he says: He acts by himself, not by an originated power, it is objected based on what we have established.
Source pages p. 289

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the Christian claim that Christ's miraculous actions are empowered by God in a special way. Maturidi presents a series of hypothetical objections and responses. First, he questions whether Christ acts on bodies. If yes, then Christ must be created, since his body is like ours, yet he has abilities we lack. The source of this power must be either a part of God or an originated (created) power. If it is a part of God, then either the part remains attached to God or becomes detached. If attached, then both God and Christ act, leading to two gods. If detached, then that part acts independently, which is absurd. If the power is originated, then Christ acts by a created power, not by God directly. Thus, any option leads to inconsistency. The argument proceeds in the author's own order, examining each possibility and showing its theological consequences.

Key Terms
جزء الله
قوة محدثة
فاعل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think Maturidi denies Christ's miracles entirely, or that the argument is simply about rejecting Christ's power.
Correction: Maturidi does not deny that Christ could perform miracles; rather, he refutes the Christian claim that the source of that power is a part of God's essence, showing that it leads to shirk or absurdity.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, if Christ's power to act on bodies comes from a part of God that remains attached to God, what follows?
A) Christ acts alone, not God.
B) Both God and Christ would be agents, implying two gods.
C) The part is created.
D) Christ is not a human being.

Key Takeaway
Maturidi demonstrates that ascribing divine power to Christ leads to either shirk or logical absurdity, confirming that only Allah is the true agent of all actions.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you ever catch yourself attributing the good things in your life to your own ability or to another person's help, as if they have an independent power? How does this argument force you to redirect every cause back to Allah alone?
?
When you see someone with extraordinary talent or influence, does your heart incline to admire them as if they possess something divine? What would it take for you to see them only as Allah's creation, utterly dependent on Him?
?
If you truly believed that no created being has any power except what Allah gives moment by moment, how would your fear of people and your hope in them change today? Would you still seek their approval or fear their harm?

The Flaw in the Honorific Interpretation: 'Father' and 'Brother' Analogies
Maturidi responds to the honorific claim with analogies of 'father' and 'brother'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's refutation of the claim that calling Christ 'son' is merely an honorific, using the analogies of 'father' and 'brother'.

Core Idea
Honorific titles like 'son' cannot be mere honors if they imply superiority or shared genus.

Maturidi begins by addressing the argument that 'son' is the greatest honor. He points out that 'father' is an even greater honor, but it would imply temporal precedence. He then uses the example of 'brother' to show that honorifics can be used without implying equality, yet the term 'son' across species is absurd.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once and then the translation. Notice the two distinct objections Maturidi raises.
The first objection concerns 'father', the second concerns 'brother'.
2
Identify the 'father' objection
Maturidi counters that 'O my father' is greater honor but would imply precedence, invalidating the honorific interpretation.
3
Identify the 'brother' objection and honorific sharing
Maturidi argues that 'brother' does not imply equality, and that if 'son' were an honorific, other prophets and disciples would share it.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم عارض من يقول : ليس من الإكرام أعظم من قوله : «يا بُنَيَّ». قيل: بلى، يا أبي أكبر في التعظيم ولكن لو قال: يوجب التقدم، أبطل اعتباره بالتعظيم، لأنه من ذلك الوجه لا يراد بذلك . ثم إن ثبت ذا لعل غيره ممن قد سماه به . فإن قيل: في ذلك تسوية بنفسه، قيل : قد يقول الرجل لآخر يا أخي، ولا يريد التسوية. وبعد، فإن في خلقه الكرام ، ولعل غيره سمّى به فيشركه فيه الحواريون والأنبياء. وعورض بالخلة وبالأمور أنه يجوز القول به على الإكرام. قيل: أما البنوة فلا تجوز إلا في متفق الجنس ؛ لأنه لا يجوز أن يقول للحمار والكلب يا بني، فلذلك لم يجز في الأول. وفي الجملة جهة المحبة والولاية قد يكون في غير الجنس كما يجب الحق من جهة الولاية والمحبة ونحو ذلك . مع ما يجوز أن يكون الله أخلاء وأحباب من الخلق ولا يجوز مثله في البنين . ولا قوة إلا بالله .
Then he who says: There is no greater honor than His saying: 'O my son' is objected to. It is said: Yes, 'O my father' is greater in veneration, but if he said it, it would necessitate precedence, invalidating its consideration as veneration, because from that aspect it is not intended. Then if this is established, perhaps another whom He has called by it. If it is said: In that is equalization of himself, it is said: A man may say to another 'O my brother' and not intend equalization. Moreover, indeed among His creatures are the noble ones, and perhaps another is called by it, so the disciples and prophets share in it. And he is opposed by friendship and matters that it is permissible to say it for honor. It is said: As for sonship, it is not permissible except in agreement of genus; because it is not permissible to say to a donkey or dog 'O my son', therefore it is not permissible in the first case. In summary, the aspect of love and patronage may exist across different genera, as truth is due from the aspect of patronage and love and the like. While it is permissible that Allah has close friends and beloved ones among creation, the like of that is not permissible in sons. And there is no power except with Allah.
Source pages p. 289

Commentary
This passage refutes the Christian argument that calling Christ 'son' is merely an honorific, similar to other titles. Maturidi first points out that if 'son' implies honor, 'father' would be greater, but it would imply temporal precedence. Then he argues that honorifics like 'brother' can be used without implying equality, but 'son' across species (e.g., to an animal) is absurd because sonship requires agreement of genus (جنس). Since God is not of the same genus as humans, calling Christ 'son' cannot be a mere honorific. Furthermore, if it were honorific, it would apply to other prophets and disciples. Maturidi also distinguishes between friendship (خلة) and sonship: friendship can exist between different species (e.g., God and humans), but sonship cannot. Thus, the Christian claim is refuted.

Key Terms
بنوة
الإكرام

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Christians claim that calling Jesus 'son' is a mere honorific, like calling someone 'father' or 'brother'.
Correction: Maturidi shows that 'father' implies precedence, and 'brother' does not imply equality, but 'son' across species is different.

Checkpoint
What does Maturidi say about the term 'O my father' compared to 'O my son'?
A) It is less honorable.
B) It is equally honorable.
C) It is more honorable but implies precedence.
D) It is not used in scripture.

Key Takeaway
Honorific titles must not imply temporal precedence or shared genus; the Christian claim fails both tests.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you refer to God as 'Lord' or 'Father', do you ever consider the logical implications of those titles on your understanding of His nature? How would you defend your use against someone who insists those titles imply something unbefitting of God?
?
This lesson shows that even well-intentioned honorifics must be consistent with divine transcendence. Is there a title or description you use for God that you have never examined critically? What would you do if you found it implies something you don't believe?
?
Maturidi's argument forces us to think carefully about the language we use for God. How often do you pause to question whether your words about God align with His absolute uniqueness? Will you commit to examining your religious vocabulary this week?
