The Genus Requirement and Distinction Between Sonship and Friendship
Maturidi refutes the honorific claim by genre requirement and distinguishes sonship from friendship.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's argument that sonship requires agreement of genus, and that friendship between God and humans is possible but sonship is not.

Core Idea
Sonship is impossible across different genera, so God cannot be called 'Father' of Christ literally or metaphorically as an honorific.

Maturidi continues by addressing the objection that 'son' could be an honorific based on friendship. He explains that sonship requires the same genus—one cannot call a dog 'son'—but friendship and love can exist across species. Thus, God can have friends but not sons.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage about genus requirement
Read the Arabic and translation. Note the key principle: sonship requires agreement of genus.
2
Understand the example of donkey/dog
Maturidi uses the example of calling a donkey or dog 'son' to illustrate the absurdity without same genus.
3
Distinguish sonship from friendship
Maturidi explains that friendship can cross genera, but sonship cannot. God can have friends (khulla) but not sons.

Refutation of Christian Claims on Jesus' Divinity and Sonship
Maturidi refutes Christian claims of Jesus' divinity and sonship.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Maturidi considers the Christian claims of Jesus' divinity and sonship as logically impossible and contradictory to God's transcendence.

Core Idea
Jesus' human needs and worship prove servanthood, not divinity or sonship.

Maturidi engages with Christian claims about Jesus (peace be upon him) by examining two core assertions: that Jesus is divine, and that he is the son of God. Using reason and scriptural evidence, he demonstrates why these claims cannot be reconciled with God's nature. This lesson walks through his arguments step by step.

Guided Path
1
Understand the context
Read the passage orientation and note that Maturidi is responding to Christian theology. The passage is a refutation, not a neutral discussion.
Remember that Maturidi writes within an Islamic theological framework.
2
Identify the two main arguments
The passage is structured around two aspects: refuting divinity (rububiyya) and refuting sonship (bunuwwa). As you read, locate where each argument starts.
The transition is marked by 'والثاني أن يكون ابنه'.
3
Examine the proof against divinity
Notice how Maturidi lists Jesus' human attributes—eating, drinking, worshipping—as evidence he cannot be divine. Also note the irony that Christians rejected his prophethood during his life but claimed divinity after.
4
Examine the proof against sonship
Focus on the argument that sonship requires a common genus and that God's self-sufficiency precludes birth. Note the logical consequences: a son implies need, partnership, and susceptibility to decay.
5
Reflect on the conclusion
Maturidi ends by stating that the verses do not support sonship. Consider how his argument reinforces the Islamic concept of tawhid.
This lesson ties back to the core idea: Jesus is a prophet and servant, not divine.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الوجه لا يراد بذلك. ثم إن ثبت ذا لعل غيره ممن قد سماه به. فإن قيل: في ذلك تسوية بنفسه، قيل: قد يقول الرجل لآخر يا أخي، ولا يريد التسوية. وبعد، فإن في خلقه الكرام، ولعل غيره سمى به فيشركه فيه الحواريون والأنبياء. وعورض بالخلة وبالأمور أنه يجوز القول به على الإكرام. قيل: أما البنوة فلا تجوز إلا في متفق الجنس؛ لأنه لا يجوز أن يقول للحمار والكلب يا بني، فلذلك لم يجز في الأول. وفي الجملة جهة المحبة والولاية قد يكون في غير الجنس كما يجب الحق من جهة الولاية والمحبة ونحو ذلك. مع ما يجوز أن يكون الله أخلاء وأحباب من الخلق ولا يجوز مثله في البنين. ولا قوة إلا بالله. والأصل في هذا عندنا أن الاختلاف رجع إلى وجهين. أحدهما الربوبية. والله تعالى جل ثناؤه قد بين إحالة ذلك بأكله وشربه ودفع الحاجات إلى مكان الأقذار، ووصفه بالصغر والكهولة، وعبادته الله تعالى وتضرعه له وخضوعه، ودعائه الخلق إلى عبادة الله وتوحيده، وبشارته بمحمد، وإيمانه بالرسل. ثم جعل جل ثناؤه عليه من جميع آيات الحدث وأمارات العبودة ما جعل في جميع العالم، وكذلك هو لم يدع لنفسه سوى العبودة والرسالة. فالقول له بالإلهية قول لا معنى له مع ما لو جاز ذلك لجاز لكل من البشر. والعجب أنهم لم يكونوا في حياته ومقامه في الأرض يرضون له رتبة الرسالة مع ما له من البراهين، ثم بعد رفعه أو موته عند عامتهم لم يرضوا له بالعبودة والرسالة حتى جعلوا له رتبة الربوبية، ليشهد عليهم بالخلقة والجوهر والبيان وكل شيء منه بالكذب في الابتداء والانتهاء. والثاني أن يكون ابنه، وذلك يخرج على وجوه أحدها الولادة، وذلك محال فاسد لغنى الرب عن أن تمسه الحاجة أو تغلبه الشهوة أو تعتريه الوحشة، وهن أسباب طلب الولاد. على إحالة كون الولاد من غير جوهر الوالد والله تعالى بذاته خارج عن شبه الخلق، أو عن المعنى الذي يحتمل ذلك الوجه؛ وعلى ما بيّن الله أنه لو اتخذ لهوا لما احتمل أن يتخذ مما عندنا. وبعد، فإن كل ذي ولد يحتمل الشرك وزوال ملكه إليه، ومن هو بذاته رب ملك قادر لا يحتمل ذلك. ومن يقول: «لا معنى له: أن يكون جزء من الشيء ولده، ويجب أن يكون غير كامل حتى يوجد» فهو محق. وجهة الآيات لا توجب ذلك، لأن طريق معرفة البنوة
The aspect is not intended by that. Then if this is established, perhaps someone else whom he has named with it. If it is said: In that is an equation with himself, it is said: A man may say to another 'O my brother,' and not intend equality. Moreover, among His creation are the noble, and perhaps another named with it, so that the disciples and prophets share in it. And it was objected by the (concept of) intimate friendship (khulla) and by matters that it is permissible to say it as an honor. It is said: As for sonship (bunuwwa), it is not permissible except in a (case of) agreeing genus; for it is not permissible to say to a donkey or a dog 'O my son,' therefore it was not permissible in the first. In summary, the aspect of love and allegiance may be in a different genus, just as the truth is required from the aspect of allegiance and love and the like. Along with that, God may have close friends and beloved ones from creation, but the like of that is not permissible regarding sons. And there is no power except with God. The principle in this with us is that the disagreement returns to two aspects. One of them is Lordship (rububiyya). God Almighty, exalted be His praise, has shown the impossibility of that by His eating, drinking, relieving needs to the place of filth, His description as child and old age, His worship of God Almighty, His supplication to Him, His humility, His calling creation to worship God and His oneness, His giving glad tidings of Muhammad, and His faith in the messengers. Then His Majesty placed upon him from all signs of temporality and marks of servanthood what He placed in all the world, and likewise he did not claim for himself other than servanthood and messengership. So the statement that he is divine is a meaningless statement, considering that if that were permissible, it would be permissible for every human being. And it is strange that they were not, during his life and his stay on earth, satisfied for him with the rank of messengership despite the proofs he had, then after his ascension or his death according to their common view, they were not satisfied for him with servanthood and messengership until they assigned him the rank of Lordship, so that he would testify against them by creation, essence, eloquence, and everything from him as lying from beginning to end. And the second is that he be His son, and that falls under aspects: one of them is birth (wilada), and that is impossible and corrupt due to the self-sufficiency of the Lord from need touching Him, or desire overcoming Him, or loneliness befalling Him, and these are causes for seeking offspring. (This is) based on the impossibility of birth being from other than the substance of the parent, and God Almighty in His essence is beyond resemblance to creation, or beyond the meaning that admits that aspect; and based on what God clarified that if He had taken to Himself a diversion, He would not have taken it from what we have. Moreover, every one who has a child is susceptible to partnership and the passing of his kingdom to him, while He who is in His essence the Lord, the Owner, the Powerful is not susceptible to that. And whoever says: 'There is no meaning to the statement that a part of a thing is its child, and it must be imperfect until it exists' is correct. The indication of the verses does not necessitate that, because the way of knowing sonship
Source pages p. 290

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of Christian claims about Jesus (peace be upon him). Maturidi addresses two main aspects: the claim of divinity (rububiyya) and the claim of sonship (bunuwwa). He begins by discussing a possible objection: if God called Jesus 'son' as an honorific, that would imply equality with other honored beings like angels and prophets, which is untenable. Then he argues that 'sonship' can only be used between beings of the same genus, and God is beyond genus. He moves to the first aspect: God has clearly shown the impossibility of Jesus being divine through his human needs (eating, drinking), his description as a child and an old man, his worship and supplication to God, and his calling people to monotheism and belief in Muhammad and all messengers. Jesus himself claimed only servanthood and messengership. Attributing divinity to him is meaningless, and if it were allowed, it would apply to all humans. Maturidi notes the irony that Christians did not accept Jesus as a messenger during his life, but after his ascension they elevated him to lordship. The second aspect concerns sonship. He argues that biological birth (wilada) is impossible for God because He is free from need, desire, and loneliness, which are motives for seeking offspring. Also, birth requires a parent's substance, but God is beyond resemblance. He refutes the idea that God could have taken a son from among created things. Furthermore, any being that has a child is susceptible to partnership and loss of sovereignty, but God is incapable of that. He concludes that the verses do not support sonship, as the way to know sonship (through physical attributes) does not apply to God.

Key Terms
ربوبية (rububiyya)
بنوة (bunuwwa)
ولادة (wilada)
عبودة (ubudiyya)
رسالة (risala)
إلهية (uluhiyya)
محبة (mahabbah)
ولاية (wilaya)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The term 'son of God' when applied to Jesus can be taken literally as a biological or metaphysical sonship.
Correction: Maturidi argues that literal sonship is impossible because it requires a common genus and implies need, desire, and imperfection in God, which contradict divine transcendence and self-sufficiency.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, which of the following is a reason why Jesus cannot be divine?
A) Jesus never performed miracles.
B) Jesus demonstrated human attributes like eating, worshipping, and being a child.
C) The Bible does not mention Jesus' divinity.
D) Jesus himself claimed to be God.

Key Takeaway
Jesus' human nature, his worship of God, and his own claim of servanthood conclusively refute any claims of his divinity or literal sonship.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
This passage shows that Jesus' human needs—eating, drinking, and relieving himself—prove his servanthood. If you are honest, do you live as if you are a servant of God in every mundane action, or do you sometimes act as your own lord? What specific habit can you change today to embody true ubudiyya?
?
Maturidi points out the irony that during Jesus' life, people were not satisfied with his prophethood, but after his death they elevated him to lordship. Is there any area of your life where you ignore clear signs from God because you want a more impressive or convenient belief? How will you confront that today?
?
The argument that claiming divinity for Jesus would make it permissible for every human is a powerful warning against elevating anyone to God's status. In your heart, do you ever give any person—a scholar, a leader, or even yourself—qualities that belong only to Allah? What is one step you can take to purify your tawheed?

Refutation of the Deification of Jesus
Maturidi refutes the Christian claim that Jesus is God by citing Jesus' own humility and servitude.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Maturidi argues that Jesus' own self-identification as a servant and messenger refutes the Christian claim of his divinity.

Core Idea
Jesus claimed servitude, not divinity — his own words refute deification.

Maturidi presents a powerful argument rooted in what Jesus himself said and did. Rather than attacking Christianity from outside, he points to the internal evidence of Jesus' own affirmation of tawhid and his commission as a messenger. This lesson walks through that evidence and the logical conclusion that follows.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that Maturidi uses a very direct style.
2
Identify Jesus' own claims
The passage lists three actions of Jesus: affirming tawhid, giving glad tidings of Muhammad, and believing in the messengers. These indicate his servitude.
3
Understand the argument from signs
God placed the same signs of temporality and marks of servitude on Jesus as on all creation. Jesus never claimed anything beyond servitude and messengerhood.
This shows Jesus is not divine by his own admission and by divine design.
4
Examine the Christian inconsistency
During his life, Christians were not satisfied with Jesus's messengerhood despite clear proofs. After his death or ascension, they elevated him to lordship.
Maturidi finds this contradictory.
5
Grasp the conclusion
The very elevation to divinity testifies against the Christians, because everything about Jesus—his creation, substance, clarity—shows it is false from beginning to end.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وتوحيده، وبشارته بمحمد، وإيمانه بالرسل. ثم جعل جل ثناؤه عليه من جميع آيات الحدث وأمارات العبودة ما جعل في جميع العالم، وكذلك هو لم يدع لنفسه سوى العبودة والرسالة. فالقول له بالإلهية قول لا معنى له مع ما لو جاز ذلك لجاز لكل من البشر. والعجب أنهم لم يكونوا في حياته ومقامه في الأرض يرضون له رتبة الرسالة مع ما له من البراهين، ثم بعد رفعه أو موته عند عامتهم لم يرضوا له بالعبودة والرسالة حتى جعلوا له رتبة الربوبية، ليشهد عليهم بالخلقة والجوهر والبيان وكل شيء منه بالكذب في الابتداء والانتهاء.
And his affirmation of tawhid, and his giving glad tidings of Muhammad, and his belief in the messengers. Then the Exalted and Majestic placed upon him from all signs of temporality and marks of servitude what He placed in all the world, and likewise he did not claim for himself anything but servitude and messengerhood. So the assertion of divinity for him is an assertion without meaning, since if that were permissible, it would be permissible for every human being. And astonishingly, they were not, during his life and his stay on earth, satisfied with the rank of messengerhood for him, despite the clear proofs he had; then after his ascension or his death according to the majority of them, they were not satisfied with servitude and messengerhood for him until they assigned to him the rank of lordship, so that it bears witness against them through creation, substance, clarity, and everything from him of falsehood at the beginning and at the end.
Source pages p. 291

Commentary
This passage refutes the Christian deification of Jesus by pointing to Jesus' own actions and the Christians' inconsistency. Maturidi begins by noting that Jesus himself affirmed tawhid, gave glad tidings of Muhammad, and believed in the messengers—thus embodying servitude and messengerhood, not divinity. Then he states that God placed upon Jesus all the signs of temporality and marks of servitude that are found in all creation, reinforcing that Jesus never claimed anything beyond servitude and messengerhood. Therefore, claiming divinity for Jesus is meaningless; if it were permissible for him, it would be permissible for any human. Maturidi then highlights the Christians' inconsistency: during his life, when Jesus had clear proofs, they were unwilling to even grant him the rank of a messenger; but after his death or ascension, they were not satisfied with servitude and messengerhood and elevated him to lordship. This very elevation, Maturidi concludes, bears witness against them—through creation, substance, and clarity—that everything attributed to Jesus in this regard is false from beginning to end.

Key Terms

عبودة
رسالة
إلهية
ربوبية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that performing miracles or being honored by God implies divinity.
Correction: Maturidi argues that Jesus' own self-identification as a servant and messenger, along with the clear signs of temporality placed on him, negates any claim to divinity.

Checkpoint
What is the main evidence Maturidi uses to refute the claim that Jesus is God?
A) Jesus performed many miracles.
B) Jesus himself was born of a virgin.
C) Jesus only claimed servitude and messengerhood, not divinity.
D) The Christians accepted Jesus as a prophet during his life.

Key Takeaway
Maturidi refutes the deification of Jesus by highlighting Jesus' own affirmation of tawhid and his exclusive claim to servitude and messengerhood, and by exposing the Christians' inconsistency.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Maturidi points out that the Christians elevated Jesus to lordship only after his death. When have you, in your life, held someone in higher regard than they would have wanted or claimed for themselves? How can you ensure you respect others according to their own stated boundaries?
?
The passage says that if claiming divinity for Jesus were permissible, it would be permissible for every human. Consider something you have admired or glorified in others—a leader, a scholar, or a loved one—that you have placed on a pedestal beyond what is right. Are you willing to bring that back to a proper Islamic perspective, even if it feels uncomfortable?
?
Jesus affirmed tawhid and gave glad tidings of Muhammad—two acts that clearly define his role as a servant. In your daily life, do your words and actions affirm the oneness of God as clearly as Jesus did, or do you sometimes let others define your identity in ways that contradict it?

Refuting the Claim of Jesus as Son of God
Maturidi systematically refutes Christian sonship claim through three aspects.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the multiple rational and scriptural arguments that Imam Maturidi uses to refute the Christian claim that Jesus is the son of God.

Core Idea
Sonship is impossible for God and misapplied to Jesus; Messiah and Messenger are higher titles.

Imam Maturidi tackles one of the most critical theological claims of Christianity: that Jesus is the son of God. He systematically dismantles this claim by examining the concept of sonship from several angles—physical birth, linguistic meaning, honorific usage, and metaphorical interpretation. This passage demonstrates the rigor of Islamic theological reasoning in defending divine transcendence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without stopping. Then read the translation to grasp the flow of arguments.
Note that Maturidi uses a dense, argumentative style; take your time.
2
Identify the first argument: literal birth
Find where Maturidi argues that literal birth is impossible for God due to His self-sufficiency and transcendence.
He lists three causes of seeking offspring: need, desire, and loneliness.
3
Analyze the second argument: linguistic usage
Notice Maturidi's point that the word 'son' in Arabic implies smallness and weakness, so it cannot be an honorific for Jesus.
He contrasts it with 'Messiah' and 'Messenger' as more honorable titles.
4
Examine the third argument: metaphorical interpretation
Understand how Maturidi shows that if 'son' means refuge, all creatures are sons of God, so it loses distinctiveness.
The example of calling the abyss 'mother of its inhabitants' illustrates this.
5
Reflect on the conclusion
Consider why Maturidi ends with 'there is no power except through Allah'—a reminder that correct belief comes only from divine guidance.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والثاني أن يكون ابنه، وذلك يخرج على وجوه أحدها الولادة، وذلك محال فاسد لغنى الرب عن أن تمسه الحاجة أو تغلبه الشهوة أو تعتريه الوحشة، وهن أسباب طلب الولاد. على إحالة كون الولاد من غير جوهر الوالد والله تعالى بذاته خارج عن شبه الخلق، أو عن المعنى الذي يحتمل ذلك الوجه؛ وعلى ما بيّن الله أنه لو اتخذ لهوا لما احتمل أن يتخذ مما عندنا. وبعد، فإن كل ذي ولد يحتمل الشرك وزوال ملكه إليه، ومن هو بذاته رب ملك قادر لا يحتمل ذلك. ومن يقول: لا معنى له أن يكون جزء من الشيء ولده، ويجب أن يكون غير كامل حتى يوجد فهو محق. وجهة الآيات لا توجب ذلك، لأن طريق معرفة البنوة في الشاهد ليس الآيات؛ مع ما قد شورك فيها. وبعد، هو يدعى الصدق في الخلوص له بالعبودة، فالآيات توجب ذلك لا غير. أو من جهة الفضل ينسب إلى ذلك، والأمر المعروف في الشاهد أن ذلك ليس من أسماء التعظيم، بل تسمية المسيح والرسول أجل وأعظم في ذلك. وبعد، فقد أعطيت لكثير من الخلق من الله تعالى كرامات خصوا بها، لم يوجب شيء منها اسم البنوة على أن البنوة في الكلام إنما هو من الصغار والضعاف، لا من أصحاب القوة والرفعة، وهكذا شأن أثر البنين فيكون بها إكرامه وتعظيمه بصغره؛ إذ قد يكون ذلك من العظماء في الصغار ولا قوة إلا بالله. أو أن يكون الله من حيث مفزعه وملجأه في كل أمر ونائبة، فمن ذا الوجه كل الخلق كذلك؛ وذلك كتسميته الهاوية أم أهلها. والأرض أم أهلها، فمن ذا الوجه يكون من حيث المفزع للخلق والمصمود إليه، وإن كان لا يتكلم بمثله إلا بإذن. ولا قوة إلا بالله.
And the second is that he be His son. That proceeds upon several aspects: one of them is birth, and that is impossible/invalid due to the Lord's self-sufficiency from being touched by need, or being overcome by desire, or being afflicted by loneliness, and these are the causes of seeking offspring. [This is] upon the impossibility of birth occurring without the substance of the parent, while Allah the Exalted is in His essence beyond resemblance to creation, or beyond the meaning that would admit that aspect. And according to what Allah clarified: that if He had intended to take something for play, He would not have taken it from what we have. Moreover, every possessor of offspring is likely to suffer from partnership and the loss of his kingdom to him, while He who is in His essence the Lord of a kingdom, the Powerful, is not susceptible to that. And whoever says: 'There is no meaning to his being a part of something as his child, and it must be that he is imperfect until he exists' is correct. The import of the verses does not necessitate that, because the way of knowing sonship in the witnessed realm is not the verses; despite what has been shared in them. Moreover, he is called truthful in sincerity to him through servitude, so the verses necessitate that and nothing else. Or from the aspect of excellence, one might attribute that to him, but the known practice in the witnessed realm is that this is not among the names of glorification; rather, the naming 'the Messiah' and 'the Messenger' is more sublime and greater in that regard. And moreover, many of the creation have been given by Allah the Exalted special honors with which they were distinguished, but none of those necessitated the name of sonship; indeed sonship in speech is only from smallness and weakness, not from possessors of strength and high rank; and such is the matter of the effect of sons, so that he is honored and magnified by his smallness; for indeed that may occur from the great ones regarding the small ones, and there is no power except through Allah. Or that Allah is such that He is his refuge and asylum in every matter and calamity; from this aspect all creatures are likewise; that is like naming 'the abyss' the mother of its inhabitants, and 'the earth' the mother of its inhabitants; from this aspect, He is the refuge for the creation and the one sought, even if one may not speak of the like of it except with permission. And there is no power except through Allah.
Source pages p. 291

Commentary
This passage refutes the Christian claim that Jesus is the son of God through several interconnected arguments. Maturidi first states that there are multiple aspects to the claim. The first aspect is physical birth, which is impossible for God because God is self-sufficient, free from need, desire, and loneliness—the very causes that drive creatures to seek offspring. Moreover, birth cannot occur without the substance of a parent, and God's essence is beyond resemblance to creation. Maturidi also cites the Quranic verse that if God had wanted play, He would not have taken it from what we have (implying that fatherhood is a creaturely concept). He then notes that anyone who has offspring is susceptible to partnership and loss of kingdom, which cannot apply to God. Next, he addresses a possible argument that 'son' might mean a part of something; but that would imply imperfection until the part exists, which is also impossible for God. Maturidi explains that the verses do not necessitate sonship; they only indicate servitude. The second aspect is that the title 'son' might be intended as an honorific, but in common usage, 'son' indicates smallness and weakness, not greatness; indeed, 'Messiah' and 'Messenger' are more honorable titles. The third aspect is that 'son' could be metaphorical, meaning one who takes God as a refuge, but all creatures are like that, and the term would be too broad. Maturidi provides examples from Arabic usage: 'the abyss' is called the mother of its inhabitants, and 'the earth' is called the mother of its inhabitants, both meaning refuge or origin. So the title 'son of God' for Jesus, if taken as a metaphor of refuge, would apply to all creatures and thus is not distinctive. He concludes that one should not speak of such matters without divine permission.

Key Terms
بنوة
ولادة
عبودة
رسالة
مفزع
ملجأ

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That 'son of God' in scripture implies a literal family relationship between God and Jesus.
Correction: Maturidi shows that literal sonship is impossible for God; the term can only be understood metaphorically (as refuge) or incorrectly as an honorific, but neither applies exclusively to Jesus.

Checkpoint
Which of the following is NOT one of the arguments Maturidi uses to refute Jesus being the son of God?
A) God is self-sufficient and does not need offspring.
B) The title 'son' in Arabic implies smallness and weakness.
C) Jesus himself explicitly claimed to be the son of God.
D) The term 'son of God' could be metaphorical, like calling the earth 'mother'.

Key Takeaway
Maturidi's refutation of Jesus being the son of God is based on the impossibility of literal birth, the linguistic inappropriateness of 'son' as an honorific, and the lack of distinctiveness in a metaphorical interpretation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read Maturidi's argument that 'son' implies smallness and weakness, do you ever think of God in terms that diminish His majesty? How do you correct that in your worship?
?
Maturidi says that if 'son of God' means taking refuge, then all creation are sons. Are you truly taking God as your sole refuge, or do you still rely on other things? What would change if you lived as if God alone is your refuge?
?
The passage shows that the titles 'Messiah' and 'Messenger' are greater than 'son'. Do you assign higher status to worldly titles than to the titles God has given? How can you align your honor system with Allah's?

Refutation of Dualists and Mu'tazila on Benefit and Harm
Maturidi refutes dualists and Mu'tazila on wisdom in creation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam Maturidi refutes the dualists and Mu'tazila by showing that every harmful thing can have a beneficial aspect, whether direct or indirect.

Core Idea
No harm exists without some form of benefit.

In this passage, Imam Maturidi continues to argue for the wisdom in all creation, including things that appear harmful. He refutes two opposing groups: the dualists who believed in two principles of good and evil, and the Mu'tazila who claimed that any action not directly benefiting another is not wise. As you read, notice how Maturidi uses the principle that circumstances change, and that benefits can be indirect, such as through lessons or reminders.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once to get the rhythm, then read the literal translation. Observe the structure: Maturidi builds on a previous argument and then refutes two groups.
Notice the phrase 'وإذ ثبت ذا' which links back to prior reasoning.
2
Identify the two groups refuted
Identify the dualists (الثنوية) and the Mu'tazila (المعتزلة). Understand their claims: dualists posit two principles, Mu'tazila say actions must directly benefit another to be wise.
Dualists believed in light and darkness as separate creators.
3
Analyze the types of benefits listed
Maturidi lists several ways harm can benefit: through indication (دلالة), admonition (موعظة), reminding of blessings, warning of punishment, and recognizing Allah's sovereignty. List them from the text.
These benefits are spiritual or educational, not always material.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإذ ثبت ذا بطل قول الثنوية بالاثنين وثبت جهلهم بوجوه الحكمة في خلق الضار والنافع، إذ قد يجوز أن يصير كل ضار في حال نافعا في أخرى. وبطل قول من يقول من المعتزلة: إن كل فعل لا ينفع آخر فهو غير حكمة. مع ما لا يوجد ضرر ألبتة إلا وأمكن أن ينتفع به أحد، إما من طريق الدلالة، أو من طريق الموعظة، أو ما فيه من تذكير النعمة وتحذير النقمة، ومن تعريف من له الخلق والأمر في الخلق، وغير ذلك مما يكثر ذكره. ولا قوة إلا بالله .
And when this is established, the statement of the dualists regarding the two [principles] is invalid, and their ignorance of the aspects of wisdom in the creation of harm and benefit is established, for it is possible that every harmful thing becomes beneficial in another situation. And the statement of those among the Mu'tazila who say: 'Every action that does not benefit another is not wisdom' is invalid. Moreover, no harm exists at all except that it is possible for someone to benefit from it, either by way of indication, or by way of admonition, or what is in it of reminding of blessings and warning of punishment, and of defining the One to Whom creation and command belong in the creation, and other aspects whose mention is extensive. There is no power except with Allah.
Source pages p. 296

Commentary
This passage refutes two groups: the dualists (al-thunawiyya) and the Mu'tazila. Maturidi builds on the previous argument that one action can be wise or foolish depending on circumstances. Now he applies it to the creation of harmful and beneficial things. The dualists posited two principles (light and darkness) to account for good and evil. Maturidi argues that their claim is invalid because they are ignorant of the aspects of wisdom in creating both harmful and beneficial things. A harmful thing can become beneficial in another situation, so no absolute dichotomy is needed. He then refutes a Mu'tazili claim that any action that does not benefit someone else is not wise. Maturidi counters by stating that there is no harm from which someone cannot derive benefit, whether through indication (dalala), admonition (maw'iza), reminding of blessings, warning of punishment, or by knowing the One to whom creation and command belong. Thus, all harm has some benefit, so the Mu'tazili objection is invalid.

Key Terms
الثنوية (al-thunawiyya)
الضار والنافع (al-darr wa al-nafi')
الدلالة (al-dalala)
الموعظة (al-maw'iza)
المعتزلة (al-Mu'tazila)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Harmful things have no benefit at all.
Correction: Maturidi shows that every harm can benefit through indication, admonition, reminding of blessings, warning of punishment, and recognizing Allah's sovereignty.

Checkpoint
What does Maturidi say about the existence of harm in the world?
A) Harm is always evil and purposeless
B) Harm cannot have any benefit
C) Every harm can potentially benefit someone in some way
D) Harm only benefits the dualists

Key Takeaway
Maturidi refutes dualists and Mu'tazila by showing that all harm contains potential benefit, which is a sign of divine wisdom.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of a past trial you considered 'pointless' or 'unjust.' How might this passage challenge you to see it differently? What would it mean for your relationship with Allah if you genuinely believed every harm contained hidden benefit? Are you willing to look for that benefit?
?
The dualists invented a second principle to explain evil. Have you ever, even implicitly, attributed a difficulty to 'bad luck' or 'the system' rather than to Allah's wisdom? What would change in your heart if you completely rejected that notion and affirmed that all harm is from Allah with purpose?
?
The Mu'tazila demanded immediate, tangible benefit from every act. Examine your own worship: do you feel restless when you don't 'feel' spiritual benefit? How does this passage call you to trust that even unseen, indirect benefits are real and sufficient in Allah's plan?

Disagreement Among Believers is Not from the Quran
Maturidi proves that disagreement among believers is not from the Quran.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's argument that sectarian disagreement among Muslims does not originate from the Quran but from human interpretation and the confusion between clear and ambiguous verses.

Core Idea
The Quran is free from contradiction, so disagreement must come from elsewhere.

In this passage, Imam al-Maturidi addresses a pressing theological and communal problem: why do Muslims who all affirm the Quran as truth still fall into sectarian disagreement? He begins by noting that believers acknowledged the Quran as truth and that salvation lies in adhering to it. Yet each faction assumed it had grasped the clear (muhkam) verses and interpreted the ambiguous (mutashabih) according to its own preconceptions, leading to conflict. Maturidi then invokes the Quran's own claim to be free from contradiction and argues logically that contradiction in proof indicates error. His conclusion is that the source of disagreement is not the Quran itself but human error in interpretation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage carefully. Then read the translation. Notice how Maturidi sets up the problem: people acknowledged the Quran as truth but fell into disagreement.
2
Identify the argument's structure
Maturidi presents a logical argument. Identify the premise (Quran is without contradiction), the observation (people disagreed), and the conclusion.
3
Reflect on the implication
Think about what this means for sectarianism. Does Maturidi blame the Quran or human interpretation? How does his argument encourage unity?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولما جمع جل ثناؤه كتابه على الأمرين تعرف الناس الذين أقروا بالكتاب أنه حق من العدول عنه، وأن من لزمه أفلح ونجا، ومن مال عنه شقى وخسر. حتى ظن يسع كل فريق أنه قد أصاب المحكم من ذلك ولزمه، وأن عليه فيما ذهب إليه خصومه أن يقف في ذلك أو يحمله على ما تقرر عنده فيما اعتقده فألزم تفرقهم في الحاجة كلا تعرف المحكم من المتشابه ولزوم العلم بالمتشابه لأن لا يناقض المحكم منه. ثم معلوم أنه لا يحتمل القرآن الاختلاف وبه وصف الله أنه ولو كان من عند غير الله لوجدوا فيه اختلافا كثيرا. وفي العقل أن تناقض الأدلة دليل سفهه وجهله؛ فثبت بذلك أن الذي له تفرقوا ليس من حيث القرآن، ولا لما ليس فيه بيان؛ بل دل تكليف الرد إلى القرآن ولزوم اتباعه على أن فيه بيان ذلك.
And when the Exalted One compiled His Book upon the two matters, the people who acknowledged the Book knew that it is truth from turning away from it, and that whoever adheres to it succeeds and is saved, and whoever deviates from it is wretched and loses. So much so that each faction assumed that it had attained the muhkam from it and adhered to it, and that it is upon it regarding what its opponents went to, to stop or to interpret it according to what was established with it in its belief, so their disagreement in need compelled each one to know the muhkam from the mutashabih and the necessity of knowledge of the mutashabih so that it does not contradict the muhkam from it. Then it is known that the Quran does not admit contradiction, and by it God described that 'if it had been from other than God, they would have found in it much discrepancy.' And in the intellect, contradiction of proofs is a proof of its foolishness and ignorance. So it is established by that that what they disagreed about is not from the Quran, nor due to lack of clarity in it; rather, the obligation to refer to the Quran and adherence to it indicates that there is clarification for that.
Source pages p. 300

Commentary
This passage addresses the problem of sectarian disagreement among Muslims. Maturidi explains that despite everyone affirming the Quran as truth, each group claimed to have grasped the clear (muhkam) parts and interpreted the ambiguous (mutashabih) according to its preconceptions. This led to disagreement. However, the Quran itself is free from contradiction, as the verse from Surah an-Nisa (4:82) states. Therefore, the cause of disagreement is not the Quran but other factors. The obligation to refer to the Quran proves that it contains the clarification needed.

Key Terms
محكم
متشابه
اختلاف
القرآن

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that the Quran's ambiguous verses (mutashabih) are the cause of sectarian division.
Correction: Maturidi argues that the Quran itself is free from contradiction and contains clarity; disagreement arises from human interpretation and prejudice.

Checkpoint
What does Maturidi conclude about the source of disagreement among those who accept the Quran?
A) The Quran is contradictory.
B) The Quran is unclear.
C) The disagreement is not from the Quran.
D) Only scholars can understand.

Key Takeaway
Sectarian disagreement stems from human interpretation, not from the Quran itself, which is free from contradiction and provides sufficient guidance.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you encounter a disagreement about a religious matter, do you immediately assume that your understanding is the muhkam and that others should interpret according to your belief? How can you humbly check yourself against the clear verses first?
?
Do you ever use the existence of mutashabih verses as an excuse to avoid acting on clear commands of the Quran? What would change if you consistently interpreted ambiguity through the muhkam?
?
If the Quran is free from contradiction, then every division among Muslims—including those you participate in—is not from Allah but from your own limitations. Will you now seek to reconcile rather than argue, starting with the next disagreement you face?

Six Causes of Mistaking Clear for Ambiguous
Maturidi enumerates six causes for mistaking muhkam for mutashabih.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the six reasons Maturidi identifies as obstacles to recognizing the clear verses of the Quran and recognizing divine oneness.

Core Idea
Confusion about clear truths often stems from inclination, habit, imitation, or overconfidence—not just ignorance.

Maturidi, after establishing the distinction between muhkam and mutashabih, now turns to why people mistakenly take the clear as ambiguous. He identifies six causes that cloud human judgment, ranging from natural inclinations to intellectual arrogance. This passage reveals that the problem is not only rational but also psychological and social.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic text once to get the rhythm. Then read the translation carefully.
Notice the six reasons are introduced with 'إما' (either) for each.
2
Identify each cause
Go through each of the six causes. Label them with a short description (e.g., inclination, habit, imitation, etc.).
3
Understand the common thread
Ask yourself: what do all these causes have in common? They all involve a failure of submission or proper intellectual effort.
This connects to the learning objective about obstacles to clear understanding.
4
Reflect on the concluding sentence
Maturidi says these are the 'aspects of doubt' that lead away from tawheed. Consider how each cause undermines the universal testimony of creation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإنما خفي المحكم على من لم يبلغه لمعان: (أ) إما لميل طبيعة الجوهر إلى ما يتلذذ به؛ (ب) أو لإلف بعض ما اعتاده؛ (ج) أو لتقليد من وثق به؛ (د) أو لتقصير في الطلب؛ (هـ) أو لثقة منه بعقله أحب أن يسوس عليه حكمة الربوبية دون أن يتبع عقله ما ألقى في سمعه فصار به المحكم عنده متشابها؛ (و) أو لتقصير في البحث. إذ هذه الوجوه هي وجوه الشبهة على الذين عدلوا عن التوحيد على شهادة كلية الأشياء له بذلك. ولا قوة إلا بالله.
And the muhkam is hidden from those who have not attained it due to reasons: (a) either due to the inclination of the nature of the substance to what it delights in; (b) or due to familiarity with some of what he is accustomed to; (c) or due to blind imitation of one he trusts; (d) or due to deficiency in seeking; (e) or due to confidence in his own intellect, preferring to manage the wisdom of lordship over it rather than following his intellect to what has been cast into his hearing, so the muhkam becomes ambiguous to him; (f) or due to deficiency in research. For these are the aspects of doubt upon those who deviated from tawheed, despite the universal testimony of all things to it. And there is no power except through God.
Source pages p. 301

Commentary
This passage lists six psychological and epistemological barriers that prevent people from recognizing the clear (muhkam) verses of the Quran. They are: (1) natural inclination towards pleasure, (2) habit and custom, (3) blind following of authority, (4) lack of effort in seeking knowledge, (5) overconfidence in one's own reasoning that refuses to submit to divine wisdom, and (6) insufficient research. These are called 'aspects of doubt' that lead people away from tawheed, even though all creation testifies to it.

Key Terms
محكم
متشابه
شبهة
تقليد
طبيعة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: People mistakenly think that confusion about clear verses is purely due to lack of knowledge.
Correction: Maturidi shows that psychological factors like inclination to pleasure, habit, and overconfidence are also major obstacles.

Checkpoint
Which of the following is NOT one of the six reasons Maturidi gives for why the clear appears ambiguous?
A) Inclination to pleasure.
B) Lack of research.
C) Divine predestination.
D) Blind imitation.

Key Takeaway
The six causes reveal that the path to clear understanding requires both intellectual effort and humility before revelation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Which of these six causes is most active in your life right now? Is there a desire, a habit, or an authority figure that might be clouding your understanding of a clear truth about Allah? What would it cost you to let that go?
?
Have you ever been overconfident in your own intellect when reading a verse, thinking you know better than what it plainly says? How would you begin to humble your mind before revelation today?
?
When you find yourself doubting a clear teaching of tawheed, do you honestly examine which of these causes is at work in your heart? If you discovered it was blind imitation or deficiency in seeking, would you have the courage to change course?

The Necessity of Signs and Refutation of Mu'tazila
Maturidi uses Quran 23:91 and a rational principle about signs to refute Mu'tazila.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's rational argument that every created accident has a sign of its creation, and how this refutes the Mu'tazila position on human actions.

Core Idea
God marks every created thing with a sign — even unseen substances produce visible effects.

This passage continues Maturidi's argument against those who deny that all actions are created by God. He quotes a Quranic verse about the impossibility of multiple gods and then establishes a universal principle: every accident God creates is accompanied by a sign that indicates its createdness. He then applies this principle to refute the Mu'tazila, showing their inconsistency.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Begin by reading the Arabic passage once, then the English translation. Notice the flow from the Quranic verse to the rational principle to the conclusion.
The passage is dense, so take your time.
2
Identify the universal principle
Look for the statement: 'God never created an accident except that He placed upon it a proof by which it is known that it was created.' This is the key rational claim.
This principle applies to all accidents, not just those we can see.
3
Consider the case of unseen substances
Maturidi points out that even substances we don't see can produce observable changes. This shows that the sign of creation is not limited to visible things.
4
Understand the refutation of Mu'tazila
If the Mu'tazila claim that human actions are not created by God, then those actions lack the divine sign. This makes their position similar to atheists who deny God's creative role entirely.
The closing prayer underscores the severity of this view.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأيضا إن الله تعالى قال : ما اتخذ الله من ولد وما كان معه من إله ، ثم قال : إذا لذهب كل إله بما خلق ﴾ [المؤمنون ، ۹۱/۲۳] . ثم الله جل ثناؤه لم يخلق عرضاً قط إلا جعل عليه دليلاً يعلم أنه خُلق، لما كانت الأعراض كما ذكرنا . ويجوز أن يكون في خلقه خلق يُجمع ويفرّق ويحرك ويسكن ونحن لا نراه، كما كان فيهم من لا نراه بجوهره وإن كان يُرى. وتلك الأفعال لأنفسها لا تُرى، إنما تُرى وتعلم بتغير الأحوال على الجوهر . فإذ كانت جواهر لا تُرى، جائز منها مثلها لم يجعلـــه ] لما خلق عَلَمًا ولا ذهب به. فكيف ناقض به قولُ المعتزلة قول الملحدة وهم / شركاؤهم في هذا الوجه. فنسأل الله النجاة من قول هذا عقباه.
And also, God Most High said: 'God has not taken a son, nor has there been with Him any god' then He said: 'Then each god would have taken away what it created' [Al-Mu'minun 23:91]. Then God, exalted be His praise, never created an accident ('arad) except that He placed upon it a proof (dalil) by which it is known that it was created, since accidents are as we mentioned. And it is possible that among His creation there is a creation that gathers and separates, moves and is still, and we do not see it, just as among them there is that whose essence we do not see even though it is seen. And those acts in themselves are not seen; they are only seen and known by the change of conditions (ahwal) upon the substance (jawhar). So since there are substances that are not seen, it is possible from them similar to what He did not make as a sign for what He created, nor did He go with it. So how does the saying of the Mu'tazila contradict the saying of the atheists, and they are their partners in this aspect. So we ask God for salvation from the consequence of this saying.
Source pages p. 311

Commentary
This passage presents a second argument, beginning with a Quranic verse (23:91) that negates the existence of multiple gods. Maturidi asserts that God never creates an accident without placing a sign upon it, so that its createdness is known. He then considers the possibility of unseen substances that nonetheless produce observable changes. The point is that actions themselves are not directly seen; only their effects on bodies are observed. If, as the Mu'tazila claim, human actions are not created by God, then those actions lack the divine sign that all other created things have. This inconsistency aligns the Mu'tazila with atheists who deny divine creation. The passage ends with a prayer for salvation from such a view.

ملحدة (atheists)

Misconception: The argument is purely scriptural without rational basis.
Correction: While starting with a Quranic verse, Maturidi then provides a rational principle that every created accident has a sign, and applies it to unseen substances and to refuting the Mu'tazila.

Checkpoint

A) A name
B) A proof (dalil)
C) A body
D) A reward

Key Takeaway
Maturidi argues that God's creation always includes a sign, and those who deny divine creation of human actions break this pattern, contradicting both reason and scripture.

When you perform an action today, will you pause to see the sign of God's creation in it, or will you claim it as your own creation like the Mu'tazila?

?
Maturidi ends with a prayer for salvation from attributing actions to oneself. Are you truly asking Allah to save you from this? What would that look like in practice tomorrow?

Maturidi's Argument by Analogy with Dualists
Maturidi compares Mu'tazila denial of God creating evil acts to dualist errors.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Maturidi uses the analogy of dualists (Majus) to argue that denying God creates evil human actions leads to similar inconsistencies.

Core Idea
Denying God creates moral evil leads to the same error as positing a second deity.

Maturidi presents a sharp argument: those who hesitate to attribute evil actions to God fall into the same inconsistency as dualists who posit two creators to avoid attributing evil to the good God. By comparing the two positions, he shows that the Mu'tazila's objection about moral ugliness applies equally to physical evils, which everyone accepts God creates. This passage invites us to see the logical consequences of denying God's creative power over all actions.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Notice the structure: first a statement about dualists, then a comparison to the Mu'tazila position. Then read the English translation.
The passage ends abruptly; focus on the argument presented.
2
Identify the dualist error according to Maturidi
Maturidi says dualists agree with monotheists about God's attributes but then go astray by ascribing creation of the world to two principles. He argues this is more blameworthy than simply exonerating God from evil.
Notice that Maturidi does not fully refute dualism here; he uses it as a comparison.
3
Understand the analogy with the Mu'tazila
Maturidi then applies the same reasoning to those who deny God's creation of evil human acts because those acts contain obscenities. He points out that the same objection can be raised about physical substances (filth, stinks). If dualists can accept that God creates those, then the Mu'tazila should accept that God creates evil acts.
The key is consistency: if we allow God to create physical ugliness, we cannot reject moral ugliness.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم الأصل أن مذهب الثنوية والمجوس في صرف خلق العالم إلى اثنين، وأنهم يوافقون أهل التوحيد على أن الإله الحكيم الحق الذي لم يجر ولا يجور واحد عليم قدير، فمن أربي عليهم حتى جعل خلق العالم لمن لا يُحصى عددهم وأبطل أن يكون للإله الذي قال الخلق بألوهيته قدرة خلق أكثر العالم [ فهو ] أحق بالذم ممن نزهوه عن الشرور والقبائح. ولا قوة إلا بالله . ومما يقولون في فعل العباد مما فيه قبح الإضافة إلى الله تعالى في خلق ذلك من أن فيها فواحش ومناكير ونحو ذلك، فيه مثل ذلك للثنوية والمجوس في الجواهر: أن فيها قبائح وخبائث وأقذاراً وأنتانا. ومع ما إضافة تلك الأشياء إلى الله فليست هي ـ عند التفسير بأن الله تعالى خلقها قبائح [ و ] فواحش من مرتكبيها، مخالفة للمحاسن والمصالح من أفعالهم ـ بأقبح
Then the principle is that the sect of the dualists and the Magians in diverting the creation of the world to two [principles]—and they agree with the people of tawhid that the true wise God who has never wronged nor wrongs is one, knowing, powerful—so who is more insolent against them so that he made the creation of the world belong to countless [beings] and nullified that the God who claimed creation by His divinity has power to create the greater part of the world? [ Such a one ] is more deserving of blame than those who exonerated Him from evils and ugly things. And there is no power but in God. And among what they say concerning the acts of servants, regarding the ugliness of attributing to God Almighty the creation of that, because it contains obscenities, abominations, and the like—there is the like of that for the dualists and Magians concerning substances: that they contain ugly things, foul things, filths, and stinks. And even if the attribution of those things to God is not—according to the interpretation that God Almighty created them as ugly and obscene from their perpetrators, contradicting the good and beneficial aspects of their acts—uglier?
Source pages p. 315

Commentary
This passage is a third argument, comparing the Mu'tazila position to that of dualists (Majus) who attribute the creation of evil to a second principle. Maturidi notes that the dualists agree with monotheists that God is wise, just, one, knowing, and powerful. The dualists, however, ascribe the creation of the world to two gods to avoid attributing evil to the good God. Similarly, the Mu'tazila hesitate to attribute evil human acts to God, claiming the acts are created by humans. Maturidi argues that the dualists' error is even more severe because they assign creation to countless beings and deny that the one God has power to create most of the world. Such a stance is more blameworthy than simply exonerating God from evil. Then he addresses a common Mu'tazila objection: that attributing evil acts to God is ugly because they contain obscenities and abominations. Maturidi retorts that the same objection can be raised against God regarding physical substances (like filth and stinks). If the dualists can accept that God creates such substances despite their apparent ugliness—explaining that they are created as ugly from their perpetrators—then the same reasoning applies to human acts: God creates them as ugly from the perpetrators. Thus, the Mu'tazila objection fails.

Key Terms
ثنوية
مجوس
إله
شرور
قبائح
فواحش

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think refuting dualism only applies to polytheists; Maturidi shows that Mu'tazila fall into a similar error by denying God's creation of evil actions.
Correction: The argument shows that anyone who tries to exempt God from creating evil—whether by positing a second deity or by denying His creation of human acts—falls into inconsistency. The Mu'tazila are not polytheists but share the same logical flaw.

Checkpoint
How does Maturidi use the example of dualists (Majus) to argue against the Mu'tazila?
A) The dualists agree with Mu'tazila on human free will.
B) The dualists also deny that God creates evil human acts.
C) The dualists attribute physical evil to God, so Mu'tazila cannot object to God creating moral evil.
D) The dualists believe in two gods, so Mu'tazila are better than them.

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazila objection that God cannot create evil acts is refuted because the same reasoning would force one to deny God creates physical evils, which is absurd.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face a situation in your life that seems unjust or evil, do you instinctively distance God from that event, thinking He could not be involved? How does Maturidi's argument challenge your tendency to protect God's reputation at the expense of His sovereignty? What would it cost you to believe that God is truly in control of everything, even the ugly parts of your story?
?
Imagine someone tells you that your sin is too great for God to have caused it—that it was entirely your own doing. Based on this lesson, would you accept that comfort? Why or why not? How does Maturidi's exposure of the dualist error in your own thinking pressure you to repent not just of your sins, but of your desire to keep God safe from them?
?
If you honestly examine your prayers and petitions, do you ever find yourself asking God to intervene in one area of your life while assuming He is absent from another? How does the analogy with the dualists reveal that you might be functionally believing in two realms—one where God reigns and one where He does not? What concrete step can you take today to surrender that second realm back to Him?

Maturidi's Comparison: Mu'tazila Worse Than Dualists
Maturidi compares Mu'tazila to dualists, showing greater blame.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Imam Maturidi considers the Mu'tazila position on evil to be more blameworthy than that of the dualists.

Core Idea
Mu'tazila limit God's power; dualists at least honor God by exonerating Him from evil.

Imam Maturidi presents a striking polemical comparison: the Mu'tazila, who claim to uphold God's justice, end up denying His power to create most acts, attributing them to human servants. In contrast, the dualists (such as Zoroastrians) at least exonerated God from evil by assigning it to a separate principle. As you read this passage, notice how Maturidi turns the tables on the Mu'tazila by showing their position is logically worse than an obviously erroneous sect.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the literal translation, to get the flow of Maturidi's argument.
The text has some textual uncertainties, but the overall meaning is clear.
2
Identify the key comparison
Notice that Maturidi first states the dualists' agreement with People of Tawhid on God's attributes, then contrasts how the Mu'tazila have multiplied creators and limited God's power.
3
Reflect on the blameworthiness
Ask yourself: Why does Maturidi say the Mu'tazila are more deserving of blame? The commentary will help unpack this.
The dualists at least exonerated God from evil; Mu'tazila directly limit His power.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم الأصل أن مذهب الثنوية والمجوس في صرف خلق العالم إلى اثنين، وأنهم يوافقون أهل التوحيد على أن الإله الحكيم الحق الذي لم يجر ولا يجور واحد عليم قدير، فمن أربي عليهم حتى جعل خلق العالم لمن لا يحصى عددهم وأبطل أن يكون للإله الذي قال الخلق بألوهيته قدرة خلق أكثر العالم فهو أحق بالذم ممن نزهوه عن الشرور والقبائح. ولا قوة إلا بالله.
Then the principle is that the doctrine of the dualists (al-thānawiyya) and the Magians (al-majūs) concerning assigning the creation of the world to two [principles], and that they agree with the people of tawḥīd that the wise, true God who does not deviate and is not unjust is one, knowing, powerful. Then who surpasses them until they made the creation of the world to those whose number cannot be counted, and nullified that the God whose creation they affirm by His divinity has the power to create most of the world — he is more deserving of blame than those who exonerated Him from evils and abominations.
Source pages p. 315

Commentary
This passage is a comparison between the Muʿtazila and the dualists (like Zoroastrians). Maturidi states that the dualists and Magians, despite their error of positing two creators, still agree with the people of tawḥīd that the true God is one, wise, never does wrong or injustice, knowing and powerful. Then he argues that the Muʿtazila are worse: they attribute creation of most of the world to countless beings (human servants) and deny that the God they claim to worship has power to create most of the world. Thus, the Muʿtazila are more deserving of blame than the dualists, who at least exonerated God from evil. The dualists assign evil to a separate principle, but the Muʿtazila limit God's power directly.

Correction: He uses dualists as a foil to show the Mu'tazila are even worse.

B) Because Mu'tazila deny God's power to create most acts and attribute it to many servants
C) Because Mu'tazila affirm God's power completely
D) Because dualists reject prophethood

Key Takeaway
Maturidi argues that the Mu'tazila, by limiting God's power to create, are more blameworthy than dualists who at least exonerate God from evil.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In your daily life, when you succeed, do you attribute it primarily to your own effort or to Allah's power? Maturidi warns that attributing creation to many beings (like your own actions) limits God's power. Ask yourself: On a scale from 1 to 10, how much do you genuinely believe that every breath you take is created directly by Allah? What one thing can you do today to increase that belief?
?
Think of a recent difficulty or evil you experienced. Did you unconsciously try to 'exonerate' Allah from it by blaming someone or something else? How does Maturidi's point that even the dualists exonerated God from evil challenge you to instead accept that both good and evil come from Allah? Write down one specific event and a sentence of re-affirmation that Allah's power is over all of it.
?
The Mu'tazila claimed to honor God by denying He creates evil, but Maturidi says they actually dishonor Him by limiting His power. Have you ever, in trying to defend God's justice, fallen into a similar trap—subtly limiting what God can do to make Him 'fit' your sense of fairness? Repent now and declare: 'I affirm that Allah is powerful over everything, including what I cannot understand.'

The Aspect Contradiction: Al-Ka'bi on Pain and Free Will
Maturidi reports al-Ka'bi's view that free agents are compelled in pain and refutes him.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how al-Ka'bi's claim that free agents are compelled in pain leads to a contradiction regarding aspects (jihaat) and how Maturidi refutes it.

Core Idea
One action cannot be both compelled and free without specifying aspects.

Al-Ka'bi, a Mu'tazili theologian, attempted to reconcile divine compulsion and human free will by claiming that while humans are free in their actions, they are compelled in the resulting pain. Maturidi exposes the inconsistency in this view by showing that al-Ka'bi denied the same principle of aspects when applied to divine creation and human acquisition. This passage is a concise example of the kalam method of exposing internal contradictions.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without attempting analysis. Then read the English translation to grasp the flow of the argument.
Notice the compact style of Maturidi's critique.
2
Identify al-Ka'bi's claim
Look for what al-Ka'bi said: every free agent is compelled in the pain and harm resulting from his action.
He is trying to combine free will (mukhtar) and compulsion (mudtar) in one act.
3
Spot the inconsistency
Al-Ka'bi allowed two aspects (jihaat) for one thing in the case of pain but denied it for the same action being both created by God and acquired by the servant. Find where Maturidi points this out.
The key phrase: 'فمثله في الخلق والتعذيب' – similarly in creation and punishment.
4
Understand Maturidi's conclusion
Maturidi concludes that since command and prohibition are established (implying aspects), al-Ka'bi's error in his estimation (taqdir) becomes clear, and the same applies to promise and threat.
The command/obligation structure requires that actions have multiple aspects.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقد قال الكعبي : إن كل مختار في فعله مضطر في تألّمه به، وتأذيه به. فألزمه الأمرين في الشيء الواحد. وكذلك زعم أن قد يجوز أن يعرف الفعل من لا يعرفه كفرًا أو إيمانًا، أو شيئًا عرضاً وحركة وسكونًا، وهو ذلك بعينه. ولم يجز في الجملة أن يقال : الفعل الذي يجهله هو الذي يعلمه، والذي هو مضطر فيه هو الذي هو مختار فيه حتى يُذكر معه الجهات، فمثله في الخلق والتعذيب، وغير ذلك. ولا قوة إلا بالله . واحتج في الوعد والوعيد بذلك. وإذ ثبت الأمر والنهي، بان إغفاله في تقديره، وظهر تمويهه، فكذلك شأن الوعد والوعيد.
And al-Ka'bi said: Every free agent in his action is compelled in his being affected by it and being harmed by it. So he forced the two matters on one thing. And similarly he claimed that it is permissible that an action be known from one who does not know it as disbelief or faith, or something accidental (arad) and motion (haraka) and stillness (sukun), while it is the very same thing. But he did not allow, in general, to say: The action that he is ignorant of is the one he knows, and that which he is compelled in is the one he is free in, unless the aspects (jihaat) are mentioned with it. Similarly in creation (khalq) and punishment (ta'dhib) and other than that. And there is no power except with Allah. And he argued by that concerning promise (wa'd) and threat (wa'id). And when command and prohibition are established, his negligence in estimation became clear, and his falsification appeared, so similarly is the matter of promise and threat.
Source pages p. 320

Commentary
This passage presents al-Ka'bi's view and Maturidi's response. Al-Ka'bi claimed that every free agent is compelled in the pain and harm resulting from his own action, thus combining both free choice and compulsion in one action. He also claimed that an action can be known from someone who does not know it as disbelief or faith, etc., meaning that the same action can be known under one description and unknown under another. However, he did not allow saying that the same action is both known and unknown, or both compelled and chosen, without specifying aspects. Maturidi points out the inconsistency: al-Ka'bi allows two aspects for the same thing in some cases (like pain) but denies it in the matter of creation and acquisition (khalq and kasb). Maturidi then says that al-Ka'bi used this argument in the discussion of divine promise and threat, but since command and prohibition are established, al-Ka'bi's neglect in estimation is exposed.

Key Terms
الكعبي
مختار
مضطر
تألم
تأذي
جهات
الوعد والوعيد
الأمر والنهي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What inconsistency did Maturidi point out in al-Ka'bi's position?
A) He allowed aspects for one thing but denied them for creation and choice
B) He denied all aspects
C) He claimed actions are created by humans
D) He denied God's creation of actions

Key Takeaway
Maturidi exposes al-Ka'bi's contradiction: he applies aspects to pain and harm but denies them in the case of divine creation and human acquisition, undermining his own argument.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you experience pain or hardship from a decision you made, do you ever secretly feel God is unjust, forgetting the aspects that preserve both His control and your responsibility? How can you train yourself to see these aspects in your daily trials?
?
Al-Ka'bi held a contradiction without realizing it. What belief do you hold that might be internally inconsistent if you examined it honestly? Will you ask Allah tonight to reveal such blind spots in your own theology?
?
Maturidi uses logic to defend divine justice. Have you ever avoided deep reflection on difficult theological issues because they make you uncomfortable? Will you commit today to studying one such issue with an open heart, trusting that truth will strengthen your faith?

Maturidi's Refutation of al-Ka'bi on Dual Agency
Maturidi refutes al-Ka'bi's argument that dual agency necessitates partnership in action.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Maturidi argues that God's creation of human actions does not entail partnership with humans, and how he refutes al-Ka'bi's claim using the inheritance example and the lack of empirical evidence.

Core Idea
Dual agency does not imply partnership because the aspects differ and no example proves it.

In this passage, Imam al-Maturidi responds to the Mu'tazilite al-Ka'bi, who argued that if an action is both the human's act and God's creation, it would imply partnership (shirk) in the act. Maturidi forcefully refutes this by pointing out logical flaws and the lack of evidence. He also sarcastically uses the example of inheritance to expose al-Ka'bi's confusion about what partnership actually means.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation once without analyzing. Let the structure of al-Ka'bi's claim and Maturidi's response become clear.
Pay attention to the inheritance example.
2
Identify al-Ka'bi's core claim
Al-Ka'bi claims that dual agency (action being both human and divine) is impossible and entails partnership. Find where he argues this.
He says it necessitates 'shirka ma'qula'.
3
Follow Maturidi's refutation strategy
Notice how Maturidi first calls it ignorance, then uses al-Ka'bi's own statement about aspects, and then brings the inheritance analogy.
Maturidi shows al-Ka'bi contradicted himself.
4
Understand the final argument
Maturidi concludes that since there is no example in the witnessed world that proves partnership, the claim is mere assumption and imagination.
This is the key refutation: lack of evidence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم زعم الكعبي أنه محال أن يكون ذلك في الحقيقة فعلاً لي خلقا الله . قال الشيخ أبو منصور رحمه الله : وهذا الجهله بالمحال ، وقد بينا بعض ذلك. ثم زعم أن ذا يوجب الشركة المعقولة، إذ محال انفراد كل بجزء، وأن كان لا يتجزأ . ثم عارض نفسه بقول الخصم أن ذلك يوجب فيما كانت الجهة واحدة ، فأما فيما اختلفت فلا يعارض بملك ورث بعضه واشترى بعضه. ثم عورض بملك لى ولعبد لى. فأطنب في جواب ذلك. ونحن نقول وبالله التوفيق من تأمل الذي ذكر، وله أدنى فهم، ولا يكابر عقله علم سفهه، وإن شاء استدل بالذي قدّم من الميراث ليعلم جهله بالشركة الحاضرة، فيكون ذلك عذرًا في الجهل بما كان طريقه الاستدلال، إذ خفى عليه حق العيان. لكن هذا سؤال لم يزل المعتزلة تظن أن ذلك يوجب ذلك . وإن كانوا لا يستحقون الجواب في ذلك، فإنا ننزع به عليهم، فإنهم قصدوا بالقول قول من يقول : خلق الشيء هو ذلك، ولا يوجد شيء واحد لاثنين في الشاهد لكل كلُّه . ولهذا الوجه أنكر أن يكون فعل واحد لاثنين. فإذا لم يوجد له مثال يُعلم أنه يوجب الاشتراك أو لا، فقولهم «يوجب» ظن وخيال.
Then al-Ka'bi claimed that it is impossible for that [action] to be in reality an action of mine and a creation of Allah. Shaykh Abu Mansur (may Allah have mercy on him) said: This is ignorance of the impossible, and we have already explained some of that. Then he claimed that this entails understood partnership (shirka ma'qula), since it is impossible for each to be alone with a part, even if it is indivisible. Then he contradicted himself with the statement of the opponent that that entails [partnership] when the aspect (jiha) is one, but when it differs, there is no contradiction with a property inherited partially and purchased partially. Then it was objected with a property of mine and a property of my slave. So he elaborated in answering that. And we say, with Allah's success: Whoever reflects on what has been mentioned, and has the least understanding, and does not argue with his intellect, will know his foolishness. And if he wishes, he may use the example of inheritance to know his ignorance of the present partnership, so that becomes an excuse for his ignorance of what is known by reasoning, since the manifest truth was hidden from him. But this is a question that the Mu'tazila have always thought entails that. And even if they do not deserve an answer on that, we will refute it against them, because they intended the statement of those who say: The creation of a thing is that thing itself, and there does not exist in the witnessed world one thing for two [agents] each with the whole. And for this reason, they denied that one action can belong to two. So if there is no example by which one can know whether it entails partnership or not, then their statement 'it entails' is assumption and imagination.
Source pages p. 320

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of al-Ka'bi's argument that an action cannot be both the servant's action and God's creation. Al-Ka'bi claimed it is impossible, and Maturidi responds by calling it ignorance. Al-Ka'bi then argued that dual agency would entail partnership (shirk) because if two agents share an action, each cannot have sole possession of a part, especially if the action is indivisible. He then contradicted himself by considering the opponent's distinction of aspects: partnership only arises when the aspect is one; if aspects differ, it is like property inherited partially and purchased partially. Maturidi points out that al-Ka'bi's argument is flawed because there is no example in the witnessed world that demonstrates that dual agency necessarily entails partnership. Since they cannot provide a clear example, their claim is mere assumption and imagination. Maturidi sarcastically suggests that if they reflect on inheritance laws, they would see their ignorance of actual partnership, and that might excuse their failure to understand what is known by reasoning.

Key Terms
الكعبي
الشركة المعقولة
جزء
لا يتجزأ
جهة
الواحد
الاثنين
ظن
خيال

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That if both human and God are agents of an action, it necessarily implies partnership (shirk) in the act.
Correction: Maturidi argues that partnership is not entailed because the aspects differ (divine creation vs. human acquisition) and there is no empirical example to prove such partnership.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, why does al-Ka'bi's claim that dual agency entails partnership fail?
A) Because partnership only applies if both agents have the same aspect
B) Because there is no example in the witnessed world proving it
C) Because the Quran denies partnership
D) Because actions are not created

Key Takeaway
Maturidi refutes al-Ka'bi by showing that there is no empirical example to prove that dual agency entails partnership, so the claim is mere assumption and imagination.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you truly believe that every action of yours—your sins, your worship, your everyday choices—is created by Allah, yet you are fully accountable? How does this truth change the way you approach even your smallest intentions today?
?
When you fall into sin, do you secretly blame Allah's decree to ease your conscience, or do you take full ownership? Maturidi's distinction of aspects leaves no room for excuses—will you finally stop hiding behind qadar and repent sincerely right now?
?
Have you ever assumed something about Allah based on your own limited experience, like the Mu'tazila did with their 'creation is the thing itself' error? What false assumption about Allah are you holding onto that you need to dismantle with the truth from this lesson?

Al-Ka'bi's Objection on 'Creator of Everything'
Al-Ka'bi enumerates two objections and a specification argument against the universal interpretation of 'Creator of everything'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand al-Ka'bi's objections that the verse 'Creator of everything' would imply God's responsibility for evil, and how he attempts to argue for specification.

Core Idea
If God creates everything, does He create evil? Al-Ka'bi says yes, and that's a problem.

This passage records the Mu'tazilite theologian al-Ka'bi's objection to the verse 'Creator of everything.' He argues that if taken universally, it would include blameworthy human acts, contradicting the verse's status as praise and God's own condemnation of disbelief. He also tries to limit its scope. Let's examine his reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the literal English translation. Notice the structure: al-Ka'bi presents two objections and then a specification argument.
The passage ends mid-sentence; Maturidi's response follows in subsequent pages.
2
Identify the two main objections
Find al-Ka'bi's first objection: the verse is praise, but a universal reading would include self-dispraise (like creating disbelief). Find the second: God criticizes and punishes disbelief, but that would be inappropriate if He created it.
These objections target the logical and moral consistency of the verse under a universal interpretation.
3
Understand the specification argument
Al-Ka'bi argues that the verse is specific: not everything is created. He uses the pronoun 'He' (referring to God) as an example of something not included in 'everything.' Consider why that is a weak or strong argument.
The argument relies on the fact that God is not created, so 'everything' must exclude some things.
4
Prepare for the response
Note that the passage ends without Maturidi's reply. Think about how you might respond to al-Ka'bi. What distinctions could Maturidi make?
This sets up a dialectic: the objection is the first move in a theological debate.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم ذكر [الكعبي] أسئلتنا. من ذلك قوله [تعالى]: خالق كل شيء، وأعمال العباد أشياء. فزعم أن ذا امتداح، وليس ذلك في شتم نفسه ولا في الكفر به ولا في قتل الأنبياء. والثاني أنه عاب الكفر وعذب عليه، ولا يجوز ذلك على ما يفعله. وقال: خصصنا أيضاً بما تلونا من الآيات. ودليل ذلك أنه لم يدخل [«هو»] في ذلك، و«هو» شيء؛ مع وجود آيات
Then al-Ka'bi mentioned our objections. Among that is His statement (Exalted): 'Creator of everything,' and the deeds of servants are things. So he claimed that this is praise, and that is not in reviling Himself nor in disbelief in Him nor in killing the prophets. And the second is that He criticized disbelief and punished it, and that is not permissible upon what He does. And he said: We also specified by what we recited of the verses. And the proof of that is that 'He' did not enter into that, and 'He' is a thing; with the existence of verses.
Source pages p. 324

Commentary
This passage records al-Ka'bi's objections based on the verse 'Creator of everything' (خالق كل شيء). Al-Ka'bi argues that if God creates everything, then He creates human actions like disbelief and killing prophets, which are blameworthy. But the verse is praise, and praise cannot include self-dispraise. Also, God criticizes disbelief and punishes it, which would be inappropriate if He created it. Al-Ka'bi claims that the verse is specific and does not include everything, using the pronoun 'He' (هو) as an example of something not created. The excerpt ends without the full response, setting up the debate.

Key Terms
