أعمال العباد
امتداح
شتم
كفر
قتل الأنبياء

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The verse 'Creator of everything' means everything without any exception, including God Himself and all human actions.
Correction: Al-Ka'bi argues that this cannot be correct because it leads to self-dispraise and God punishing His own act. He tries to show that 'everything' must be restricted.

Checkpoint
What is al-Ka'bi's first objection regarding the verse 'Creator of everything'?
A) The verse is not authentically transmitted
B) It would include self-dispraise because it would cover blameworthy human acts
C) The verse only applies to natural phenomena
D) It is metaphorical and not literal

Key Takeaway
Al-Ka'bi's objection challenges the universality of 'Creator of everything' by pointing to moral and theological problems, but it sets the stage for a deeper discussion on divine agency and human acts.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face a trial or commit a sin, do you truly believe that God created that moment? How does that belief affect your relationship with Him—do you resent Him, or do you trust that His wisdom is beyond your understanding? This lesson confronts the tension between God's creation of all things and His praise for goodness. Where do you stand in that tension right now?
?
Al-Ka'bi argues that if God created disbelief, He would be criticizing His own act. Think of a fault in yourself that you often criticize in others. Are you willing to admit that both the fault and the criticism come from your own choices, yet still turn to God for forgiveness? Or do you blame God for your shortcomings? What would it mean to take full responsibility for your actions while affirming that God is the Creator of all?
?
The verse 'Creator of everything' is meant as praise. Can you honestly praise God for the difficult things He has allowed in your life this week? If not, what prevents you from seeing those moments as part of His perfect plan? Write down one specific hardship you face, and then, in light of this lesson, try to express gratitude to God for it—even if you don't understand why it happened.

Comparing Mu'tazila and Magians on the Creation of Evil
Maturidi compares Mu'tazili doctrine to Magian dualism and evaluates both.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Islamic theological critique of the Mu'tazila for denying God's creation of evil, and why Maturidi argues that the Magian dualists are actually better in this respect.

Core Idea
Denying God creates evil leads to a position worse than dualism.

Imam Maturidi examines a famous prophetic tradition that likens certain views to the Magians. He explains the basis of the comparison and goes further, showing that the Mu'tazila, who deny God's creation of evil, end up in a logically worse position than the Magians themselves. This passage sharpens our understanding of tawhid and the necessity of affirming God's absolute sovereignty.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once to get the flow, then read the translation. Note the key terms: Magians, Mu'tazila (referred to as Qadariyya), and the concepts of good and evil.
2
Identify the two groups and their positions
Identify the Magian view (deny God creates evil, attribute all good to God) and the Mu'tazili view (similar via verse interpretation). See how the Prophet likened them.
Maturidi says the Mu'tazila 'specify' a verse to exclude evil from God's creation.
3
Understand Maturidi's evaluation
Notice that Maturidi says the Magians' statement is better than the Qadariyya's. Ask yourself: why would a dualist position be considered better in this specific point?
Because the Magians exalt God by not attributing evil to Him, even though they associate a partner.
4
Reflect on tawhid implications
Consider how this critique reinforces the doctrine that God is the creator of all things, both good and evil. The Mu'tazila's attempt to protect God from evil inadvertently leads to a position that is even more problematic.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وما ذكر في المجوس، فهم قالوا ذلك لإنكارهم خلق الله الشرور، ونسبتهم كل خير إلى الله خلقا وإرادة، وذلك رأى المعتزلة في تخصيص هذه الآية، ليخرجوا بذلك الشرور عن خلقه، فهذا وجه تشبيه رسول الله إياهم بالمجوس. ولا قوة إلا بالله. ثم كان قول المجوس خيرًا من قول القدرية عند التحصيل؛ لأنهم نزهوا الله عز وجل.
And what was mentioned concerning the Magians, they said that out of denial of God's creation of evils, and their attribution of all good to God in creation and will. And that is the view of the Mu'tazila in specifying this verse, to thereby exclude evils from His creation. So this is the aspect of the likening of the Messenger of Allah to them with the Magians. There is no power except with Allah. Then the statement of the Magians was better than the statement of the Qadariyya (i.e., the Mu'tazila) upon examination, because they (the Magians) exalted God Almighty.
Source pages p. 328

Commentary
This passage is a comparison between the Mu'tazila and the Magians (Zoroastrians). Maturidi explains that the Mu'tazila, like the Magians, deny that God creates evil. The Magians attribute all good to God but attribute evil to another principle (darkness). The Mu'tazila similarly try to exclude evil from God's creation by interpreting a verse in a particular way. The Prophet likened those who hold this view to the Magians. However, Maturidi notes that the Magians are actually better than the Qadariyya (a term here used for the Mu'tazila) because the Magians at least exalt God by not attributing evil to Him, even though they associate a partner. The Mu'tazila do the same but claim to be monotheists. Thus, their position is worse.

Key Terms
مجوس
قدرية
الشرور
الخير
تشبيه

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That comparing the Mu'tazila to Magians means they are identical in all aspects.
Correction: Maturidi shows the Magians are actually less blameworthy in this specific point because they exalt God by not attributing evil to Him.

Checkpoint
Why does Maturidi say the Magians' statement is better than the Qadariyya's (Mu'tazila)?
A) Because the Magians are Muslims
B) Because the Magians exalt God by not attributing evil to Him
C) Because the Magians deny the creation of all things
D) Because the Qadariyya attribute evil to God

Key Takeaway
Denying God's creation of evil puts one in a theological position inferior even to dualism, as it compromises God's absolute sovereignty.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you confront evil in your life, do you instinctively try to separate God from it—like the Mu'tazila—or do you affirm that Allah creates both good and evil, as the Qur'an teaches? Which position truly exalts God's sovereignty?
?
Are you willing to accept that the very suffering you despise comes from Allah, or does your heart secretly desire a 'second force' to blame? What does Maturidi's comparison reveal about your own attitude toward God's will?
?
If you find yourself uncomfortable saying 'Allah created this evil,' ask yourself: is your discomfort rooted in reverence for God or in a desire to protect your own understanding of Him? Will you repent and submit to His absolute creation, even when it costs you your intellectual comfort?

The Mu'tazila Compared to the Majus on Good and Evil
Maturidi compares Mu'tazila to Majus regarding creation of good and evil.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Imam Maturidi argues that the Mu'tazila are worse than the Majus in their denial of God's creation of evil.

Core Idea
Denying God's creation of evil leads to denying His creation of good.

In this passage, Imam Maturidi explains a striking comparison made by the Prophet: likening the Mu'tazila to the Majus (Zoroastrians). He unpacks the logic behind this comparison and even shows that the Majus' position is superior to the Mu'tazila's. Let's read carefully to see why.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice how Maturidi moves from describing the Majus to drawing the parallel.
2
Identify the parallel between Mu'tazila and Majus
The passage states that both groups deny God's creation of evils, attributing only good to God. Find the specific phrases that show this.
The Majus deny creation of evil; the Mu'tazila use a specific interpretation of a verse to exclude evil from God's creation.
3
Understand Maturidi's surprising conclusion
Maturidi says the Majus' statement is better than the Mu'tazila's upon analysis. Why? Because the Majus at least exalt God by affirming His creation of good, while the Mu'tazila nullify God's creation of both good and evil.
The key word is 'tahsil' (analysis).
4
Reflect on the theological implications
Consider how the Mu'tazila's approach of interpreting the verse to avoid attributing evil to God actually leads to a worse position. How does this affect our understanding of God's power and creation?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وما ذكر في المجوس، فهم قالوا ذلك لإنكارهم خلق الله الشرور، ونسبتهم كل خير إلى الله خلقا وإرادة، وذلك رأى المعتزلة في تخصيص هذه الآية، ليخرجوا بذلك الشرور عن خلقه، فهذا وجه تشبيه رسول الله إياهم بالمجوس. ولا قوة إلا بالله. ثم كان قول المجوس خيرا من قول القدرية عند التحصيل؛ لأنهم نزهوا الله عز وجل عن فعل الشر وما يدم الفاعل عليه، وحققوا له فعل الخير وما يحمد عليه. ثم القدرية بالوجه الذي أنكره المجوس صرفوا الآية عن المفهوم تنزيها له، وأبطلوا عنه أيضا خلق كل شيء يحمد عليه من الخيرات. ونسأل الله العصمة.
And what was mentioned about the Majus: they said that out of their denial of God's creation of evils, and their attribution of every good to God in creation and will. And that is the view of the Mu'tazila in specifying this verse, to thereby exclude evils from His creation. This is the aspect of the Messenger of God likening them to the Majus. There is no power except with God. Then the statement of the Majus is better than the statement of the Qadariyya upon analysis, because they declared God exalted above doing evil and what disgraces the doer, and they affirmed for Him doing good and what He is praised for. Then the Qadariyya, by the aspect which the Majus denied, diverted the verse from its understood meaning as a sanctification for Him, and they also nullified from Him the creation of everything that is praised for good things. And we ask God for protection.
Source pages p. 328

Commentary
This passage compares the Mu'tazila to the Majus (Zoroastrians). Maturidi explains that the Majus denied God's creation of evils and attributed only good to God. The Mu'tazila do the same by interpreting the verse (all things are created by God) to exclude evils. The Prophet likened them to the Majus. Maturidi then notes that the Majus' position is actually better because they at least attribute good to God, while the Mu'tazila also deny God's creation of good things by interpreting the verse away. Thus, the Mu'tazila are worse.

Key Terms
المجوس
الشرور
الخير
القدرية
التحصيل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Prophet's likening of the Mu'tazila to the Majus is merely an insult without substantive reasoning.
Correction: Maturidi shows that the comparison is based on a shared theological methodology: both groups deny God's creation of evil. He further argues that the Majus are actually better because they affirm God's creation of good, while the Mu'tazila deny it as well.

Checkpoint
Why does Maturidi say the Majus are better than the Qadariyya (Mu'tazila)?
A) They believe in two creators
B) They deny God's creation of evil while affirming His creation of good
C) They worship fire
D) They reject all revelation

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazila's denial of God's creation of evil leads them to also deny His creation of good, making their position worse than that of the Majus.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face a tragedy or hardship, do you find yourself instinctively attributing it to anything other than Allah—like bad luck, people's mistakes, or your own shortcomings—rather than seeing it as His decree? How does your heart react to the teaching that denying Allah's creation of evil actually leads to denying His creation of good?
?
Are you willing to accept that every single event, including the ones you dislike, comes from Allah and is ultimately good in His wisdom? Or do you, like the Mu'tazila, secretly want to 'sanctify' Allah by exempting Him from the hard things in your life? What would change in your relationship with Allah if you fully believed, without exception, that He creates all things?
?
If you truly believed that denying Allah's creation of evil also strips Him of the praise for creating good (as this passage shows), would you still be comfortable with a theology that lets you blame others or the world for evil? Or would you repent from any inclination to excuse Allah from what happens and instead submit completely to His decree?

Reason and Revelation Affirm God's Absolute Power
Maturidi refutes Mu'tazila on God's power over actions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's argument that reason and revelation together necessitate affirming God's power over all actions, refuting the Mu'tazilite position.

Core Idea
Reason and revelation together affirm God's absolute power over all actions.

In this passage, Imam al-Maturidi engages with the Mu'tazilite claim that God's power only extends to creation in general, not to individual human actions. He argues that without divine command and prohibition, reason itself would not deny God's power over anything. Moreover, the Mu'tazila's own acceptance of creation from nothing should lead them to accept the lesser wonder of God creating actions for others. Denying this would imply a deficiency in God's power.

Guided Path
1
Read the source passage
Read the Arabic passage carefully, then its translation. Let Maturidi's argument unfold in his own words.
The passage is dense; take your time.
2
Identify the Mu'tazilite position
Notice that the Mu'tazila claim God only brings things into existence in general, not specifically human actions. Maturidi begins by stating their view.
3
Understand the role of command and prohibition
Maturidi argues that without divine command and prohibition (shari'a), reason would not exclude anything from God's power. Revelation provides the basis for moral responsibility.
This links reason and revelation.
4
Analyze the comparison argument
Maturidi compares creating actions for others to creating from nothing. Since the Mu'tazila accept the greater (creation from nothing), they should accept the lesser (creation of actions).
This is a logical refutation.
5
Consider the conclusion
Denying God's power over human actions would make God's power dependent on another—an impossibility. The passage ends with affirming Allah's transcendence.
This is the ultimate absurdity of the Mu'tazilite position.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مع ما لم يكن عند المعتزلة من الله إلى خلقه جملة سوى أنه أوجده ] بعد أن لم يكن"، وأن الله لم يزل موجودا؛ وذلك المعنى في فعل كل أحد موجود . على أنه لولا الأمر والنهي لم يكن العقل يحتمل إخراج شيء عن قدرة الله وصرف شيء إلى فعل غيره. والأمر والنهي [في] محل حق بالمعنى الذي يلزم القول به، لولاهما لم يلزم ذلك ؛ فيكون المعروف بالعقل وما يوجبه ضرورة ذلك مدفوعا بالجهل بالحكمة والحادث " . ولو جاز ذلك لجاز إنكار الأمر والنهي بما كان في العقل من إثبات قدرة الله على كل شيء ؛ بل قدرته على أشياء لا من شيء أو إحداث أعيان لا عن مثال أعجب من خلق فعل لآخر؛ إذ لولا ما للآخر من القدرة على ذلك لكان " لا يضطرب عاقل في تحقيق ذلك الله ؛ وقدرته لا يجوز أن تنفي عن الله قدرة ذلك [١٣٤] بعينه، فيكون كالقادر على الشيء بغيره لا بنفسه، جل الله / عن ذلك وتعالى .
Along with what the Mu'tazila had that from God to His creation as a whole is only that He brought it into existence after it was not, and that God has always existed; and that meaning exists in the action of everyone. However, were it not for command and prohibition, the mind would not tolerate removing something from God's power and directing something to the action of another. And the command and prohibition are in a place of right in the sense that makes it necessary to say, without them that would not be necessary; so what is known by reason and what its necessity entails is repelled by ignorance of wisdom and the contingent. And if that were permissible, then denying command and prohibition would be permissible because of what is in the mind of affirming God's power over everything; but rather His power over things from nothing or creating entities without a model is more wondrous than creating an action for another; for if the other had no power over that, then no sane person would doubt in realizing that God; and His power cannot deny that God has power over that very thing, for He would be like one who is able to do something by someone else, not by Himself—exalted is God above that and high.
Source pages p. 339

Commentary
This passage refutes the Mu'tazila by showing that their position leads to contradiction. They claim that God only creates the world in general, and that human actions are not from Him. But reason itself knows God's power over all things. Without divine command and prohibition (shari'a), reason might still understand that nothing is outside God's power. If the Mu'tazila deny God's power over human actions, they contradict reason which affirms God's absolute power. Moreover, creating an action for another is less wondrous than creating from nothing; if they accept the greater, they should accept the lesser. Denying it would make God's power dependent on another, which is impossible.

Key Terms
المعتزلة (Mu'tazila)
الأمر والنهي (command and prohibition)
قدرة الله (God's power)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think reason alone can establish moral responsibility without revelation.
Correction: Maturidi argues that reason itself acknowledges God's absolute power, but without command and prohibition, it would not deny God's power over any action. Revelation is necessary to define human responsibility.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, what would happen without divine command and prohibition?
A) Reason would deny God's power over human actions.
B) Reason would not tolerate removing anything from God's power.
C) Human actions would be impossible.
D) The Mu'tazila would be correct.

Key Takeaway
Maturidi shows that reason and revelation together affirm God's absolute power over all actions; denying this leads to absurdity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You claim to believe in God's absolute power, but do you truly live as if every single action of yours—every word, every glance, every thought—is under His direct control? If you did, would you still make the same excuses for your sins? How would your daily choices change if you realized that denying God's power over your actions is, in fact, a subtle form of shirk?
?
The passage says that without divine command and prohibition, reason itself would not assign any action to anyone other than God. Have you ever relied on your own reasoning alone to justify your actions, ignoring what Allah has commanded and forbidden? Where in your life have you made your own intellect the ultimate judge instead of submitting to revelation? What would it look like to truly surrender your judgments to Allah's commands even when you don't understand?
?
Maturidi argues that denying God's power over human actions leads to the absurd conclusion that God is able through another. Reflect: In what areas of your life have you acted as if you are independent of God's power—as if your successes were your own doing or your failures merely bad luck? How does this passage expose the emptiness of that pride? Will you today repent and acknowledge that every action, good or bad, is under His power and that your only hope is His mercy?

Prevention and the Loss of Ability
Maturidi refutes the Mu'tazila claim that prevention does not negate ability.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's refutation of the Mu'tazilite claim that prohibition does not entail loss of ability.

Core Idea
Prevention and loss of ability both render action impossible.

In this passage, Imam Maturidi directly addresses a key Mu'tazilite argument: that a prohibited action remains possible even if ability is intact, because prohibition does not remove ability. Maturidi counters by asserting that prevention and loss of ability are equivalent in rendering action impossible. Let's examine his reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the source passage
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation to understand the gist.
Notice the key terms: ممنوع, فوت القدرة, منع.
2
Identify the Mu'tazila claim
Identify what the Mu'tazila claim: that a prohibited action does not result from loss of ability, so the action can occur if not prevented.
3
Follow Maturidi's counter-argument
Maturidi argues that prevention and loss of ability are one in making action impossible. Also, action cannot occur during prevention, but can when ability is lost due to prior ability.
Consider the logical equivalence Maturidi draws.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
The final statement 'لا قوة إلا بالله' underscores that all power is from Allah, reinforcing the dependence on divine power.
This is a common closing phrase in theological discussions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ووجه آخر مما زعم جماعة المعتزلة أن الممنوع لا بفوت القدرة، فيقع له الفعل مع الإطلاق. فما أنكروا ذلك بفوت القدرة والمنع؛ وفوت القدرة وإحالة الفعل معه واحد. مع ما لا يجوز وجود الفعل في حال وقوع المنع بحال، ويجوز مع فقد القدرة بما تقدم من القدرة. ولا قوة إلا بالله.
And another aspect of what a group of the Mu'tazila claimed: that the prohibited [action] does not [occur] through loss of ability, so the action occurs for him absolutely. So they did not deny that through loss of ability and prevention; and loss of ability and impossibility of action are one with it. Along with the fact that it is not permissible for the action to exist in the state of occurring of prevention in any way, and it is permissible with the loss of ability due to what preceded of ability. And there is no power except through Allah.
Source pages p. 346

Commentary
This passage refutes the Mu'tazila claim that a prohibited action (mamnu') does not result from loss of ability (fawat al-qudra), implying that one can still act if allowed. Maturidi argues that prevention and loss of ability are equivalent in making action impossible. He points out that action cannot occur during prevention, but can occur when ability is lost due to previous ability. The argument upholds that ability is a prerequisite for action.

Key Terms
ممنوع
فوت القدرة
منع
إحالة الفعل
معتزلة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think prevention merely restricts but does not negate ability.
Correction: Maturidi argues that prevention and loss of ability both render action impossible; they are equivalent in effect.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, how does prevention compare to loss of ability?
A) Prevention is less severe
B) Loss of ability is more severe
C) They both render action impossible
D) Prevention does not affect ability

Key Takeaway
Maturidi refutes the Mu'tazila by establishing that prevention is functionally equivalent to loss of ability—both make action impossible.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you are prohibited by Allah from something, do you secretly think you could do it if the prohibition were removed, relying on your own ability? How does Maturidi's argument that prevention itself makes action impossible challenge that assumption? Will you now view prohibitions as absolute barriers that require Allah's power to overcome?
?
Do you truly live as if all power belongs to Allah (لا قوة إلا بالله), or do you still assume you have independent ability to choose and act? What concrete change in your daily life would reflect the belief that your ability is entirely from Allah?
?
Have you ever attributed your success in avoiding sin to your own willpower rather than to Allah's prevention and grace? How does this lesson shift the credit for obedience from yourself to Allah?

Refuting al-Ka'bi's Dilemma
Maturidi presents two options for al-Ka'bi's reasoning.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Maturidi refutes al-Ka'bi's argument by presenting a dilemma: either al-Ka'bi applies all human attributes directly to God, leading to absurdities, or he considers the underlying meaning, which nullifies his claim.

Core Idea
Analogy requires considering underlying meaning, not just outward similarity.

Al-Ka'bi, a Mu'tazili theologian, argued that if we distinguish between God and humans in terms of actions, then lying from humans could be truth from God. Maturidi shows that this argument forces al-Ka'bi into a dilemma: either he applies all human attributes directly to God, leading to absurdities, or he considers the actual meaning of the action, which nullifies his claim.

Guided Path
1
Read al-Ka'bi's argument
Read the passage and identify al-Ka'bi's claim: if distinction between God and others is allowed, then lying from others could be truth from God.
Notice how Maturidi dismisses it as foolishness.
2
Identify the two options
Maturidi says al-Ka'bi has only two possibilities: either he directly applies everything from the witnessed to the absent, or he considers the underlying meaning.
These are the two horns of the dilemma.
3
Examine the first option
If al-Ka'bi takes the first path, he must accept absurd consequences like creating useless things and punishing without cause. His own argument about lying is turned against him.
Whatever he answers, it binds him.
4
Examine the second option
If he considers the meaning, his statement is nullified by the clear intuition of the intellect that distinguishes what is permissible for God.
This shows his argument has no basis.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم ذكر الكعبي معنى يدل على سفهه، فقال: لو جاز التفريق بين الله وبين ما يكون من غيره لجاز أن يكون الكذب من غيره يكون منه صدقاً. فلا أدري أي شيء دفعه إلى هذا الخيال؛ وقد بينا بخروجه عن ذلك وتعنّته فيهما ادعى. على أنه لا يخلو من أحد أمرين: إما أن يجعل كل شيء يعرفه في الشاهد من البشر حكمة أو سفها يقول به في الغائب، أو ينظر إلى المعنى الذي له صار كذلك في التحقيق فيقول به في الغائب. فإن قال بالأول لزمه ذلك في خلق ما لا ينتفع به، وفي خلق الشيء من لا شيء، وفي التعذيب من غير دفع، ثم يقال نفسه في إجازته قوله بالكذب. فمهما أجاب من شيء فذلك لازم له فيما قال. وإن نظر إلى المعنى أبطل قوله ذا ببديهة العقل. وذا يجوز له، وذا لا يجوز، وهذا النوع من الخيال الذي لا أصل ومتى تدرك حقائق الأشياء ببداية العقول؟ وإنما العقول ركبت مميزة بين مختلف الأشياء بمعانيها التي توجب الاختلاف ومؤلّفة بين مجتمعها بمعان توجب الجمع، وذا حق الفكر والنظر ليوصل بهما إلى ذلك. ثم إنه لا أحد يعلم عالما بشيء لا علم له به، قادرا على شيء لا قدرة عليه؛ بل كل معروف بنفي القدرة والعلم موصوف بالجهل والعجز، فإذا احتمل الوصف بالقدرة والعلم فإنه عالم قادر. ولا قوة إلا بالله. وإن رجع إلى اعتبار المعاني التي هي أسباب حقائق الأشياء فذلك له مسلم، ولا معنى لقوله بديهة العقل إنما ذلك حق الطباع ونفاره. ثم ينكر أن يكون في خلق الله قبيحا في الحقيقة وشرا وفسادا، على وجود ما لا يحصى من ذلك على هذه الأوصاف بالعقول. بل الله جل ثناؤه بما جعلها كذلك ضرب بها مثل ما قبح من الأفعال وأراد تفظيع منظره الذي أوعد به ذو عقل؛ أين الذي ينكر مثل هذا من دعوى بداية العقول؟ ثم لم يزل أئمتهم كلموا الثنوية بجواز كون الخير والشر والطيب والخبيث من واحد ليدفعوا به القول بالاثنين
Then al‐Ka‘bī mentioned a point indicating his foolishness, saying: If it were permissible to make a distinction between God and what comes from other than Him, then it would be permissible for lying from other than Him to be truth from Him. So I do not know what drove him to this fancy. And we have already shown by his departure from that and his obstinacy in both claims. However, it is not free from two possibilities: either he makes everything he knows in the witnessed realm from humans either wisdom or foolishness and says the like of it about the absent, or he considers the meaning (al‐ma‘nā) on account of which it became so in reality and says the like of it about the absent. If he says by the first, he is required to accept that in creating what is not beneficial, in creating a thing from nothing, and in punishing without removal, then the same statement is said about his own permission of lying. Whatever he answers about something, that is binding upon him in what he said. And if he considers the meaning, his statement is nullified by the intuition of the intellect: 'this is permissible for Him, and this is not permissible.' This is a kind of fancy with no basis. When are the realities of things perceived by the beginnings of intellects? Intellects are composed as distinguishing between different things by their meanings that necessitate difference, and unifying between similar things by meanings that necessitate union, and that is the right of thought and reflection to arrive through them at that. Then no one is known to be knowledgeable of something with no knowledge of it, nor powerful over something with no power over it; rather, everything known by the negation of power and knowledge is described as ignorant and powerless. If it admits the description of power and knowledge, then He is knowledgeable and powerful. And there is no power except with God. If he returns to considering the meanings which are the causes of the realities of things, that is conceded to him, and his saying 'by the intuition of the intellect' has no meaning; that is rather the nature of temperaments and their aversion. Then he denies that there is in God's creation anything truly ugly, evil, or corrupt, despite the existence of countless things with these descriptions in the intellects. Rather, God – exalted be His praise – by making them so, struck a similitude of what is ugly of actions and intended to make horrific the sight with which a person of intellect is threatened. Where is one who denies such a thing from the claim of the beginnings of intellects? Then their imams continued to speak to the Dualists about the permissibility of good and evil, pure and impure coming from one, in order to repel the doctrine of duality.
Source pages p. 351

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of a specific argument by al‐Ka‘bī, a Mu‘tazilī theologian. Al‐Ka‘bī claimed: If a distinction is made between God and what comes from other than Him – i.e., if we say that the same kind of act (e.g., lying) is impossible for God but possible for humans – then it would follow that lying from someone else could be truth from God. In other words, he denied the possibility of any qualitative difference between divine and human actions, assuming a strict analogical inference (tamthīl) from the witnessed (shāhid) to the absent (ghā’ib). Maturidi first dismisses this as foolishness and reminds that al‐Ka‘bī's obstinacy has already been shown. He then offers a logical analysis: al‐Ka‘bī has only two options. Either he takes every observable human action as being either wise or foolish and directly applies the same judgment to God (the first alternative), or he considers the underlying meaning (al‐ma‘nā) that makes an action wise or foolish in reality and then applies that meaning to God (the second alternative). If he takes the first path, it would force him to admit that creating useless things, creating something from nothing, and punishing without removal are also wise – which contradicts his position. Moreover, his own argument about lying would be turned against him. Whatever he answers will apply equally to his own claim. If he takes the second path – considering the underlying meaning – then his statement is nullified by the clear intuition of the intellect that distinguishes between what is permissible for God and what is not. Maturidi then criticizes the very idea that realities of things can be grasped by 'the beginnings of intellects' (bidāyat al-‘uqūl) without proper reflection on meanings. Intellects are composed to differentiate things by their specific meanings that necessitate difference, and to unify things by meanings that necessitate coming together. This is the proper function of thought and reflection. Furthermore, no one can know a thing as knowledgeable without knowledge, or powerful without power; the very definition of knowledge and power requires their subjects to be qualified accordingly. If a subject admits the description of power and knowledge, it is knowledgeable and powerful. Thus, God's attributes cannot be denied based on mere analogy from human attributes. Maturidi then turns to al‐Ka‘bī's denial of real ugliness and evil in creation, pointing out that countless things are recognized by intellects as ugly and corrupt. God Himself used such things as similitudes for ugly actions to warn people. The passage concludes by noting that earlier Muslim imams argued against the Dualists (al‐Thanawiyya) that good and evil can come from one source (God) in order to refute dualism.

Key Terms
الكعبي
التفريق
الشاهد والغائب
المعنى

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Al-Ka'bi believes that any action that appears similar in the witnessed realm must be treated the same for God.
Correction: Maturidi shows that one must consider the underlying meaning that makes an action what it is, not just outward similarity.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, what happens if al-Ka'bi considers the underlying meaning (al-ma'na) rather than direct analogy?
A) It leads to absurdities
B) It nullifies his claim
C) It proves his point
D) It requires further proof

Key Takeaway
Maturidi demonstrates that proper analogy from the witnessed to the absent requires considering the underlying meaning, not mere superficial similarity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think about God's attributes, do you ever assume that because something is good or permissible for humans, it must be the same for God? Which analogy in your own thinking might be based on outward similarity rather than the true meaning?
?
This lesson shows that analogies about God must be grounded in the underlying meaning, not mere appearance. What area of your faith—such as understanding God’s justice, mercy, or power—might be distorted by a superficial analogy from human experience?
?
Maturidi says the second option is ‘nullified by the intuition of the intellect.’ Is your intellect truly submitted to divine revelation, or have you allowed cultural or personal biases to shape your image of God? What one step will you take this week to purify your understanding of God from false analogies?

Critical Role of Meanings in Theology
Maturidi criticizes reliance on intuitive intellect and argues for considering meanings.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's critique of relying on 'beginnings of intellects' and his emphasis on using meanings to know the realities of things.

Core Idea
Intellects are tools for distinguishing by meanings, not for raw intuition.

After refuting al-Ka'bi's analogy, Maturidi criticizes his method of appealing to 'the beginnings of intellects.' He explains that intellects are composed to differentiate things by their specific meanings, and that one cannot grasp realities without proper reflection. He then argues that denying real ugliness and evil in creation is inconsistent with clear intellectual evidence.

Guided Path
1
Read Maturidi's critique
Read the rhetorical question 'When are the realities of things perceived by the beginnings of intellects?' and understand his skepticism.
He is challenging the very idea of grasping realities through mere intuition.
2
Understand the function of intellect
Maturidi explains that intellects are composed to distinguish different things by their meanings and unify similar things by their meanings.
This is the proper method, not raw intuition.
3
Examine the argument on attributes
No one can be knowledgeable without knowledge or powerful without power. If something can be described by knowledge and power, it is necessarily knowledgeable and powerful.
This supports affirming attributes for God.
4
See the response on evil and ugliness
Maturidi denies al-Ka'bi's claim that there is no real ugliness or evil in creation, pointing to countless examples recognized by intellects. God uses such things as similitudes for warning.
This refutes al-Ka'bi's denial.

Then al‐Ka‘bī mentioned a point indicating his foolishness, saying: If it were permissible to make a distinction between God and what comes from other than Him, then it would be permissible for lying from other than Him to be truth from Him. So I do not know what drove him to this fancy. And we have already shown by his departure from that and his obstinacy in both claims. Then no one is known to be knowledgeable of something with no knowledge of it, nor powerful over something with no power over it; rather, everything known by the negation of power and knowledge is described as ignorant and powerless. If it admits the description of power and knowledge, then He is knowledgeable and powerful. And there is no power except with God. If he returns to considering the meanings which are the causes of the realities of things, that is conceded to him, and his saying 'by the intuition of the intellect' has no meaning; that is rather the nature of temperaments and their aversion. Then he denies that there is in God's creation anything truly ugly, evil, or corrupt, despite the existence of countless things with these descriptions in the intellects. Rather, God – exalted be His praise – by making them so, struck a similitude of what is ugly of actions and intended to make horrific the sight with which a person of intellect is threatened. Where is one who denies such a thing from the claim of the beginnings of intellects? Then their imams continued to speak to the Dualists about the permissibility of good and evil, pure and impure coming from one, in order to repel the doctrine of duality.
Source pages p. 351

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of a specific argument by al‐Ka‘bī, a Mu‘tazilī theologian. Al‐Ka‘bī claimed: If a distinction is made between God and what comes from other than Him – i.e., if we say that the same kind of act (e.g., lying) is impossible for God but possible for humans – then it would follow that lying from someone else could be truth from God. In other words, he denied the possibility of any qualitative difference between divine and human actions, assuming a strict analogical inference (tamthīl) from the witnessed (shāhid) to the absent (ghā’ib). Maturidi first dismisses this as foolishness and reminds that al‐Ka‘bī's obstinacy has already been shown. He then offers a logical analysis: al‐Ka‘bī has only two options. Either he takes every observable human action as being either wise or foolish and directly applies the same judgment to God (the first alternative), or he considers the underlying meaning (al‐ma‘nā) that makes an action wise or foolish in reality and then applies that meaning to God (the second alternative). If he takes the first path, it would force him to admit that creating useless things, creating something from nothing, and punishing without removal are also wise – which contradicts his position. Moreover, his own argument about lying would be turned against him. Whatever he answers will apply equally to his own claim. If he takes the second path – considering the underlying meaning – then his statement is nullified by the clear intuition of the intellect that distinguishes between what is permissible for God and what is not. Maturidi then criticizes the very idea that realities of things can be grasped by 'the beginnings of intellects' (bidāyat al-‘uqūl) without proper reflection on meanings. Intellects are composed to differentiate things by their specific meanings that necessitate difference, and to unify things by meanings that necessitate coming together. This is the proper function of thought and reflection. Furthermore, no one can know a thing as knowledgeable without knowledge, or powerful without power; the very definition of knowledge and power requires their subjects to be qualified accordingly. If a subject admits the description of power and knowledge, it is knowledgeable and powerful. Thus, God's attributes cannot be denied based on mere analogy from human attributes. Maturidi then turns to al‐Ka‘bī's denial of real ugliness and evil in creation, pointing out that countless things are recognized by intellects as ugly and corrupt. God Himself used such things as similitudes for ugly actions to warn people. The passage concludes by noting that earlier Muslim imams argued against the Dualists (al‐Thanawiyya) that good and evil can come from one source (God) in order to refute dualism.

Key Terms
بداية العقول
الثنوية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Al-Ka'bi thinks that the 'beginnings of intellects' can directly grasp the realities of things without considering underlying meanings.
Correction: Maturidi explains that intellects are composed to distinguish by meanings, and realities are known through reflection on those meanings, not through raw intuition.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, what is the proper function of the intellect?
A) To directly grasp all realities
B) To distinguish and unite things based on their meanings
C) To deny all evil
D) To create its own knowledge

Key Takeaway
Maturidi insists that true knowledge comes from reflecting on the meanings that differentiate things, not from raw intuition.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of a time when you immediately judged a situation as 'unfair' or 'evil' without reflecting on any deeper meaning or wisdom behind it. How might Maturidi's insistence on considering meanings before passing judgment change how you react next time? Will you pause and ask yourself what meaning God might intend through that trial?
?
You claim to believe that God is all-knowing and all-powerful, but do you ever act as if He cannot bring good out of the evil you see? When you witness suffering, do you secretly wonder if God is really in control? Maturidi says that even ugliness is used by God as a similitude to warn. How will you remind yourself of this truth the next time doubt creeps in?
?
Are there areas of your faith where you trust your own 'gut feeling' over the careful reasoning of the scholars? This lesson warns against relying on raw intuition without meanings. What specific area of your aqeedah have you been treating casually based on 'how it feels'? Will you now commit to studying the meanings behind that belief to ground it properly?
